Chapter 1: The Basin That Voted in Bodies

Ren Okoye had been warned not to call it a parliament.

The warning came in six legal languages, three Orison relational registers, one Compact advisory memo, and a private message from Mira Cael that opened with: If you think the word is wrong, you are paying attention. Use it anyway until they give you a better one.

The descent capsule passed below the last clear ice shelf of the Shoals and entered water full of light that had never seen a sun.

Parliament Basin opened beneath Ren like a city remembering how to be an animal. Reef walls curved away in pale terraces, each terrace veined with salt-white channels and slow bands of blue-green luminescence. Warm brine rose from vents in disciplined columns. Filament bridges flexed between living platforms. Hundreds of Orison bodies moved through the basin in clusters, fans, braids, sheets, knots, and forms Ren's training refused to name because naming them too quickly was how humans got themselves forgiven by their own records.

The capsule's human display tried to help.

DELEGATE BODY CLUSTER / SOUTH RIM

ARCHIVAL REEF / NONVOTING

SPEAKER CURRENT / ACTIVE

EXCHANGE VESSELS / CAUTION: POLITICAL CONTACT

Ren turned the labels off.

The display did not accept silence easily.

It replaced the labels with smaller outlines, as if shrinking a mistake made it less consequential. Thin boxes clung to the moving Orison forms. A cluster near the south rim became probable delegate mass. A layered sheet near the vent field became maintenance-affiliated nonvoting body. A braided current crossing the central basin became procedural traffic. Each phrase arrived with a confidence percentage, and every confidence percentage was worse than ignorance because it would look humble in a report.

Ren opened the audit pane and marked the first refusal of the day:

MACHINE LABELS SUPPRESSED BEFORE WITNESS. SUPPRESSION DOES NOT MEAN ABSENCE OF CLASSIFICATION PRESSURE.

The capsule logged it with a soft tone.

Vela-Short glanced over again. "You are making a record before they let you make a record."

"The record starts before permission," Ren said.

"That sentence will annoy everyone."

"Good."

The pilot's hands moved through the guide-current controls with almost Orison patience. "Most observers think the labels are harmless because they can delete them later."

"Later is where labels become memory."

Vela-Short made a small approving sound and then hid it by checking a pressure gauge.

Ren kept the audit pane open. Human systems always wanted a first noun. Delegate. Body. Faction. Chamber. Vessel. Host. Threat. If they did not receive one, they generated one from older harm and called it situational awareness. Ren had spent too many years repairing transcripts where the first machine label survived every later correction because the correction read like nuance and the label read like fact.

Mira Cael's private warning had included a paragraph on that too:

The first category offered by a helpful system becomes the floor under every later sentence. If you cannot remove the floor, mark that you are standing on it.

Ren had memorized the line because it sounded useful and because it had made them angry. Mira's best warnings did that. They arrived as tools and accusations.

Below the capsule, the maintenance-affiliated nonvoting body unfolded into three temporary limbs and joined a current exchange that made the term nonvoting absurd. It was not voting in any human procedural sense. It was altering the pressure around a vote body before speech, which meant the eventual vote would carry a shape made partly by it. Calling that nonvoting was like calling weather undecided because it did not sign the evacuation order.

Ren added:

PRIOR MACHINE CATEGORY NONVOTING REJECTED AS UNSAFE. BODY MAY ALTER VOTE CONDITIONS WITHOUT ENTERING HUMAN COUNT.

The audit pane accepted the line. Somewhere in the capsule's storage, a future clerk would either preserve it or curse it.

Ren preferred both outcomes to invisibility.

The pilot, who had not introduced herself beyond the call sign Vela-Short and the sentence I do not interpret, glanced over from the forward cradle. "Most human observers leave those on."

"Most human observers write reports that start with 'although complex,'" Ren said.

Vela-Short smiled without warmth. "You have read the older files."

"I have corrected some of them."

"That is a worse credential."

Ren accepted the rebuke. The Shoals had a gift for making human expertise look like a clean suit worn into a flood.

The capsule sank along a spiral guide current rather than a mechanical track. Orison stations did not like straight approaches to political chambers. A straight approach implied the visitor already knew where arrival was. The guide current turned them through layers of heat, salt, color, pressure, and chemical tone. Ren's translator cuff tightened and loosened against their wrist as it sampled gradients. The cuff was expensive, treaty-approved, and still less honest than skin.

Below, the voting had begun before any speech.

Ren had studied body exchange for fourteen years. They had translated three minor reef adoptions, two boundary apologies, and one disaster review in which an Orison maintenance lineage accepted partial responsibility for drowning a human repair crew after correctly warning the crew not to enter a current the crew's insurance map had declared safe. Ren knew the theory. Orison deliberation was not debate first and decision after. A faction did not merely argue its position. It made a body capable of carrying the sensory, metabolic, historical, and relational pressures that produced the position. Another faction entered that body, partially and temporarily, then returned changed enough to answer with earned difficulty.

Human law called this "embodied voting."

Orison translators called it six different things depending on current, tide, injury, and whether the exchange was voluntary.

Ren privately called it terrifying.

A broad blue body unfolded from the western terrace. It had no face, but the front edge of it turned toward the basin with such unmistakable attention that the capsule's safety system dimmed the windows. Filaments extended from its underside, each one tipped with a bead of darker tissue. The beads detached, drifted through the water, and entered a waiting cluster of smaller red-brown forms.

The red-brown cluster shuddered.

Ren's cuff produced a provisional gloss:

POSITION OFFERED: HUMAN TREATY OBLIGATION HAS BECOME SHELL WITHOUT CURRENT.

The gloss vanished, replaced by a warning.

DO NOT QUOTE.

"Good cuff," Ren murmured.

"It learned that here," Vela-Short said.

The red-brown cluster opened. For a moment Ren saw, or thought they saw, the exchanged position move through it: not an idea, not a sentence, but a whole pressure system. Human treaty obligation as shell: hard, protective, dead if separated from the water that made it useful. Human treaty obligation without current: law still shaped like care after the relation that fed it had stopped moving.

Then the cluster answered by changing color, bands of iron and milk crossing its outer lobes.

The basin brightened in response.

Ren's chest tightened.

They had come prepared to witness dispute. They had not come prepared for beauty to feel like evidence tampering.

The capsule docked to a human membrane bolted into the basin wall. It was not old enough to be trusted by the Orison and not new enough to reassure humans. Condensation moved down the inside in slow sheets. Beyond it, brine pressed against clear flexglass with the patient confidence of an ocean that had outlived every treaty.

Lael Shun waited in the dry lock.

She wore Compact gray with no decorative rank markers, which on a treaty advocate meant either humility or a knife hidden in procedure. She was small, exact, and looked as if sleep had filed a complaint against her and lost on jurisdiction.

"Interpreter Okoye," Lael said. "You are late."

Ren checked the lock chronometer. "I am three minutes inside the arrival window."

"The Parliament began early."

"The Parliament began before it summoned us."

Lael's mouth moved by one millimeter. Not a smile. A note that a smile could have been filed here if conditions allowed. "Mira said you would do that."

"Do what?"

"Answer the accusation under the process instead of the process under the accusation."

Ren removed their travel gloves. The lock air was wet and mineral-thick. "Dr. Cael sends everyone unfairly useful warnings."

"She also said you dislike being used as a clean human conscience."

"She used kinder words."

"No," Lael said. "She did not."

The lock cycled. Brine smell entered first: salt, copper, iodine, warm stone, and the faint living sweetness Ren associated with Orison reef tissue under stress. The dry gallery beyond the lock curved along the basin wall. Human observers had been given a narrow platform with anchored chairs, emergency masks, record terminals, and a sign written by someone who had either understood the Orison very well or not at all:

WITNESS WITHOUT REACHING.

Ren liked it immediately and distrusted themselves for liking it.

Through the membrane, the basin moved.

Not bustled. Not gathered. Moved. Hundreds of political bodies exchanged contact in layered patterns. Some carried stable faction colors: blue for treaty continuity, amber for withdrawal, green-white for reef repair obligation, black-silver for Pale Arc caution. Others were temporary compounds, bodies made for one question and already dissolving at the edges as the question changed.

Human delegations liked to count participants.

Ren counted exits.

There were fewer than they wanted.

Lael followed their gaze. "The chamber is under ordinary procedural seal."

"Ordinary for whom?"

"No one is detained."

"That was not my question."

Lael looked tired enough to be honest. "For the Orison, a sealed process means participants remain in relation until the current resolves. For humans, it feels like being locked in a room with people who dislike our verbs."

"Are we free to leave?"

"Physically, yes. Politically, no."

"Then say that in the next briefing."

"I did. A routeholder envoy asked me to remove it because it sounded unstable."

"Is he here?"

Lael pointed down the gallery.

Aven Rusk stood beside the primary record terminal, speaking quietly to a clerk who looked too young to have learned how expensive quiet could become. Rusk was tall, silver-haired, and dressed in the soft dark fabrics of private route power. Not ostentatious. Worse. A man who could afford to let his influence be inferred. His family consortium controlled convoy insurance through three meridian legs feeding the Shoals. If Orison treaty obligations lapsed, medicine, instruments, and half the legal trade in brine-compatible polymers would become more expensive by morning.

Rusk saw Ren looking and lifted two fingers in greeting.

Ren did not return it quickly enough to be polite.

"Try not to insult him in the first hour," Lael said.

"How long should I wait?"

"Until I know which insult will be useful."

Ren revised Lael upward.

A ripple passed through the basin. The human terminals lagged, then produced a synchronized alert:

SPEAKER COMPOSITE APPROACHING

The membrane darkened to protect human eyes from the sudden brightness.

Third-Voice-of-Reef rose from the lower chamber in three colors that refused to blend.

They were larger than Ren expected. The speaker body had been grown for Parliament scale, not human comfort: a central translucent lobe the size of a cargo sled, three folded fans extending from it like living banners, and a trailing mass of fine cilia that wrote chemical notes into the water faster than any machine could parse. One fan glowed blue-white, another amber-gold, the third deep green shot through with black threads. Each moved on a different rhythm. Each seemed to listen in a different direction.

The translator did not speak immediately.

Ren respected it for that.

Third-Voice touched the membrane with a dozen cilia. Tiny contact points bloomed against the flexglass. The human gallery's speakers warmed.

"Ren Okoye arrives dry," the machine said. "Ren Okoye carries Mira-Cael method, Dock-Seven question, Lumen warning, and personal reluctance."

Aven Rusk turned his head.

Lael closed her eyes very briefly.

Ren stepped closer to the membrane. "Ren Okoye arrives under summons and without authority to decide."

The three fans moved.

The translator clicked.

"Better than prior humans."

"A low basin," Ren said.

The blue-white fan brightened. Humor, probably. Or irritation. Or the ancient alien pleasure of watching a human step carefully and still put weight on the wrong shell.

"You will witness," Third-Voice said.

"If permitted."

"You will not simplify."

"I will try not to."

The amber fan snapped open, hard enough to send a pressure wave against the membrane. The gallery floor vibrated under Ren's boots.

"Trying is a human basin for failure," Third-Voice said.

Ren took the hit. "Then I will mark every simplification I cannot avoid."

The green-black fan slowed.

"Acceptable beginning."

Not approval. A place to put the next breath.

The membrane between them cleared by one degree.

Ren understood the gesture only because Mira had once made them watch three hours of Pelagos contact recordings with the sound removed. Orison chambers did not open or close like human rooms. They altered the amount of consequence a visitor was allowed to perceive. A fully clouded membrane meant protection, quarantine, contempt, or mercy, depending on the current. Clear water meant permission to be implicated.

The basin was implicating them.

Vela-Short had remained near the lock, not quite inside the human gallery and not quite free of it. She touched two fingers to the wet seam where the dry platform met the Parliament membrane. The gesture made the translator cuff warm around Ren's wrist.

"Observer Okoye," Vela-Short said, and used the Compact title with such precision that Ren heard the insult folded inside it. "Do you request calibration before speech?"

Lael's head turned a fraction.

Rusk's clerk lifted his stylus.

Ren did not answer quickly. Calibration was the ceremonial test human observers hated because it proved the equipment had been trained on previous failures. A calibration could take a minute or a day. It could be technical, linguistic, sensory, or humiliating. Ren had performed minor versions in university tanks, where a student put one hand into warm saline and admitted that the word current did not mean the same thing when it was carrying someone else's memory trace.

In the Parliament Basin, calibration meant accepting that the first record of the session would not be the Compact's request or the Parliament's answer. It would be the visitor's declared limits.

The old treaty habit inside Ren wanted to refuse. Human institutions did not like beginning with incompetence. They liked credentials first, constraints later, and apologies only after harm had become impossible to hide.

"Yes," Ren said. "I request calibration before speech."

Lael exhaled through her nose. It was the sound of a lawyer watching a bridge become longer.

Aven Rusk smiled. "Is that required?"

"No," Ren said.

"Then why delay a chamber already under deadline?"

Ren looked at him. "Because I would prefer not to make the first injury faster."

Rusk's smile thinned. "Medicine ships do not become more patient because an interpreter feels ethically elegant."

"No," Ren said. "They become safer when the document that lets them pass does not break the people maintaining the passage."

The record clerk wrote that down.

Rusk noticed and stopped smiling.

Third-Voice-of-Reef lowered the blue-white fan until it lay almost flat against the membrane. A pattern moved through it, pale spots appearing and vanishing like stars seen through water. Ren's cuff tried to render the pattern, failed, and produced a single bracketed warning:

[CALIBRATION WILL USE PRIOR HUMAN ERROR]

The gallery lights dimmed.

The basin did not project an image. Projection was a human habit: light thrown at a surface, meaning separated from the body that made it. Orison evidence arrived as relation. A thread of brine rose from a socket in the floor beside Ren's boots and opened into a fist-sized globe. Inside it, color moved in layers: blue-white, amber, green, then a flat gray Ren knew from old Compact documents.

The gray was not Orison color. It was human administrative compression.

"Prior record," Third-Voice said.

The globe split into two nested currents.

Ren saw a treaty room forty-two years earlier, not as video but as a pressure diagram. A human negotiator stood dry behind a transparent shield. An Orison maintenance lineage spread itself across three wet cradles. The human wanted one name for the lineage. The lineage offered a relation sequence instead: those-who-maintain-the-south-current-after-harm-and-before-repair, with a conditional attachment to a reef memory Ren could feel only as a cold line across the tongue.

The human record reduced it to South Current Authority.

Ren flinched.

The calibration did not let them look away.

South Current Authority signed a mineral access delay. South Current Authority later appeared in a sanction file. South Current Authority became, in three different human databases, a party, a resource custodian, and a political faction. None of those were exactly false. That was why the harm had lasted. A clean lie could be contested. A useful simplification entered everything.

The globe tightened until the old relation sequence became a hard gray bead.

"Name survived," Third-Voice said. "Relation did not."

Ren swallowed. The air in the gallery tasted metallic, though they knew that was memory crossing the cuff through skin.

"I receive," Ren said.

The amber fan sharpened.

"Not enough."

Ren closed their eyes for one second. They could feel Lael beside them, motionless and alert. They could feel Rusk waiting for a procedural weakness. They could feel the basin beyond the membrane, full of bodies that had learned to expect humans to mistake sorrow for ceremony.

"The prior record made an administratively useful party out of a repair relation," Ren said. "It let human systems enforce obligations against a shape that Orison participants had not offered as a stable self. It preserved enough truth to defend itself and removed enough relation to cause damage."

The gray bead loosened.

"What damage?" Third-Voice asked.

Ren had not expected the question to turn inward. They had studied the South Current file. Everyone in treaty-science had. It was the example professors used when they wanted students to understand that translation errors could compound over decades. But the classroom version ended with a corrected glossary and a stern note about humility.

Here, the file had bodies.

"Three maintenance lineages were treated as one enforceable party," Ren said. "One accepted repair debt. One had only witnessed the current. One had not yet budded into the chamber when the human record assigned continuity backward. Sanctions fell on all three. The relation sequence stopped being usable because any use of it reopened liability."

The globe warmed, not in approval but in pain.

Vela-Short said quietly, "Continue."

Ren looked at the membrane. "Human law then blamed Orison instability for the dispute. It did not admit that the instability came partly from being named wrongly and punished consistently under the wrong name."

No one in the gallery moved.

The cuff cooled.

Third-Voice's green-black fan turned toward Lael. "Compact advocate receives?"

Lael's face had gone pale, but her voice did not fail. "The Compact receives that stable-party doctrine has caused prior injury when applied to Orison relation without preservation of internal difference."

"Advocate speaks as institution."

"Yes."

"Advocate speaks as Lael-Shun?"

Lael's jaw worked once. Ren knew that movement. It was the body trying to swallow an answer the office had not cleared.

"I receive that I came prepared to ask for a stable party because the ships outside need one," Lael said. "I also receive that if I ask in the old way, I may purchase passage by damaging the people who make passage meaningful."

The blue-white fan lifted.

Rusk said, "The ships outside are not an abstraction."

The calibration globe turned toward him.

It did not have a face. Rusk still stepped back.

"Routeholder receives?" Third-Voice asked.

Rusk adjusted one cuff. His fabric did not wrinkle. That annoyed Ren more than it should have.

"I receive that prior treaty language was imperfect," he said. "I also receive that commerce cannot wait for every party to become a philosophy seminar."

The basin darkened by a shade.

The record clerk stopped writing.

Ren felt the old disaster begin: the human sentence that sounds practical to humans and violent to everyone who has been made convenient.

Lael said, "Envoy."

Rusk lifted a hand. "No. If this is calibration, calibrate on the full pressure. There are six medical containers in holding orbit whose contents degrade by the hour. There are children on cold-chain organ support in High Shoal clinics. There are reef-compatible antibiotics whose delivery windows are not improved by our respect for nuance. If the Parliament wants human systems to stop simplifying, then the Parliament must also stop pretending delay is harmless because it feels morally rich in the basin."

The words entered the gallery like spilled acid.

Ren hated him for saying them well.

Third-Voice did not strike the membrane. The fans folded inward, all three, until the speaker body looked smaller and more dangerous.

"Routeholder names need as ownership," Third-Voice said.

"No," Rusk replied. "I name need as need."

"Need has been used as mouth for ownership."

"Then answer the need without becoming owned."

Ren almost turned. It was the first useful thing Rusk had said, and he had said it with the impatience of a man who expected usefulness to belong to him.

The calibration globe divided again.

This time it showed no old file. It showed the present holding orbit: six containers, their thermal bands pulsing amber; a convoy captain pacing a narrow deck; a child in a High Shoal clinic with translucent tubes fixed to a chest too small for the machinery around it. The image was not visual exactly. It carried the taste of sterile air, the anxiety of temperature alarms, the dry-paper smell of insurance waivers, and beneath it all the faint brine signature of Orison clinicians waiting for supplies they could not synthesize locally.

Ren's throat tightened.

The Parliament had let human need enter the room before Lael formally introduced it. That mattered. Orison process was not ignoring the ships. It was refusing to let Rusk own them by being the first to name them.

"Observer receives?" Third-Voice asked.

Ren kept their hands open at their sides. "I receive that urgency is real and can still be used falsely. I receive that refusing simplification does not absolve the Parliament of people waiting outside the chamber. I receive that my record has to preserve both facts without letting either consume the other."

The globe held.

Then it sank back into the floor.

The membrane clouded, cleared, and settled at a new transparency. Not fully open. Not closed.

Vela-Short stepped away from the seam. "Calibration enters record."

Rusk looked at the clerk. "All of it?"

The young clerk looked once at Ren, once at Lael, then down through the membrane where Third-Voice waited.

"All of it," the clerk said.

Ren wondered if that was courage or simply the shock of having been present when a room changed shape around a sentence.

Either would do for a beginning.

Lael moved beside Ren. "Third-Voice-of-Reef, the Compact recognizes the Parliament's summons and requests procedural clarity on treaty obligation continuity during deliberation."

The speaker body's three colors dimmed at once.

Ren knew enough to feel alarm before the terminal translated it.

"Compact asks whether relation continues while relation examines its wound," Third-Voice said.

Lael's face did not change. "The Compact asks whether ships carrying medicine may rely on Shoals passage while the Parliament examines its wound."

That was better.

The basin answered before Third-Voice did.

A low pulse moved through the water, starting in the western reef and traveling from body to body. Ren felt it in their teeth. Blue-white bodies opened. Amber bodies contracted. Green-white repair lineages sent thin ribbons of light into the lower vents. The Parliament was not silent. It was doing something more dangerous than speech: letting the question become bodily before anyone could trap it in a clause.

One of the exchange vessels near the central floor convulsed.

Ren saw it because they were watching exits.

The vessel was small by Orison standards, a pale oval grown for temporary occupancy. Two faction filaments had entered it from opposite sides, one blue-white and one amber. They should have withdrawn after contact. Instead both remained threaded through the vessel, pulsing too fast.

The human terminal tried to classify.

EXCHANGE DELAY

Then:

NONCRITICAL PROCEDURAL VARIANCE

Then:

NO HUMAN ACTION REQUIRED

The vessel split along one side.

A ribbon of dark brine unfolded from the opening.

Every Orison body in the basin stopped moving.

Ren's cuff tightened until it hurt.

The gallery speakers whispered, not in Third-Voice's register, but in a smaller, damaged tone assembled from the vessel itself.

"Position does not release," the machine said.

Lael went very still.

Aven Rusk said, too softly, "That will affect the schedule."

Ren turned on him so fast the record clerk flinched.

But Third-Voice struck the membrane first.

The impact was not violent by Orison standards. By human standards, it turned the flexglass white and dropped every emergency mask from the gallery ceiling.

No one put one on.

The speaker's three fans opened together now, not blended, not unified, but equally bright.

"Witness," Third-Voice said.

Ren looked back through the membrane at the cracked exchange vessel, at the two faction filaments trapped inside a body grown to hold disagreement only briefly, at the dark brine of a political process becoming injury.

They had been summoned to interpret.

They understood, with the clean terror of the first true lesson, that the Parliament had not yet decided whether interpretation was part of the cure or part of the wound.

Chapter 2: Salt-Change Tide

The tide arrived through the floor.

Ren felt it before the instruments named it: a pressure shift in the soles of their boots, a mineral cold through the gallery supports, then a warm bloom rising through the membrane wall as if the basin had inhaled from a deeper ocean. The cracked exchange vessel tightened around its trapped filaments. Every Orison body in sight changed color by one shade, not in unison, but in relation. Blue leaned toward violet. Amber thickened toward rust. Green-white repair lineages dimmed as if listening with their whole surfaces.

The human terminals stayed behind the event for three seconds.

Then they panicked politely.

SALT-CHANGE TIDE / EARLY ARRIVAL

CHEMICAL MEMORY DENSITY RISING

TRANSLATION CONFIDENCE REDUCED

POLITICAL CONTACT HAZARD INCREASED

Aven Rusk stepped back from the record terminal. "Early by how much?"

Lael Shun was already at the secondary console, pulling up tide tables. "Twenty-nine hours."

"That is not a tide," Rusk said. "That is a failure."

Ren kept their eyes on the cracked vessel. "For humans, those are often synonyms."

Rusk heard the insult this time. "Interpreter, if the basin's environmental system is unstable, we need evacuation clarity."

"Parliament Basin is not an environmental system."

"It contains an environment."

"So do you."

Lael did not look up. "Both of you can become useful at any time."

The tide thickened.

It came as a visible line now, a low band of white particulate moving through the lower basin. Salt was the human word, and wrong in the way all usable words were wrong. It was not table salt, not mineral precipitate alone, not the dead residue of evaporation. It was a seasonal chemical carrier: crystallized memory proteins, reef ions, hormonal triggers, microbial shells, and fragments of old deliberative states released from deep porous chambers when the Shoals' parent planet pulled the subsurface seas into a particular strain.

During ordinary salt-change, Orison bodies adjusted. Memories loosened. Political positions became easier to exchange and harder to isolate. It was why the Parliament met here.

During early salt-change, with an exchange vessel cracked and two faction filaments trapped inside it, the tide was not ceremonial.

It was an accelerant.

The gallery speaker opened a channel without human permission. Third-Voice-of-Reef's register came through fractured, three fan-rhythms arriving in imperfect sequence.

"Dry witnesses remain behind membrane."

Aven said, "We were not planning to enter."

The amber fan flashed through the water outside. The translator offered:

HUMAN PLANS ARE OFTEN DISCOVERED AFTER THEIR DAMAGE.

Ren did not smile. It would have been emotionally satisfying and diplomatically cheap.

They moved closer to the membrane, stopping at the yellow line painted on the gallery floor. The sign above it read NO DIRECT CONTACT DURING ACTIVE EXCHANGE. Someone had added, in smaller script below, THIS INCLUDES WELL-MEANING CONTACT. Ren wondered if Mira had written it.

"Third-Voice," Ren said, "is the trapped vessel in pain?"

The basin answered before the speaker did.

The cracked oval pulsed once. Dark brine seeped from the split and curled into the salt band. The blue-white filament inside it brightened in sharp bursts. The amber filament contracted around it, not strangling, not releasing.

Ren's cuff tried a gloss, failed, tried again.

PAIN / ARGUMENT / DUTY / UNFINISHED BORROWING

Then:

DO NOT QUOTE.

Ren obeyed.

Third-Voice said, "The body carries more position than it was grown to hold."

"Can it release one?"

"Release to where?"

That was the first question Ren should have asked.

They felt Lael turn toward them. Not interfering. Watching whether Ren would make a clean human answer because a wounded body had made everyone hungry for one.

Ren said, "To a protected vessel, if one exists. To its original faction, if return would not erase the change. To the basin, if diffusion is acceptable. I do not know the right answer."

The green-black fan slowed. "You name ignorance with branches."

"I can name more branches if needed."

"Not yet."

The salt-change tide reached the central terraces.

Orison bodies opened to it by instinct or training. Surfaces grew porous. Fans lifted. Braids untied. A thousand cilia touched currents that had been carrying old arguments since before any human treaty used the word sovereignty. Ren watched one amber withdrawal cluster receive the tide and flash with images no human device could fully translate: a mining pylon under Pelagos glass, a medical convoy waiting outside Shoals jurisdiction, a human signature smearing into reef mucus, a dead route marker near the Pale Arc, a child-body grown for a vote and retired too early.

The terminal tried to make bullets.

Ren turned that display off too.

Aven made a controlled sound of irritation. "We need records."

"We have records," Ren said.

"You keep disabling them."

"I keep disabling bad summaries."

"A bad summary is better than no record when insurers and convoy captains need decisions."

Lael finally looked up. "A bad summary is sometimes how insurers get the decisions they paid for."

Rusk's face closed. "Advocate Shun."

"Envoy Rusk."

"The Compact invited private routeholders because our corridors carry the consequences of Orison delay."

"The Parliament summoned witnesses," Lael said. "You arrived as consequence and have been permitted to stand near a terminal. Do not confuse that with invitation."

Ren revised Lael upward again and made a mental note not to trust the pleasure of doing so. Competence in one direction could still become harm in another.

The cracked vessel convulsed.

This time the gallery heard it.

Sound passed through the membrane as a low, wet chord, too deep for the speakers and too organized to be accident. It moved through Ren's ribs. The record clerk covered her ears, then stopped when she realized no one else had. Rusk gripped the terminal rail. Lael's lips parted, counting silently.

Ren's cuff displayed:

EXCHANGE BODY REQUESTS CONTINUANCE

Then the words shattered into uncertainty marks.

Third-Voice struck the water with all three fans. Not the membrane this time. The basin. A command, a plea, a political act. Green-white repair lineages detached from their terrace and flowed toward the central floor in long sheets. Blue-white treaty bodies opened routes for them. Amber withdrawal bodies did not block, but they did not assist.

The tide carried everything faster than intention.

Ren watched an amber filament near the cracked vessel absorb a trace of blue-white position from the leaking brine. Its color shifted. The change lasted only a second, but the surrounding bodies reacted as if someone had shouted a treasonous prayer.

"What happened?" Rusk demanded.

Ren answered because no one else would do it safely. "The tide is moving positions outside exchange protocol."

"Meaning?"

"A faction may feel another faction's obligations without agreeing to carry them."

"That sounds like empathy."

"No," Ren said. "Empathy is what humans call it when we want the experience to stay morally flattering. This can injure identity, dissolve boundaries, or create responsibility without consent."

Rusk went pale in the dry human way. "Can it affect us?"

The gallery speaker clicked.

Third-Voice said, "Humans are less porous than you believe and more porous than you hope."

The membrane warning lights changed from yellow to blue.

Lael read the alert. "Chemical aerosol risk in dry gallery."

"From the tide?" Ren asked.

"From the membrane compensators. They are overcorrecting."

Vela-Short's voice came over the pilot channel from the capsule lock. "I can pull the capsule free in ninety seconds if your procedural seal becomes a drowning story."

Third-Voice's blue-white fan turned toward the lock.

"Pilot who does not interpret," the speaker said, "remains accurate."

Vela-Short replied, "Pilot who does not interpret charges double for compliments."

Even Rusk looked briefly confused enough to forget fear.

Ren used the moment to open a manual witness slate. Not the Compact terminal. Not the cuff. A blank field with time, location, raw signal attachments, and a warning header they wrote themselves:

PARTIAL WITNESS / TRANSLATION UNSTABLE / DO NOT REDUCE TO FINAL POSITION

Lael noticed. "That will not satisfy formal record requirements."

"It is not for formal satisfaction."

"Then what is it for?"

Ren looked at the cracked vessel. The repair sheets had reached it now. They did not touch the split. They formed a moving chamber around it, a living basin within the basin, slowing the tide without stopping it.

"For not lying quickly," Ren said.

Lael absorbed that without comment.

The tide rose high enough to touch the membrane.

White crystals struck the flexglass and dissolved into faint branching patterns. The human gallery smelled suddenly of sea caves, blood metal, and hot rain. Ren's eyes watered. Their cuff flashed exposure warnings, then shut down three interpretive layers to avoid false certainty.

The world became less translated.

Outside, the Orison were no longer political colors on a display. They were bodies under pressure, changing because relation had weight. A blue-white delegate opened too wide and had to be folded back by two green repair sheets. An amber cluster withdrew from the main current, then sent one filament forward despite itself. Third-Voice hung above them all, three disagreements in one body, each fan fighting to remain distinct while the tide tried to make distinction expensive.

Ren understood almost nothing.

It was the most honest the day had been.

The record clerk collapsed without drama.

One moment she stood beside the terminal with her stylus clenched in both hands, face held in the careful blankness of junior staff under powerful witnesses. The next she sat hard on the wet floor, knees folding as if someone had removed a command from her muscles. Her stylus skittered across the gallery and stopped against Ren's boot.

Lael moved first.

Not toward the terminal. Toward the clerk.

That, too, entered Ren's record.

"Tamsin," Lael said, and the name mattered because the clerk's badge had offered only T. VARE. "Look at me."

Tamsin tried. Her pupils had widened until the brown of her eyes was almost gone. A thin line of blood-colored fluid ran from one nostril. She lifted one hand and stared at it as though it had become a document in a language she did not read.

"I can taste the vote," she whispered.

Rusk stepped back. "Is she contaminated?"

The word landed badly.

Every Orison body near the membrane altered color.

Lael's voice became cold. "Do not use that word in this chamber."

"Then give me the correct one while your clerk is on the floor."

Ren knelt beside Tamsin but did not touch her. The sign's smaller script burned in memory: THIS INCLUDES WELL-MEANING CONTACT. Human first aid training said contact anchored a distressed person. Orison warning said contact during active exchange could make a helpful hand into an uninvited route.

"Tamsin," Ren said. "Can you hear my voice as sound?"

The clerk swallowed. "Mostly."

"What else?"

Her gaze moved past Ren to the membrane. "A blue argument. No. Blue is wrong. It is a promise that keeps asking to be believed because it is tired of proving itself. And amber. Amber is not anger. Amber is... a door refusing to be called locked by the person outside it."

Rusk whispered something obscene.

Lael took a medical patch from the wall kit and stopped with it halfway open.

"Will that interfere?" she asked Ren.

Ren hated that she was right to ask them and hated more that they had an answer.

"If this is aerosol-mediated sensory bleed, a suppressant may protect her but erase useful evidence of how the membrane failed."

"She is not evidence," Lael said.

The basin brightened.

Ren felt the sentence move through the water before the translator caught up. Not approval. Recognition.

"No," Ren said. "She is not. Use the patch."

Lael pressed it behind Tamsin's ear.

Tamsin gasped. Her hand shot out and caught Ren's sleeve.

Ren went still.

The contact was dry fabric through damp fingers, human skin to human sleeve, not Orison membrane, not brine. It should have been nothing. It was not nothing. The cuff on Ren's wrist flared hot, then shut itself down with a hard click. For half a second Ren tasted rust, cold medicine, and a child's fear of a door that would not open because adults had put a delay into the wrong system.

Then it was gone.

Ren did not move until Tamsin released them.

Lael saw enough. "What crossed?"

"Not a position," Ren said. Their voice sounded far away to themselves. "A pressure trace. Medical convoy. Human waiting fear caught in the salt-change because the membrane compensators are overcorrecting toward our side."

Rusk seized on it. "Then the ships are part of the chamber now."

"No."

"You just said their pressure crossed."

"Crossing is not ownership."

"It is relevance."

"Yes," Ren said. "And relevance is not a deed."

Vela-Short came through the lock carrying a sealed breather rig and a coil of emergency line. She stopped at the threshold, looked at Tamsin, looked at the branching salt patterns on the membrane, and said, "Your dry room is becoming philosophical."

"Can you extract her?" Lael asked.

"Physically, yes. Politically, ask the water."

Third-Voice answered before any human could object.

"Clerk may leave. Record remains."

Tamsin's face twisted. The suppressant had steadied her eyes, but not her voice. "No."

Lael bent closer. "That was not a request I need you to approve."

"If I leave, he will use my notes," Tamsin said, and looked not at Rusk but at the terminal behind him. "He will say the human record shows Orison instability forced emergency corridor authority. I wrote too much before I understood what I was writing."

Rusk's expression sharpened. "Careful."

Tamsin laughed once. It sounded like pain finding a small door. "I am junior staff, Envoy. Careful is the only rank they gave me."

Ren picked up the fallen stylus and set it beside her, not in her hand.

"You can revoke provisional authority over your notes," Ren said. "If Advocate Shun witnesses it."

Lael's eyes flicked to Ren. "That is not standard."

"Neither is tasting a vote through a failing membrane."

"The Compact will challenge."

"Let it."

Rusk said, "You are inventing procedure while cargo dies."

The medical containers returned to Ren then, not as an image but as an obligation that refused to be cheaply used. They forced themselves to turn toward Rusk.

"Give the exact decay windows," Ren said.

He blinked. "What?"

"You keep invoking the containers. Give the exact decay windows, clinic destinations, and which convoy contracts profit if Orison passage is declared unreliable."

Rusk's face closed again. "That information is commercially sensitive."

The basin gave a low pulse.

Ren did not need translation.

"Then stop carrying it as moral evidence," they said.

Lael stood.

For a moment Ren thought she would protect the Compact record from the mess Ren had just made. Instead she opened the secondary console and disabled its private routeholder feed. Rusk moved toward her. Vela-Short shifted the emergency line in one hand, not threatening, simply reminding the room that pilots who did not interpret still understood leverage.

"Advocate Shun," Rusk said.

"The envoy will provide container-level urgency data to the shared witness record," Lael said. "Commercial terms may be sealed for later review, but medical decay windows enter now or leave your mouth."

"You exceed your role."

"Frequently. This time I am writing it down."

Rusk did not answer immediately.

That pause did more work than his objections. It showed every human in the gallery the shape of the hidden ledger behind his urgency: six containers named as dying need, but bundled inside contracts, penalties, priority claims, secondary cargo, insurance triggers, and reputation risk. If he opened the window, the chamber would see need and machinery together. If he kept it closed, the need would lose its borrowed sanctity.

Ren wrote:

ROUTEHOLDER URGENCY CLAIM ENTERS WITNESS ONLY IF DECAY WINDOWS, CLINIC DESTINATIONS, BENEFICIARIES, AND PROFIT-BEARING CONTRACT STRUCTURES ARE SEPARATED. NEED MAY BE URGENT; BUNDLE MAY STILL BE LEVERAGE.

The basin received the line with a low vibration.

Rusk's eyes flicked to the membrane. "You are turning disclosure into accusation."

"No," Lael said. "We are stopping accusation from dressing as disclosure."

Vela-Short made another gauge-checking movement that looked suspiciously like approval.

Rusk opened his slate.

The first data block appeared on the shared witness pane:

CONTAINER 1: PLACENTAL CULTURE / HIGH SHOAL CLINIC / SIXTEEN HOURS TO VIABILITY LOSS.

CONTAINER 2: IMMUNE GRAFT MATRIX / WESTERN REPAIR SCHOOL / TWENTY-TWO HOURS.

CONTAINER 3: BRINE-ADAPTED ANTIBIOTICS / MULTIPLE CLINICS / THIRTY-ONE HOURS.

Then a pause.

Ren looked at him.

Rusk's jaw tightened. He opened the next block:

CONTAINERS 4-6: NONMEDICAL HIGH-PRIORITY CARGO BUNDLED UNDER CONVOY PROTECTION.

The membrane darkened.

Lael said, very quietly, "There."

Rusk did not defend it. That was the only wise thing available.

Ren wrote:

MEDICAL NEED CONFIRMED. NONMEDICAL CARGO BUNDLED UNDER SAME URGENCY CLAIM. FUTURE USE OF CONVOY AS MORAL EVIDENCE MUST DISTINGUISH BODY-CRITICAL NEED FROM CONTRACT-PRIORITY CARGO.

Third-Voice's amber fan flashed once through the tide.

"Now need has edge," the speaker said.

Tamsin reached for the stylus. Ren let her take it.

Her hand shook as she signed a revocation of private summary use. Lael countersigned. Ren marked the witness as partial, unstable, and human-origin. Vela-Short clipped a breather mask over Tamsin's face with brisk tenderness and guided her toward the lock.

At the threshold, Tamsin turned back.

"The amber door," she said through the mask. "It was not refusing because it hated the person outside. It was refusing because the person outside kept calling the room empty."

No translator spoke.

No one needed it to.

The lock closed behind her.

The human gallery had lost its clerk, one clean terminal feed, and the comforting fiction that dry witnesses could remain outside the event by staying dry.

Ren opened a new line in the witness slate:

HUMAN RECORD ALSO CHANGES UNDER TIDE.

They stared at the sentence. It was too simple. It was also the most dangerous thing they had written so far.

The cracked vessel pulsed again.

This time no machine spoke.

Instead a pattern appeared on the membrane where salt crystals touched warm flexglass. Lines branched, joined, broke, and rejoined. Ren recognized none of the grammar. But they recognized the problem. Any human would, if they looked long enough and did not ask the record to rescue them.

Two positions had entered one body to borrow each other's burden.

The body could not give either burden back unchanged.

Aven Rusk whispered, "Can the vote continue?"

Ren did not turn around.

Lael answered him, voice flat and dangerous. "A body is cracked."

"I understand that."

"No," Ren said. "You understand that a process is delayed. Start there and you will misunderstand everything after."

The basin darkened.

The early tide reached the speaker current. Third-Voice folded inward, then unfolded with visible difficulty. The amber fan moved first, then the blue-white, then the green-black. Not harmony. Sequence.

"Ren Okoye," Third-Voice said.

"I am here."

"Dry witness will record salt-change condition."

"I can record partial observations."

"Dry witness will record that translation made before tide cannot govern speech inside tide."

Ren wrote it exactly, then added a mark indicating direct translation uncertain, political intent clear.

Third-Voice continued. "Dry witness will record that a body changed by exchange is not failed because it cannot return unchanged."

Ren wrote that too.

Lael inhaled sharply. She saw the treaty consequence. If a body changed by exchange was not failed, then a treaty party changed by relation might remain a party without staying identical. Compact law would hate that sentence. Or worse, love it cheaply.

"Third-Voice," Ren said, "does that statement apply to the cracked vessel only, or to Parliament parties under human treaty?"

The basin went still in the way an ocean could go still while still moving.

Lael's hand hovered above the console.

Rusk stopped breathing loudly.

The tide pressed white branching lace against the membrane.

Third-Voice's three fans opened again.

"Good," the speaker said.

Ren waited.

"Now you hear the injury."

The cracked exchange vessel did not release.

But the repair sheets around it stopped trying to make it.

They widened the chamber instead, giving the trapped filaments room to remain in unfinished contact while the early salt-change tide moved past them and carried the Parliament into a session where no old translation could be trusted.

Ren saved the witness slate in three forms: raw, provisional, and not for law.

The third form would make everyone angry.

That was how Ren knew it might be useful.

Chapter 3: Third-Voice Receives Three Disagreements

Third-Voice-of-Reef asked for Ren after the tide passed its first crest.

Asked was the word Lael used, because Lael was already trying to make the event fit into a procedural record. The basin did not ask. It opened a route in the water, dimmed every current except one, and turned the speaker body toward the human gallery with all three fans extended like incompatible flags.

The human terminal translated:

INTERPRETER REQUESTED FOR CLOSE WITNESS

Then, after a pause:

CONSENT CONDITIONS PENDING

Ren liked the second line better.

"Close witness is not entry," Lael said before anyone could panic usefully.

Vela-Short's voice came from the capsule lock. "Define close."

"Membrane-side wet contact," Lael said. "No immersion. No tissue crossing. No political exchange."

Third-Voice's amber fan snapped once.

The speaker said, "No intended political exchange."

Lael closed her mouth.

Ren appreciated the correction and hated what it implied.

Aven Rusk stood too near the record terminal, one hand resting on the rail as if the rail owed him stability. "Is this necessary?"

Ren looked at him. "For whom?"

"For the process."

"That is not a person."

"It is the thing keeping ships from waiting outside Shoals jurisdiction until cargo spoils and patients die."

That was the problem with Rusk. He could say something self-serving and still be partly right. Private routeholders loved humanitarian urgency because it came wrapped in moral insulation, but the convoy queues were real. Human infants in pressure cradles, transplant tissue in cold cases, reef-safe antibiotics, replacement membranes for stations that could not grow their own. Every hour of Orison treaty uncertainty cost something more intimate than profit.

Ren turned back to the basin.

"I will do membrane-side close witness," they said. "On conditions."

The blue-white fan brightened.

"Name them," Third-Voice said.

"I do not enter exchange. I do not carry faction memory. I do not sign for the Compact, routeholders, human observers, or Parliament. I can stop if the witness state begins changing me beyond observation."

The green-black fan moved slowly.

"Observation changes," Third-Voice said.

"Yes. Beyond observation is the phrase we will argue about if needed."

"Good."

"And my record remains marked partial."

The amber fan tightened. "Human partial records become weapons when attached to deadlines."

"Then help me keep this one visibly unfinished."

The three fans moved in different directions.

Ren had a sudden, vertiginous sense that they were not watching one being consider a request. They were watching three obligations fail to agree on the shape of caution while sharing one body that could not walk away from itself.

The terminal produced:

THIRD-VOICE CONSENTS / CONDITIONS ACCEPTED

Then:

WITNESS WILL LEARN WHY ACCEPTANCE IS NOT AGREEMENT.

Lael's hand caught Ren's sleeve before they stepped to the contact station.

"Your cuff is already in reduced mode," she said.

"I know."

"If it misclassifies an affect state, do not trust your first correction either."

Ren studied her. "That sounds like field advice."

"It is."

"You have done wet witness?"

"Once."

"And?"

Lael released their sleeve. "I wrote a clean clause afterward. It took three years to undo the damage."

There was nothing useful to say to that.

Ren crossed the yellow line.

The contact station was a half-circle of dark composite pressed directly against the membrane. It had been designed by a committee that understood risk and not bodies. There were handholds at the wrong height, a drainage groove where no sane person would put their feet, a consent screen angled for a taller species, and a pressure hood that smelled faintly of sterilized regret. Ren ignored the hood. The basin had not requested a hood. It had requested closeness, and closeness through too much apparatus became theater.

"Interpreter Okoye," the station AI said, "please confirm you understand that membrane-side witness may expose you to chemical, acoustic, emotional, political, and interpretive stressors."

"Confirmed."

"Please confirm you do not enter as representative of any legal party."

"Confirmed."

"Please confirm you waive claims arising from ordinary political discomfort."

Ren looked back at Lael.

Lael said, "Do not confirm that."

Ren smiled despite the pressure in their chest. "I do not confirm that. Ordinary political discomfort is not defined."

The station AI paused, offended by precision.

Third-Voice touched the membrane.

Every warning light on the station softened to blue.

"Dry machine asks human to forgive harm before harm names itself," Third-Voice said. "Bad vessel."

"Agreed," Ren said.

The waiver vanished from the screen.

Ren placed both hands on the membrane.

It was warmer than skin.

For the first second, nothing happened except the small embarrassment of touching a wall while powerful people watched.

Then the three disagreements arrived.

Not as thoughts. Not as images in any simple sense. They came as pressures that Ren's mind tried to clothe in human memory because human minds were provincial instruments.

The blue-white disagreement came first as a convoy corridor under black water. Not space. Water pretending to be space because Ren's body had accepted the basin as the measure of distance. Along the corridor moved ships carrying human need: dialysis cartridges, reef-safe sealant, fungal cultures for famine stations, mourning relatives, technicians, smugglers, debt collectors, children. The blue-white position did not love humans. It carried the obligation created by having allowed relation to form. A route once opened could not be closed without hearing the lives that had learned to trust it.

The amber disagreement struck next.

It came as a shell with a human signature carved into it. The signature grew roots. The roots drank from reef tissue, treaty pools, adoption chambers, migration songs, salt-change tides. Every time the Orison allowed a human record to prove a relation, the record hardened. What had been witness became category. What had been category became permit. What had been permit became entitlement. The amber position did not hate humans. It feared the human talent for turning gratitude into infrastructure and infrastructure into ownership.

The green-black disagreement arrived last and did not care whether Ren could bear it.

Pale Arc dark. Lumen's impossible missing neutrinos. Boundary rhythm moving through star, brine, route, chamber. Not message. Warning. Not threat. Relation under strain. The green-black position held that both blue and amber were already too late, because human-Orison treaty form was only one local expression of a larger disturbance: routes simplified, identities simplified, reefs simplified, command simplified, law simplified until the universe pushed back through whatever body remained porous enough to feel it.

Ren gasped and tried to pull their hands away.

The membrane did not hold them.

That made it worse.

They could leave. The pressure remained because they had received enough to be changed by the attempt.

"Breathe," Lael said from very far away.

"That is mammal advice," Vela-Short said.

"Interpreter is currently mammal."

"Provisionally," Third-Voice said.

Ren laughed once, painfully, and the laugh helped. It reminded their body that air still existed and that not every pressure had to become meaning before the lungs worked.

They removed one hand from the membrane. The blue-white pressure receded but did not vanish. They removed the other. Amber and green-black withdrew more slowly, like currents leaving salt in the blood.

The contact station asked:

DO YOU REQUIRE MEDICAL REVIEW?

"No," Ren said.

Lael said, "Yes."

Ren said, "Noted. After witness notes."

"Before notes."

"If I wait, I will clean it."

Lael's expression changed. Recognition, not approval. "Two minutes."

Rusk spoke from the terminal. His voice was careful now, which made Ren like him less. "What did it tell you?"

Ren turned from the membrane. Their hands were shaking.

"It did not tell me. It received me into three disagreements."

"That is not actionable."

"Correct."

"Convoys need actionable."

Ren wanted to snap at him. Instead they looked through the membrane at Third-Voice, whose three fans were still open and still separate. The speaker body looked magnificent to a human eye. It also looked injured in a way no human display was admitting. The blue-white fan pulsed with route obligation. The amber fan held withdrawal fear. The green-black fan moved in slow warning rhythm.

Three disagreements.

One body.

Not harmony. Not hypocrisy. Not indecision.

Labor.

Ren opened the partial witness slate and began to write.

THIRD-VOICE-OF-REEF IS NOT A SINGLE COMPROMISE POSITION.

Lael stepped closer.

Ren continued.

THIRD-VOICE IS A LIVING PROCEDURE FOR KEEPING THREE INCOMPATIBLE OBLIGATIONS PRESENT WITHOUT PRETENDING THEY HAVE RESOLVED.

Rusk said, "That will not help a convoy captain."

"It might," Lael said quietly.

Ren looked at her.

Lael's face had gone pale in the gallery light. "If the captain understands that an Orison delay is not absence of decision. It is active burden."

Third-Voice's blue-white fan brightened toward her.

Lael saw it and looked away, which was honest of her.

Ren kept writing.

BLUE-WHITE: CONTINUITY OF RELATION CREATES OBLIGATION TO HUMAN NEED.

AMBER: HUMAN RECORDS OF RELATION HARDEN INTO OWNERSHIP IF NOT RESTRAINED.

GREEN-BLACK: PALE ARC WARNING SUGGESTS POLITICAL SIMPLIFICATION AND ROUTE SIMPLIFICATION MAY BE RELATED RISKS.

TRANSLATION CONFIDENCE: LOW.

POLITICAL CONSEQUENCE: HIGH.

DO NOT COLLAPSE INTO MAJORITY/MINORITY MODEL.

The last line felt important enough to frighten them.

They marked it not for law.

The human terminal immediately tried to ingest the slate.

Ren slapped the manual disconnect.

"No."

The record clerk looked horrified. "That is the primary archive feed."

"It is the primary archive appetite."

Lael said, "Okoye."

"Not yet."

Rusk's patience broke. "We cannot run a treaty crisis on poetic reluctance."

Third-Voice's amber fan struck the water hard enough to ripple the membrane.

"Routeholder speaks hunger wearing clocks," the speaker said.

Rusk flushed. "And the Parliament speaks harm wearing beauty."

The gallery went still.

Ren had to give him one thing: he was not a coward.

The green-black fan dimmed first.

It was subtle enough that the human terminal missed it and obvious enough that every Orison repair lineage near the central floor changed course. Third-Voice's trailing cilia curled inward, not in ceremony but in reflex. The blue-white fan kept its route-obligation pulse. The amber fan remained flared toward Rusk. The green-black fan, the one that had carried Pale Arc warning and older boundary strain, lost a thread of light along its outer edge.

Ren's cuff remained in reduced mode, which meant it did not lie quickly. It gave them no gloss at all.

"Lael," Ren said.

"I see it."

"Do you?"

She looked from the speaker body to the repair lineages and then to the terminal that still wanted Ren's slate. "No. I see that they see something."

That was better than pretending.

Third-Voice's cilia touched the membrane again, but not where Ren stood. The contact points formed three separate arcs, one blue-white, one amber, one green-black. The arcs did not meet. Between them the membrane fogged, cleared, fogged again. It looked like breath on glass, if breath had law inside it.

The station AI, eager to become useful after being insulted, displayed:

SPEAKER BODY STRESS EVENT

RECOMMEND OBSERVER WITHDRAWAL

Ren did not withdraw.

They did step back from the contact station and put both hands where everyone could see them. No hidden recording, no accidental touch, no human comfort masquerading as help.

"Third-Voice," they said, "does close witness continue?"

The blue-white arc brightened: yes, or need, or remain because ships are waiting.

The amber arc darkened: no, or danger, or humans always call remaining consent after they make departure costly.

The green-black arc flickered: not answerable in that frame.

The terminal wrote:

MIXED SIGNAL

Then, with cruel confidence:

NO CONSENSUS

Ren crossed the gallery in three strides and turned the display off.

Rusk made a sharp sound. "You cannot keep disabling every instrument that gives an answer you dislike."

"It did not give an answer," Ren said. "It converted three answers into one absence."

Lael was already opening a manual log. "Phrase that again."

Ren did not look away from the arcs. "It converted three answers into one absence."

Lael wrote it.

The repair lineages reached Third-Voice. They did not touch the fans directly. Instead they formed a loose spiral under the speaker body and released small dark beads into the water. The beads rose in different currents. One went blue-white, one amber, one green-black. Third-Voice took them into the cilia root one at a time.

The speaker body shuddered.

Ren felt the shudder in their teeth.

"Analgesic?" Lael asked.

Vela-Short answered from the lock. "Political electrolyte."

Lael stared at her.

"Do not ask me to interpret," Vela-Short said. "I carry people who do."

Third-Voice spoke before the gallery could settle.

"Humans ask if speaker is one."

No one had asked aloud.

That did not matter. The question had filled the room since the first moment the speaker body rose in three colors. Human law needed parties. Human records needed names. Human fear needed a unit to blame. Even Ren's notes had used Third-Voice as a grammatical subject because English had no mercy at the sentence level.

"Yes," Ren said. "Humans will ask that."

"Orison ask if speaker betrays three by carrying three."

The amber arc pulsed painfully.

Ren heard movement behind them. Rusk, perhaps. Lael. The record clerk was gone. The room had fewer witnesses than it needed and more consequences than it could hold.

"Do they ask now?" Ren said.

"Always."

The word came through the speaker system, but it was not mechanical. It was exhausted.

The blue-white fan lowered until it nearly touched the cracked vessel's widened chamber. The amber fan turned toward the withdrawal clusters on the upper terraces. The green-black fan remained angled toward the deep basin where Pale Arc rhythm moved below translation. Third-Voice was not presiding over a dispute. Third-Voice was being used as the place where the dispute could remain visible without forcing everyone else to break at once.

Ren's hands ached with the memory of the membrane.

They opened the witness slate again.

THIRD-VOICE IS NOT NEUTRAL.

That line would make human readers mistrust the witness.

Good.

Ren continued.

THIRD-VOICE IS ANSWERABLE TO THREE OBLIGATIONS THAT INJURE ONE ANOTHER WHEN FORCED INTO ONE DECISION FRAME.

They stopped. Decision frame was too human, too clean. They crossed it out in the manual slate and rewrote:

...WHEN FORCED INTO ONE BODY WITHOUT ROOM FOR THEIR DIFFERENCE TO ACT.

Lael moved beside them. "That is going to be difficult to enter."

"Yes."

"The Compact route office will ask whether the Parliament has an authorized speaker."

"It has an answerable speaker."

"That may not be enough."

Ren looked at her. "Enough for what?"

Lael did not answer.

That was answer enough.

Rusk came closer, keeping outside the yellow line. For once he did not touch the terminal rail. "If Third-Voice is injured by holding the positions, why not separate them? Three speakers. Three statements. Three signatories. Humans can work with that."

The amber fan flashed: trap.

The blue-white fan flashed: possible.

The green-black fan flashed: too small.

Ren felt a headache bloom behind one eye.

"Because separation changes the obligation," they said.

Rusk's frustration returned. "Everything changes the obligation."

"Yes," Ren said. "That is the first honest thing treaty law should say."

Lael made a tiny note on her console. Ren hoped it was not just for prosecution.

Rusk pointed through the membrane, not at Third-Voice but at the cracked vessel below. "That body broke because too much disagreement remained in one place."

"It cracked," Ren said. "We do not yet know whether it broke."

"That distinction will not matter to the people waiting six hours for a route statement."

"It will matter if the statement that saves six hours teaches every future record to call cracked bodies useless."

Rusk's mouth tightened. "You keep protecting future harm from present harm."

"No. I am trying to stop them from being made enemies."

Third-Voice's three arcs brightened together.

Together did not mean blended. Ren was learning that. It meant all three had found the same moment worth marking for different reasons.

A small Orison body detached from the repair spiral and approached the membrane. It was no larger than Ren's forearm, translucent and folded around a dark central line. Vela-Short made a low sound in the lock.

"What is it?" Lael asked.

"Not it," Vela-Short said.

The small body touched the membrane below the arcs. The station AI remained silent, perhaps because someone had finally taught it fear. A faint pattern spread through the flexglass: three narrow channels running side by side, crossing only at pressure gates. Each gate opened briefly, then closed before any channel could flood the others.

Ren understood the image before words arrived.

Not unity.

Not separation.

Regulated crossing.

The speaker system translated in Third-Voice's register, but softer.

"A body that carries difference requires gates that can refuse."

Lael whispered, "That is treaty architecture."

"It is also anatomy," Ren said.

Rusk did not speak.

Ren wrote:

PROPOSED ANALOGY: AUTHORIZED SPEAKERHOOD MAY REQUIRE INTERNAL REFUSAL GATES, NOT PROOF OF INTERNAL AGREEMENT.

They marked the line dangerous.

Then they marked it necessary.

Third-Voice's green-black fan regained a little light along the edge.

Not recovery. Not cure.

Enough to continue.

Third-Voice did not strike the membrane. The three fans folded halfway, then opened again with deliberate separation.

"True enough to remain," the speaker said.

Rusk blinked.

Ren saved that too.

The basin's repair sheets still held the cracked vessel in a widened chamber. The trapped filaments had stopped thrashing. They had not released. The salt-change tide moved around them now, carrying fragments of their unfinished exchange through guarded channels where repair lineages caught and held what they could.

Third-Voice lowered closer to the membrane.

"Ren Okoye," the speaker said, "you have received three disagreements. You will be asked by humans to make them one."

"I know."

"You will be asked by Orison to make one wound prove another false."

Ren had not known that, and the knowing landed badly.

"I hear you."

"No," Third-Voice said. "You begin."

The membrane cleared. The speaker body drifted back into the basin, and the human gallery's ordinary lights returned with the insulting confidence of infrastructure that believed visibility solved anything.

Lael's console chimed.

She read the incoming packet and closed her eyes.

Ren recognized the expression from every treaty room they had ever hated. "What?"

"Compact route office," Lael said. "They want a continuity statement before the next convoy window."

"When is the window?"

Aven Rusk answered.

"Six hours."

The packet opened on Lael's console before anyone agreed to receive it.

That was how human urgency entered: not by consent, but by display.

REQUESTED STATEMENT TYPE:

TREATY CONTINUITY CONFIRMED

TREATY CONTINUITY SUSPENDED

TREATY CONTINUITY INDETERMINATE / EMERGENCY REVIEW

Ren looked at the three options and felt the membrane pressure return to their palms.

"No," they said.

Rusk's head turned. "You cannot refuse the question before answering it."

"Watch me."

Lael did not close the packet. That would have hidden the trap. Instead she mirrored it onto a side pane and labeled it:

HUMAN CONTINUITY FORM / UNSAFE OPTIONS.

The basin saw enough through the gallery feed. Third-Voice's amber fan, still retreating toward the repair spiral, flashed once. The blue-white fan brightened in alarm. The green-black fan stayed dim, which was worse.

Ren wrote under the options:

CONFIRMED WOULD TREAT BLUE-WHITE OBLIGATION AS TOTAL ANSWER. SUSPENDED WOULD TREAT AMBER REFUSAL AS TOTAL ANSWER. INDETERMINATE / EMERGENCY REVIEW WOULD ERASE ACTIVE ORISON PROCESS AS FAILURE. ALL THREE COLLAPSE THE WITNESSED DISAGREEMENTS.

Lael read the line aloud for the route office channel and did not soften it.

The route office returned:

PLEASE PROVIDE OPERATIONAL EQUIVALENT.

Vela-Short snorted from the lock. "They mean pick one while saying you did not."

Rusk said, "They mean ships cannot navigate a paragraph."

Ren looked at him. "Ships also cannot navigate a lie forever."

"They only need six hours."

"That is how forever starts in records."

Lael held up one hand before the exchange could burn more oxygen. "We need a carrier sentence. Not their categories. Something convoy systems can use without owning the Parliament."

Third-Voice's blue-white fan pulsed: need.

Amber pulsed: trap.

Green-black flickered: warning.

Ren wrote slowly:

NO GENERAL CONTINUITY STATEMENT AVAILABLE. MEDICAL CONVOY REQUEST MAY BE HELD UNDER TEMPORARY CARE-PASSAGE REVIEW WITHOUT CONFIRMING, SUSPENDING, OR FAILING THE TREATY RELATION.

Care-passage bodies in the basin brightened.

Salt-Before-Shell's withdrawal cluster recoiled at temporary.

Ren saw it and added:

TEMPORARY CARE-PASSAGE REVIEW DOES NOT CREATE PRECEDENT, ACCESS RIGHT, ROUTE RELIABILITY CLAIM, OR STABLE-PARTY CERTIFICATION.

The amber fan eased by one degree.

Rusk read the two lines and looked dissatisfied in a useful direction. "Convoy captains can work with held under review if there is a time and a responsible channel."

"Responsible channel," Third-Voice said through the speaker, "is where humans build hook."

Ren wrote that too:

RESPONSIBLE CHANNEL MUST NOT BECOME RECEIVER SOCKET FOR OWNERSHIP.

Lael closed her eyes for one breath. "That phrase will cause trouble."

"Good," said Vela-Short.

The next crisis had arrived early, carrying deadlines in both hands.

Chapter 4: Lael Shun Brings Deadlines

Lael Shun did not curse.

Ren had worked with advocates who cursed beautifully. Treaty rooms produced profanity the way bad airlocks produced frost: as a warning sign and a pressure release. Lael read the Compact packet, read it again, and became quieter. That was worse. Quiet, in a procedural advocate, meant the knife had found the joint.

"Say it aloud," Ren said.

Lael looked at the basin instead of the screen. The salt-change tide still moved in high white bands beyond the membrane. Repair sheets held the cracked exchange vessel inside a living chamber. Third-Voice had withdrawn to the central current, three fans half-folded, each still carrying a different color of refusal.

"Compact Route Office requires a continuity assurance before convoy window Forty-Three," Lael said. "If no assurance is filed, the office will classify Shoals passage as politically unstable for insurance, medical priority, and perishables allocation."

Aven Rusk spread his hands. "That is not punishment. That is risk accounting."

"Risk accounting is what institutions call punishment when a spreadsheet does it," Ren said.

Rusk's jaw tightened. "Tell that to the captain deciding whether to take a pediatric tissue shipment through a treaty zone whose government may dissolve mid-transit."

"The Orison are not a government."

"Then perhaps they should stop controlling a route."

The sentence hit the gallery with human bluntness.

The clerk at the terminal looked at the floor. Vela-Short muttered something over the capsule channel that the system, wisely, did not transcribe. Lael did not flinch, but the hand holding her slate went white around the edges.

Ren waited for the basin to answer.

It did not.

That was more frightening than a membrane strike.

Lael turned to Rusk. "You will not repeat that in the public feed."

"I have no interest in provoking the Parliament."

"You just defined Orison route stewardship as illegitimate unless it resembles human government."

"I defined operational reality."

"You defined convenience as ontology."

Ren looked at her.

Lael noticed. "What?"

"That one you can keep."

For half a second, Lael almost smiled. Then the route packet chimed again.

URGENT: CONTINUITY ASSURANCE TEMPLATE ATTACHED

The template opened across her screen.

Ren saw the first line and understood why Lael had gone quiet.

THE UNDERSIGNED RECOGNIZED REPRESENTATIVE OF THE ORISON SHOALS CONFIRMS THAT EXISTING TREATY OBLIGATIONS REMAIN STABLE, ENFORCEABLE, AND ADMINISTRATIVELY CONTINUOUS DURING CURRENT INTERNAL DELIBERATIONS.

There were lies that announced themselves by shouting. This one wore polished shoes.

"No," Ren said.

Lael said, "I know."

Rusk leaned closer. "It is a continuity assurance. Not a settlement."

"It requires a recognized representative," Ren said. "Third-Voice is not that."

"The Parliament summoned them as speaker."

"Speaker is not sovereign."

"Then select another signatory."

Lael closed the template with such care that Ren could feel the restraint in her fingers. "That sentence is how humans broke three early contact protocols and one Mars civic treaty."

"This is not a history seminar."

"It is always a history seminar," Ren said. "The only question is whether the dead get cited before or after we repeat them."

The basin lights shifted.

Third-Voice's blue-white fan turned toward the gallery. The speaker body did not approach. It did not need to. The membrane translated the attention with a pressure change that made every human in the gallery stand straighter.

Lael opened a clean slate.

"I can draft an interim statement," she said.

Ren heard the danger in can.

"What kind?"

"Narrow. Passage continuity for humanitarian and already-cleared cargo only. No representation that Parliament identity is stable. No waiver of deliberative change. No acknowledgment that a single body signs for the Shoals."

Rusk looked relieved.

That made Ren suspicious.

"Who signs?" Ren asked.

Lael did not answer.

There it was. The gap big enough to steer a convoy through.

Rusk said, "Advocate Shun can certify Compact understanding of current conditions."

"Compact understanding does not bind Orison relation," Ren said.

"It may be enough for the route office."

"Enough for them to keep cargo moving while pretending treaty continuity exists."

"Cargo moving is the point."

"No," Ren said. "Cargo moving is a point."

Rusk looked at the basin, then back at Ren. "You can afford philosophical distinction because you are not waiting on the far side with a failing lung."

Ren hated him for being useful again.

Lael's voice cut through before Ren answered badly. "The convoy manifest."

Rusk hesitated.

"You invoked patients," Lael said. "Show the manifest."

"Private route data includes commercial material."

"Redact pricing. Show cargo class, medical priority, passenger risk, and perishability."

"That will take authorization."

Lael's expression became very calm. "You are authorized to pressure an alien parliament inside an active injury event but not authorized to prove the pressure has moral content?"

Rusk stared at her.

Then he keyed his wrist slate.

The manifest appeared on the secondary terminal in blocks of color.

Ren forced themselves to read it.

Twenty-seven medical cases. Five pediatric. Three transplant cultures with tolerance windows under eight hours. Replacement membrane kits for Tallow Reef Station. Brine-safe antibiotics bound for a mixed human-Orison clinic still using Pelagos-era sterilizers. Eighty-two passengers, including two Orison diplomatic bodies in suspended transit. Perishable algae stock for a famine-ring habitat that had lost a greenhouse to mold. Then commercial loads: luxury shellwork, routeholder instruments, private data escrow, mineral assays, sealed cultural goods with no declared humanitarian value.

Not pure.

Nothing was.

Lael copied the manifest into a separated evidence pane and marked it:

HUMAN NEED ASSERTED / COMMERCIAL INTEREST PRESENT / DO NOT COLLAPSE.

Ren saw the phrase and understood she had borrowed it from their witness slate.

"Good," Ren said quietly.

Lael did not look up. "Do not praise me. It makes me defensive."

The basin finally answered.

An Orison body detached from the lower amber terraces and rose toward the membrane. It was narrow, almost blade-shaped, with a shell ridge along one side and a soft underside trailing copper filaments. Ren did not know the body personally, but the faction color was clear enough: withdrawal, salt-hardened, suspicious of every human urgency that arrived with a clock attached.

The terminal searched for a name.

SALT-BEFORE-SHELL / PARTIAL PRESENCE / NONSPEAKER FACTION CURRENT

The body did not touch the membrane. It angled itself so its shell side faced Rusk and its soft underside faced the cracked exchange vessel.

The speaker system translated in a dry, scraping tone very different from Third-Voice.

"Human need has learned to travel with human appetite."

Rusk said nothing.

That was the first wise thing he had done all hour.

Lael bowed her head slightly. Human gesture. Imperfect. Still better than standing like a claim.

"The manifest contains both," she said.

Salt-Before-Shell's copper filaments curled. "Then why does deadline wear the sick child's face and not the luxury shell?"

Lael looked at the manifest. "Because human institutions know which face opens doors."

Ren felt the gallery change around that answer.

It was not enough. But it did not lie.

The amber body rotated toward Lael. "Compact advocate brings deadlines. Does Compact advocate also bring refusal for appetite?"

Lael's slate hovered before her, blank where a clean assurance wanted to be.

"I can bring a narrower passage statement," she said. "Humanitarian cargo only. Medical and station-safety priority. Commercial cargo delayed. No claim that the Parliament has resolved treaty continuity. No claim that Orison identity is stable. No Orison signature required."

Rusk made a sharp sound. "That will strand half the convoy."

"Half the convoy is not dying," Lael said.

"The commercial cargo subsidizes the medical passage."

"Then your system built a sick child into a tollgate."

Rusk stepped toward her. "Do you think I designed this?"

"I think you benefit from arriving with no clean hands and demanding a clean signature."

Ren expected Rusk to retreat into polish.

He surprised them.

"My nephew is on that convoy."

The gallery stilled again, this time in a smaller, more human way.

Rusk looked furious at himself for saying it. Good. Maybe shame still had some live edges.

"Pulmonary graft," he said. "Orison-grown tissue. Six-hour window if the cold chain stays perfect. I did not put his name in the pressure packet because I know what that looks like."

Ren believed him.

Ren also believed he would use any other child just as hard if his nephew were safe.

Lael's face softened by exactly nothing. That was mercy. Pity would have made the room worse.

"Then you understand the cost of false passage," she said. "If we force a continuity assurance the Parliament cannot honestly carry, it may hold for this window and break for the next ten."

Rusk looked through the membrane at the cracked vessel. "And if we wait for perfect alien honesty?"

Salt-Before-Shell answered.

"No perfect. Only less devouring."

Ren wrote that down.

The Compact template remained closed. Lael opened a new document.

INTERIM HUMANITARIAN PASSAGE NOTICE / SHOALS DELIBERATION PERIOD

She paused with the cursor after the title.

"Ren," she said.

"No."

"I have not asked."
