"You want language that says the Parliament has not refused obligation while also saying it has not consented to administrative continuity."

"Yes."

"No."

"Why?"

"Because if I write it, the Compact will call it interpretation. If you write it, they will call it advocacy. Advocacy is easier to challenge."

Lael stared at them for two seconds.

Then she nodded once. "Fair."

She did not write immediately.

That restraint cost her. Ren saw it in the tendons of her hand, in the way her thumb hovered above the slate as if the right sentence were a hot surface she could touch only once. Compact advocates were trained to produce language under pressure. Delay looked like weakness in every office that had sent her here. Delay also happened to be the only honest material in the room.

"Before you draft," Ren said, "make him enter the data."

Rusk's head turned. "We have covered this."

"No," Ren said. "We have circled it. Container-level urgency, clinic destination, decay window, and subsidy dependency. If the medical cargo is being used to carry commercial cargo through an unstable treaty window, the notice needs to know that."

"The notice needs to move ships."

"The notice needs to know what kind of ship it is moving."

Lael set the blank document aside and opened a witness intake form. Not the Compact template. A rougher instrument, meant for emergency facts that might later embarrass everyone. Ren had seen versions of it in disaster courts. It had fewer beautiful clauses and more boxes that did not let a person hide behind category names.

"Envoy Rusk," Lael said, "provide convoy composition."

Rusk looked at the membrane, then at the lock where Tamsin had been taken out, then at the terminal rail he had stopped touching after Third-Voice's stress event. "If I enter private cargo data into a shared witness record, I breach consortium confidentiality."

"If you do not," Lael said, "your medical urgency claim remains rhetorical."

"That is a lawyer's trap."

"Yes. I am a lawyer."

Vela-Short laughed once over the pilot channel. No one asked her to stop.

Rusk drew himself up. For a moment Ren thought he would refuse and let the room see the exact size of his moral claim. Then his face changed. Not softened. Calculated differently. He opened a private slate, unlocked it with two biometric prompts, and sent a packet to Lael's console.

Lael did not accept it into the main record.

"Read it aloud," she said.

Rusk froze.

Ren understood why before he did. Reading made the numbers enter the chamber as voice, not just data. Voice could be challenged by bodies. Voice could not pretend it had remained neutral after becoming leverage.

"Six medical priority containers," Rusk said.

The basin remained still.

"Container M-11. Pulmonary graft tissue, Orison-grown, High Shoal Pediatric Clinic. Cold-chain failure risk begins in five hours and forty-two minutes. Irreversible viability loss projected at seven hours, depending on transfer delay."

His voice changed on pediatric. Only slightly.

"M-12. Reef-compatible broad-spectrum antibiotic culture, three station clinics, mixed adult and juvenile population. Useful life under current refrigeration: eleven hours. M-13. Dialysis membrane kits for Kettlemouth Habitat. Twenty-three patients on rationed rotation. Delay tolerance: nineteen hours before triage alteration. M-14. Anti-coagulant algae batch, surgical use, High Shoal and Low Shoal. M-15. Replacement wet-dry membrane seals for two clinics and one ferry station. M-16. Emergency nutrition cultures for an immuno-compromised school dormitory."

Ren wrote only what was necessary. Not the child's implied face. Not Rusk's nephew. The record needed need, not a hostage portrait.

Lael said, "Commercial composition."

Rusk's mouth hardened again.

"Fourteen commercial containers."

"Priority relation to medical cargo?"

"Shared propulsion, shared insurance, shared corridor booking."

"That was not my question."

He exhaled. "No medical dependency except cost distribution."

"Meaning the medical cargo can physically travel without the commercial cargo."

"At higher cost and with insurance exposure."

"Meaning yes."

"Meaning yes, if you want the clean answer that ignores why the route exists."

Lael looked at Ren for the first time since the intake began. "Do not enjoy this."

"I am not."

"Good."

She turned back to Rusk. "List commercial categories."

"Specialty polymers. Luxury reef-safe pigment. Two containers of private archive equipment. One container of ceremonial glassware. Three containers of brine-tolerant processor housings. Food additives. Sealed personal cargo. Contracted scientific instruments. Replacement entertainment rigs for transit stations."

Salt-Before-Shell's shell ridge lifted slowly.

"Entertainment rigs," the Orison speaker said.

The translation came through dry and exact. Even the machine sounded offended.

Rusk closed his eyes. "Transit crews are people. Morale infrastructure is not frivolous on long routes."

"True," Ren said before the basin could turn the phrase into an easier weapon. "Also not pediatric graft tissue."

Rusk opened his eyes and looked at them with something close to hatred. Ren accepted it. Hatred was sometimes what happened when a person could not deny a distinction and could not bear its cost.

Third-Voice's blue-white fan brightened toward the medical list. Amber brightened toward the commercial list. Green-black pulsed once, a boundary rhythm under both.

Lael copied the intake summary into the top of the blank notice, not as attachment, as foundation. Then she added a line before the formal language:

This notice distinguishes emergency need from bundled commercial advantage for the duration of the current salt-change deliberation.

Rusk read it and gave a short, humorless laugh. "You have just made every private carrier in the Meridian more expensive."

"No," Lael said. "I have made one hidden expense visible."

"You think visibility lowers cost?"

"Almost never."

That answer, because it was honest, entered the room differently.

Salt-Before-Shell shifted in the water. "Human writes price has body."

"Human writes price has body," Ren repeated for the slate.

Lael did not look up. "Human also writes body shall not be used as blanket for price."

The amber fan flared. Not approval. Marking.

Rusk rubbed both hands over his face, destroying for one second the careful routeholder presentation. When his hands dropped, he looked older and more dangerous.

"If my nephew dies because this distinction slows transfer," he said, "I will spend the rest of my life making sure your name remains attached to the delay."

Lael met him without flinching. "If your nephew passes because we force a false Orison signer and collapse Shoals passage later, I will attach my own name to that too."

The quiet after that had weight.

Ren understood then that Lael was not being brave in the cheap sense. She was choosing the shape of the accusation that would follow her. The Compact would not thank her for saving it from cleaner harm. Rusk would not forgive her for separating his true fear from his useful cargo. The Parliament might still decide the notice was another human theft dressed as restraint.

There was no clean place to stand.

Lael began drafting anyway.

She wrote:

The Compact advocate of record observes that the Salt Parliament remains in active deliberative relation with existing treaty obligations. No stable-party continuity assurance has been issued. No withdrawal notice has been issued. Humanitarian and station-safety passage may proceed under emergency witness conditions without prejudice to unresolved Orison political identity, dissent, or future treaty interpretation. Commercial cargo lacking medical, safety, or subsistence priority is not covered by this notice.

Rusk read it and looked as if someone had offered him a rope made of law and nettles.

"Route office will hate this."

"Yes," Lael said.

"Insurers will challenge it."

"Yes."

"Commercial sponsors will accuse the Compact of selective seizure."

"They may file in the appropriate forum."

"And my nephew?"

For the first time, Lael did not answer quickly.

She looked at Salt-Before-Shell. Then Third-Voice. Then the cracked vessel, still held inside the repair chamber because release had not become honest merely because humans needed the room to move on.

"Your nephew's passage is covered," Lael said. "Not because he is yours. Because medical need is real even when carried by compromised systems."

Rusk closed his eyes.

Salt-Before-Shell's shell ridge lowered by a fraction.

Third-Voice's blue-white fan brightened. Amber did not dim. Green-black remained slow and wary.

Lael signed the notice herself.

Not for the Orison.

Not as witness.

As the human who had brought a deadline and refused, narrowly and too late but not uselessly, to let the deadline become a false signer.

The notice transmitted.

Five seconds passed.

Ten.

The route office reply arrived in all caps, which made it legally meaningless and emotionally clear.

Rusk read it and laughed once under his breath. "They accepted provisional medical passage."

Lael exhaled.

Ren did not let themselves relax.

The second line of the route office reply proved them right.

COMMERCIAL CARGO STATUS: DEFERRED PENDING OPERATIONAL REVIEW.

Lael's exhale stopped.

"Deferred is a mouth," Ren said.

Rusk's eyes moved over the text. "It means not covered."

"It means waiting for a way to become covered."

Lael was already reopening the notice. "Addendum."

Rusk made a tired sound. "You got medical passage. Take the win."

"This is not a win," Lael said. "It is a narrow door with hands reaching around it."

She wrote:

ADDENDUM: DEFERRED COMMERCIAL CARGO MAY NOT BE RECLASSIFIED THROUGH OPERATIONAL REVIEW, COST-RECOVERY CLAIM, CONVOY INTEGRITY CLAIM, INSURANCE REQUIREMENT, ROUTE EFFICIENCY, OR HUMANITARIAN ATTACHMENT WITHOUT NEW WITNESS REVIEW.

Ren watched Salt-Before-Shell's shell ridge lift again, less in anger than recognition.

"Add entertainment rigs," Vela-Short said over the channel.

Lael did, with a lawyer's precision:

MORALE, COMFORT, REPUTATIONAL, DATA, CEREMONIAL, RESEARCH, AND COMMERCIAL INFRASTRUCTURE CARGO REMAIN OUTSIDE THIS NOTICE UNLESS DIRECT BODY-SAFETY DEPENDENCY IS SHOWN.

Rusk looked as if he wanted to object and could not find a clean enough sentence.

Good.

Lael transmitted the addendum before the route office could build a workaround out of silence.

Beyond the membrane, the cracked exchange vessel pulsed again.

The repair chamber widened.

The two trapped filaments remained inside.

The vote still would not release.

Chapter 5: The Vote That Would Not Release

At first the humans tried to name it a malfunction.

They did it in small ways, which were the most dangerous. The record clerk tagged the cracked vessel as exchange anomaly. The station AI suggested procedural variance. Aven Rusk asked whether the vessel could be "reset." Even Lael, careful Lael, opened a diagnostic pane before she caught herself and closed it with a look of contained shame.

Ren did not blame them.

Malfunction was a comfortable word. It made the body into equipment and the injury into repair. It promised that somebody could find the loose hinge, the fouled line, the bad clause, the hidden contaminant, and return the process to its intended shape.

The cracked vessel pulsed inside the repair chamber, two filaments still threaded through its soft center.

Nothing about it wanted to return.

"Do not call it failed," Ren said.

The record clerk froze.

Ren heard their own voice and wished they had made it gentler. The clerk was not the enemy. She was young, frightened, and trained by systems that gave her dropdown menus when the world bled.

"Sorry," Ren said. "Mark it as unresolved exchange body. No failure classification."

The clerk looked to Lael.

Lael said, "Use Interpreter Okoye's wording."

"With attribution?"

"Yes," Ren said.

Lael glanced at them.

Ren shrugged. "If the wording is wrong, I should be findable."

"That is a costly ethic."

"So is hiding inside passive voice."

Beyond the membrane, green-white repair sheets continued to widen the chamber around the vessel. They formed a slow spiral, not touching the cracked side directly. The blue-white filament remained extended through the vessel's upper pore. The amber filament had wrapped around an inner ridge and would not withdraw. Both carried color. Both carried pressure. Neither looked trapped in the simple physical sense.

"Can they leave?" Rusk asked.

Ren looked at Third-Voice.

The speaker body had moved closer again, but not to the membrane. It hovered above the repair chamber, three fans dimmed to low bands. Salt-Before-Shell remained near the amber terraces, shell side outward. Other Orison bodies watched from every level of the basin, not passive, not intervening, each maintaining distance as if distance itself had become part of the treatment.

Third-Voice answered after a delay.

"Leaving is available."

Rusk's face tightened. "Then why don't they?"

The amber fan gave a small, hard pulse.

"Because available is not innocent."

Ren wrote it down.

Rusk saw. "Does every phrase become scripture with you?"

"No. Only the dangerous ones."

Lael stepped beside Ren at the membrane. "Third-Voice, is either faction compelling the vessel to hold?"

"No."

"Is the vessel compelling the factions?"

The basin brightened in response. Ren felt the question pass through the water and come back altered.

Third-Voice said, "Wrong grammar."

Lael accepted that with a nod. "Give me better grammar."

The green-black fan unfolded slightly.

"The body has become relation that neither entrant can leave unchanged without making the leaving carry a lie."

The clerk typed slowly.

Rusk rubbed both hands over his face. "I need someone to explain that as if it must survive a hostile committee."

"It means the exchange worked too well," Ren said.

Everyone turned toward them.

Ren kept their eyes on the vessel. "The blue-white position entered to carry amber's withdrawal burden. Amber entered to carry blue-white's treaty burden. The vote protocol expected them to borrow, answer, and release. But the salt-change tide arrived early. The vessel changed while they were inside. Now each has received enough of the other position that withdrawing as if nothing changed would be false."

Rusk said, "So they agree?"

"No."

"Then what?"

"They disagree with consequences."

Lael's mouth moved. "That is useful."

"It is not enough."

"Useful rarely is."

The cracked vessel pulsed again. This time the repair chamber translated the pulse into visible bands on the membrane. Blue-white obligation flowed into amber fear and stopped there, held, not absorbed. Amber withdrawal flowed toward blue-white relation and recoiled before fully entering. Between them, in the crack, a narrow dark warmth persisted.

The station AI produced:

MINORITY POSITION RETAINED IN SHARED BODY

Then, perhaps learning from the last hour:

CLASSIFICATION PROVISIONAL

"Better," Ren said.

"Do not praise machines," Vela-Short said over the channel. "They develop expectations."

The pilot's dry voice steadied the room more than it should have. Ren wondered whether that was why Third-Voice kept answering her jokes.

A new Orison body approached from the lower terraces.

It was pale and compact, with a lattice of tiny holes along its surface. It moved without the deliberate spectacle of the speaker bodies. A maintenance lineage, Ren guessed, or a body grown for close structural work. It carried no bright faction color. Instead it held faint traces of many positions, as if it had been near too many arguments and refused to wash them off.

The terminal named it:

VESSEL-KIN / REPAIR FUNCTION / NONVOTING

The body touched the outer edge of the repair chamber.

Third-Voice's blue-white fan opened. "Vessel-kin speaks through function."

The speakers changed register. The new voice was porous and soft, with gaps the translator left as gentle clicks.

"The vote body was grown for brief burden."

Ren moved closer.

"It received long burden," Vessel-kin said. "It cannot return to brief."

Lael asked, "Can it survive as long burden?"

The maintenance body rotated. Its holes opened and closed, tasting the current.

"Survive is not one question."

Ren felt that one in their teeth.

Vessel-kin continued. "Tissue can continue. Exchange can continue. Pain can continue. Political use can continue. These are not the same mercy."

Lael's eyes flickered. "Understood."

Rusk looked as if he did not understand and knew better than to say so.

The clerk whispered, "Should I record that?"

"Yes," Ren, Lael, and Rusk said at once.

For one absurd second, the human gallery had consensus.

Outside, the Orison did not.

An amber cluster near Salt-Before-Shell opened into a jagged fan. The chamber translated without assigning a single speaker:

RELEASE AMBER BURDEN BEFORE HUMAN URGENCY MAKES IT HOSTAGE.

Blue-white bodies answered from the opposite terrace:

RELEASE BLUE BURDEN BEFORE WITHDRAWAL MAKES RELATION DENIABLE.

The repair sheets rippled.

The cracked vessel darkened.

Third-Voice lowered all three fans.

"Parliament will not pull," the speaker said.

Salt-Before-Shell turned toward Third-Voice. The withdrawal body's shell ridge lifted.

"Parliament pulled when it made bodies for votes," Salt-Before-Shell said.

The basin's temperature seemed to drop.

Ren did not move.

There were moments when an interpreter's body wanted to do something helpful because stillness felt like complicity. Ren had been trained to distrust that impulse. The Orison were not waiting for a human to moderate. They were letting a wound accuse its architecture.

Third-Voice's amber fan brightened in answer to Salt-Before-Shell. That was important. The same body that held treaty obligation also held the withdrawal accusation against the Parliament itself.

"True enough to remain," Third-Voice said.

The phrase from earlier returned, altered by placement.

Salt-Before-Shell's copper filaments curled tight. "Then keep it."

"We do."

"No," Salt-Before-Shell said. "You keep the part that lets the process continue. Keep the part that says the process made the harm."

The cracked vessel pulsed so strongly that the membrane shook.

Ren's cuff woke from reduced mode just long enough to display:

CONSENT HISTORY QUERY

Then it shut the line again.

Ren looked at Lael. "Consent history."

Lael was already searching. "For the vessel?"

"For the body design. For the protocol. For delegates entering brief burden during salt-change."

The clerk said, "Orison internal archives require Parliament permission."

"Ask," Ren said.

Lael hesitated.

Ren understood why. Human asking could become pressure. Human not asking could preserve ignorance that benefited everyone with a deadline.

Lael opened a request pane and dictated carefully.

"Compact advocate requests consent-history disclosure for the exchange vessel class involved in active injury. Request is for witness context, not jurisdictional claim. Refusal will be recorded without adverse inference."

Ren nodded.

Lael added, after a breath, "Commercial and route urgency shall not be used as grounds to override refusal."

Salt-Before-Shell turned slightly toward her.

Not approval. Not forgiveness.

Not nothing.

The basin took the request.

For thirty seconds, only the salt-change tide moved.

Then the vessel-kin arrived.

Ren knew the term from diagrams and hated how harmless it had looked there. Vessel-kin had been rendered in training files as support organisms, ancillary bodies, maintenance relations. The words suggested a backstage crew, useful and replaceable, arriving with tools after important actors had made decisions worth recording.

The bodies that rose from the lower basin did not look ancillary.

They came in a slow ring of translucent folds, each one shaped differently around a dark, flexible core. Some were no longer than Ren's arm. Others were broad as emergency doors. None carried the bright faction colors of voting lineages. Their light was muted: pearl, ash, old milk, the faint green of deep vents. They moved without hurry and without deference, and the Parliament made room for them in a way no human official had yet been granted.

The clerk's terminal, eager for betrayal, suggested:

MAINTENANCE RESPONSE TEAM

Ren reached for the display.

The clerk beat them to it.

She deleted the label and typed:

VESSEL-KIN ARRIVE / STATUS NOT YET TRANSLATED

Ren looked at her.

She did not look back. Her hands were still shaking from earlier exposure, but they stayed on the keys.

Good, Ren thought, and did not say it. Praise from a senior interpreter could become another pressure on a junior clerk trying to learn how not to obey a bad menu.

The vessel-kin formed a loose circle around the repair sheets. One small body slipped through a gap and approached the cracked vessel's dark seam. It did not touch the wound. It opened itself beside the crack, exposing a lattice of inner tissue that pulsed in sympathy with the trapped filaments. The blue-white filament flared. The amber filament tightened.

"What is it doing?" Rusk asked.

Ren waited. No one in the water answered.

Lael said, "Do not make Okoye invent certainty."

"I am asking for situational awareness."

"So am I."

Third-Voice's green-black fan gave a low pulse.

The speaker system translated after a delay:

"Kin makes nearby body."

Rusk stared. "That is not an explanation."

"It may be the only honest one," Ren said.

The small vessel-kin continued to pulse beside the cracked seam. Its lattice did not copy the injured vessel. It offered a parallel rhythm, close enough for pressure to move, different enough that the injured body did not have to become it. Two more kin joined, one near the blue-white pore and one near the amber ridge. Together they made a kind of temporary anatomy outside the damaged body.

Ren felt the shape of the act before they understood any of it.

Not repair.

Witnessed load-bearing.

Lael saw enough to change her posture. "They are distributing stress."

Third-Voice said, "Better grammar."

Lael's eyes narrowed. She did not like being corrected, but she liked useful correction more than pride. "They are helping the vessel carry what has not yet become releasable."

The blue-white fan brightened.

The amber fan did not object.

The clerk entered Lael's phrasing with attribution.

Rusk watched the vessel-kin with an expression Ren could not place. It was not disgust. Not fear. Something closer to recognition and resentment.

"Private carriers do that," he said.

Ren turned.

Rusk kept looking through the membrane. "Distribute load. Carry risk for systems that cannot afford to break. Everyone calls us parasites until a corridor needs someone with spare capacity."

Lael made a sound that was almost a warning.

Rusk lifted a hand. "I am not comparing us to them. I am saying I understand the argument people will make when this enters the public record. They will say if Orison vessel-kin can carry a broken vote body, private routeholders can carry a broken treaty window. They will say intervention is compassion."

Ren hated that he was right.

The room acquired a new danger. Not the old one, where humans misunderstood because they lacked the right category. This one was worse: humans might understand just enough to steal the shape.

Ren opened a fresh note.

DO NOT ANALOGIZE VESSEL-KIN CARE TO ROUTEHOLDER AUTHORITY.

They stopped, because the sentence was too late even as they wrote it. Any idea that needed that warning had already become portable.

"We need a boundary around this record," Ren said.

Lael looked up. "On what basis?"

"Care practice observed under active injury. Misuse risk high."

"That is not a legal basis."

"Then make one."

Lael almost smiled without humor. "You have a generous view of my powers."

"No. I have a precise view of the mess you helped bring."

That landed. It was unfair in the way a useful sentence sometimes was: not wholly accurate, not wholly avoidable.

Lael opened a restricted-publication flag and left it visibly unfinished.

"If I restrict the record completely," she said, "routeholders will claim we hid stabilizing evidence."

Rusk said nothing.

"If I publish it without guardrail, they will use it as stabilizing evidence."

"Yes," Ren said.

Lael typed:

PUBLIC RECORD GUARDRAIL: VESSEL-KIN CARE MAY BE CITED ONLY AS CARE UNDER CONSENT-LIMITED INJURY CONDITIONS. IT MAY NOT BE CITED AS PRECEDENT FOR PRIVATE ROUTE STABILIZATION, EMERGENCY CORRIDOR AUTHORITY, THIRD-PARTY CONTROL, OR COMMERCIAL RISK ABSORPTION.

The line looked too legal and not legal enough.

Ren added beneath it:

IF A HUMAN SYSTEM USES THIS CARE PRACTICE TO JUSTIFY BYPASS, THE CITATION ITSELF BECOMES EVIDENCE OF MISUSE.

Rusk's face changed.

"That will make legal departments avoid the citation," he said.

"Good," Lael said.

"It may also make them avoid the warning."

Salt-Before-Shell answered from the amber terrace. "Then they were seeking tool, not warning."

The vessel-kin ring continued its work. None of the care bodies reacted to being protected in human text. That was probably wise. Human text had not earned their attention.

Salt-Before-Shell moved along the amber terrace, shell ridge angled toward the human gallery.

"Human sees kin," the withdrawal speaker said. "Human will make kin into permit."

Rusk said, "Not all humans."

Salt-Before-Shell's shell ridge opened a fraction, revealing a darker inner surface. "Enough humans."

No one answered quickly.

The vessel-kin closest to the blue-white filament shuddered. The filament had sent a pulse through it, not forceful, not gentle. The kin absorbed the pressure and answered with a slower wave. The amber-side kin did the same. The third kin near the wound widened itself until the dark brine leaking from the crack spread thinly across its lattice and stopped curling into the salt-change tide.

The injured vessel's pulse steadied.

The terminal suggested:

STABILIZATION SUCCESSFUL

The clerk whispered, "No."

Ren did not know whether she meant no to the terminal, no to the room, or no to the human relief trying to rise in her own body.

She typed:

PRESSURE DISTRIBUTION IN PROGRESS / RELEASE NOT ACHIEVED

Lael said, "Good."

This time the praise was necessary. The clerk swallowed and kept working.

Rusk leaned closer to the membrane despite the yellow line. "If the vessel is stable enough, can the vote resume?"

Every Orison body near the repair chamber shifted.

Rusk realized it too late.

"I mean," he said, and stopped.

Ren let him sit in the stop. The stop did more than any correction could have.

Third-Voice's amber fan spoke.

"Human asks whether body can work while still wounded."

Rusk's jaw flexed.

Lael closed her eyes for one second.

Ren wrote the sentence and marked it central.

The blue-white fan added, "Sometimes yes."

The green-black fan added, "Cost must remain named."

The three statements entered the room separately. The terminal did not collapse them. The clerk had disabled that function.

Ren wondered whether that small act would survive the day.

The vessel-kin ring tightened, not around the injured body, around the space in which humans might make it useful too quickly. Ren had the irrational impression that the kin could feel the gallery's hunger for a resolution and were shielding the cracked vessel from interpretation as much as from salt-change pressure.

They wrote:

CARE IS NOT PROOF OF READINESS.

Then:

STABILITY IS NOT RELEASE.

Then:

WOUND MAY CONTINUE TO PARTICIPATE ONLY IF WOUND'S COST REMAINS PART OF RECORD.

Lael read over their shoulder and said quietly, "That last one is going to follow us."

"Yes."

"I wish you had written it less well."

"So do I."

Then old images bloomed across the membrane, not visual records but translated archive-shapes. Ren saw early Parliament bodies grown small and simple, made for exchanges between lineages that had known each other across thousands of tides. Brief burden had meant one current borrowing another's fear for the span of a decision about reef maintenance, migration timing, repair labor, mating restriction, memory pruning.

Then human treaty arrived.

The bodies grew larger.

Not immediately. Not by command. Need did it first. Orison factions needed to carry human consequences. Medical passage. Mining claims. Route schedules. Deadlines. Debt. Human law did not enter the vessels physically, but it changed the size of the burdens Orison asked them to hold.

Brief burden became treaty burden.

Treaty burden became route burden.

Route burden became civilization burden.

The vessel class had never been redesigned. It had only been asked to endure.

Ren felt sick.

Lael whispered, "We did not make them do that."

Ren said nothing.

Lael closed her eyes. "That was not a defense. I heard it after I said it."

"Good."

Rusk stared through the membrane. His anger had gone uncertain. "Are you saying human treaty pressure cracked their voting bodies?"

Ren watched the archival shapes fade around the active wound.

"I am saying the question is now admissible."

The cracked vessel did not release.

But the chamber around it changed.

Repair sheets stopped forming a spiral and began forming a second skin, not to close the crack, not yet, but to distribute pressure across a wider living surface. Vessel-kin entered the outer layer. Other nonvoting maintenance bodies joined from below, bringing porous tissue, salt buffers, slow currents, and old chemical patterns from before human treaties had taught the Parliament to carry clocks.

The blue-white and amber filaments remained inside.

Their colors did not blend.

The dark warmth between them widened by a fraction.

Third-Voice spoke to the whole basin now.

"Vote suspended. Body retained. Burden not false. Release not compelled."

The Parliament answered in hundreds of colors Ren could not read.

Lael said, "The formal vote is suspended?"

Third-Voice turned one fan toward her.

"The formal vote ended when the body refused the old ending."

Ren wrote that sentence slowly.

Rusk looked from Lael to Ren. "What happens next?"

No one answered quickly enough to comfort him.

That was the most honest answer available.

Chapter 6: No Word for Unanimous

Ren tried to translate comfort and failed before the first sentence.

The failure happened in private, which was a mercy. They had opened a side pane on the witness slate, searching old Orison relational registers for the term Compact law would expect next: consensus. Not decision, not vote result, not emergency pause. Consensus. A word that could travel through route offices, insurance pools, medical priority boards, diplomatic relays, and judicial archives without arriving visibly wounded.

The slate offered eight candidates.

None meant consensus.

One meant currents remain in contact without tearing the reef.

One meant disagreement held long enough to feed young.

One meant no body has been abandoned outside the tide.

One meant a wound accepted into maintenance.

One meant all speakers have changed shape and none have claimed victory.

The closest human gloss, from an old Pelagos file, was durable relation under known difference.

Ren hated it immediately because it was accurate and useless to bureaucrats.

Lael leaned over their shoulder. "Do not tell me there is no word."

"There are too many words."

"Worse."

"Much worse."

Aven Rusk, who had been pretending not to listen from four steps away, gave up the pretense. "The route office will ask whether the Parliament has consensus to suspend the vote."

"Then the route office will ask a bad question."

"Bad questions still bill by the hour."

Ren looked through the membrane.

The Parliament had rearranged itself around the cracked vessel. The formal voting terraces were no longer distinct. Blue-white treaty bodies, amber withdrawal bodies, green-white repair lineages, black-silver caution forms, and hundreds of smaller local currents had shifted into rings around the living chamber. No one had announced the formation. It had emerged the way weather emerged: not by command, but because every body responded to pressure and the responses found a pattern.

The vessel remained open.

The two filaments remained inside.

The dark warmth between them had not grown since the repair skin formed, but it had stopped leaking.

"What would humans call that?" Rusk asked.

"Stalemate," Lael said.

"Crisis," said the clerk.

"A blocked vote," said Rusk.

Ren watched an amber cluster send one filament toward the ring, stop before contact, then hold the stopped position visibly while three blue-white bodies adjusted their own distance to make the noncontact part of the pattern.

"Manners," Vela-Short said over the channel.

Ren laughed before they could stop themselves.

Rusk turned toward the capsule lock. "Pilot, are you contributing officially?"

"Never."

"Good."

"But I have seen docking crews do more politics with elbows than committees do with charters."

Third-Voice's blue-white fan brightened from the central current.

The speaker said, "Pilot who does not interpret interprets by refusing title."

Vela-Short replied, "I charge triple for philosophy."

The basin shifted again, and Ren saw the lesson hiding under the absurdity. Vela-Short had not entered the record as interpreter. She had not claimed authority. But her comment had affected the room. Refusal of title did not mean absence of relation. It meant the relation stayed honest about its limits.

Ren wrote:

CONSENSUS IS NOT ABSENCE OF DISSENT.

Then stopped. Too clean. Too human.

They crossed it out.

Lael watched. "What is wrong?"

"It makes dissent sound like noise tolerated by a central agreement."

"And Orison?"

"Dissent is one of the bodies that keeps the relation from lying."

Lael absorbed that, then looked annoyed. "That is going to ruin three treaty forms."

"Only three?"

"Today."

Third-Voice approached the membrane, but not alone. A small group came with them: Salt-Before-Shell on one side, Vessel-kin beneath, two blue-white treaty bodies, a green-white repair sheet folded around itself like a living bandage, and a dark, slow body the terminal refused to classify beyond OBSERVER / DEEP CURRENT / DO NOT PROMPT.

The human gallery made the subtle collective adjustment of primates who had realized the larger room had decided to include them.

Third-Voice touched the membrane with three cilia.

"Ren Okoye searches for the word humans can carry away."

"Yes."

"This is how humans steal less violently."

Ren let that stand. "Sometimes."

Salt-Before-Shell turned shell-side toward them. "Sometimes humans steal with careful pockets."

"Yes."

Rusk said, "We are not here to steal a word."

No one answered him.

After a moment he looked at Lael. "Are we?"

Lael's face did something complicated. "We are here because human systems need portable language. Portable language often becomes theft when it stops showing what was cut off to make it portable."

"That is not comforting."

"No."

The deep-current observer opened one surface.

Ren felt the movement as much as saw it. The basin's sound dropped below hearing. The salt-change tide slowed around the observer body, or seemed to. White particles hung in the water like suspended thought.

The terminal displayed:

ARCHAIC RELATIONAL REGISTER DETECTED

TRANSLATION CONFIDENCE VERY LOW

Then it wisely went blank.

The observer spoke without the speakers at first. It sent a pressure through the membrane that touched Ren's sternum. Their cuff refused to gloss it. The meaning arrived as arrangement, not language: reef pores open at different sizes so water can move; a chorus holds multiple notes because a single note cannot test the chamber; a wound left outside the body rots; a wound swallowed whole poisons; relation is the work of keeping the wound where care can reach it without making it the whole body.

Ren gripped the slate.

The speakers finally produced a sentence:

"Same-water-with-different-salt."

Lael whispered, "Is that the word?"

Ren shook their head. "No."

Third-Voice said, "It is one old door."

"To what?"

"To what humans call consensus when humans are not lying to themselves."

Ren wrote it down, then marked it unportable.

Rusk noticed. "Why?"

"Because if I put same-water-with-different-salt into a route notice, someone will turn it into a doctrine by dinner."

"Isn't doctrine the point of law?"

"Doctrine is what law becomes after it forgets who bled while it was learning."

Lael gave Ren a look.

"What?" Ren asked.

"I am deciding whether to steal that."

"With attribution."

"Obviously."

Salt-Before-Shell moved closer to the membrane. Its copper filaments trailed through the tide, each filament carrying flecks of white chemical memory.

"Humans want unanimous because unanimous lets dissent disappear without corpse," the withdrawal body said.

The sentence landed hard.

Rusk's face changed. "We use unanimous to mean everyone agrees."

"No," Salt-Before-Shell said. "You use unanimous to mean no one remains visible enough to block action."

Rusk opened his mouth.

Closed it.

Ren respected that more than any answer he might have made.

Lael said, "In human law, unanimity can protect against coercion."

Salt-Before-Shell rotated toward her. "Does it?"

Lael did not pretend the question was simple. "Sometimes. Badly. Incompletely. But yes, sometimes a requirement that everyone agree prevents a majority from crushing one holdout."

The amber body held still.

Third-Voice's blue-white fan dimmed in thought. Green-black moved slowly, warning not against Lael, but against haste.

"Then human unanimous is two tools," Third-Voice said. "One preserves refusal. One buries it."

"Yes," Lael said.

"Humans use same word."

"Yes."

"Careless."

"Yes."

Ren wrote:

HUMAN UNANIMITY CONTAINS TWO OPPOSITE FUNCTIONS: PROTECTION OF HOLDOUT CONSENT AND ERASURE OF VISIBLE DISSENT. DO NOT MAP DIRECTLY TO ORISON RELATIONAL CONSENSUS.

That, unfortunately, could survive a hostile committee.

The clerk's console disagreed.

It chimed with the patient cruelty of a form designed by people who would never stand in the room where it was used. A required field opened under the route office intake:

PARLIAMENTARY STATUS:

UNANIMOUS / MAJORITY / MINORITY OBJECTION / DEADLOCK / FAILED VOTE / OTHER

The cursor blinked inside the field.

The clerk did not touch it.

Rusk saw the menu and made a small, unwilling sound. "Other."

Ren shook their head. "Other becomes a drawer."

"A drawer is better than no filing cabinet."

"Not if everyone forgets to open it."

Lael moved to the console. "The intake cannot transmit without that field."

"Then do not transmit the intake."

"The medical passage notice references the intake."

"Then the notice is trying to eat the record."

Lael gave them a tired look. "That sentence is less useful than you think."

"It is exactly as useful as the form deserves."

The clerk lifted one hand, hesitated, and lowered it again. Her name, Ren remembered, was Tamsin; earlier the tide had made her taste the vote, and now a mandatory field was asking her to flatten the experience into a word that could travel without her. Her face had the gray cast of someone still under suppressant. The medical patch behind her ear pulsed faintly.

"What happens if you choose Other?" Ren asked her.

Tamsin looked startled to be addressed as a person rather than a terminal location. "It opens a free-text box."

"And downstream?"

She swallowed. "Downstream systems display it as exception pending review."

"Does exception pending review hold passage?"

Lael answered before Tamsin had to. "Usually."

Rusk's gaze sharpened. "Then Other delays the medical containers."

"Usually," Lael said again.

"So your accurate language kills urgency by being administratively invisible."

Ren hated that too. Every useful trap had at least one true wall.

Tamsin opened the downstream preview because no one asked her not to.

The route office interface did not look cruel. That made it worse. It had clean borders, calm gray panels, and the sort of blue priority icons human committees chose when they wanted urgency to feel responsible rather than afraid. The preview rendered the Parliament's living crisis into four dependent systems: PASSAGE CLEARANCE, LIABILITY, MEDICAL PRIORITY, and SOVEREIGN CONTACT.

UNANIMOUS turned all four panels green.

MAJORITY turned passage amber, liability amber, medical priority green, sovereign contact green.

MINORITY OBJECTION turned passage amber, liability red, medical priority green, sovereign contact amber.

DEADLOCK turned passage red, liability red, medical priority amber, sovereign contact red.

FAILED VOTE turned everything red except liability, which acquired a second red border and a warning triangle.

OTHER did not turn anything any color at first. It opened a blank white box with a character limit and a gray note underneath:

EXCEPTION TEXT WILL NOT BE MACHINE-ACTIONABLE UNTIL REVIEW.

The sentence was so small that a tired clerk could miss it. That was how systems lied politely. They placed the knife in eight-point type and called the wound procedure.

"Show the next screen," Ren said.

Tamsin glanced at Lael.

Lael nodded.

The preview advanced to a receiver summary. Ren felt the temperature in their hands drop. On the summary view, the field name did not say OTHER. It said NONSTANDARD POLITICAL STATUS. Beneath that, in smaller text, the system had generated a logistics tag:

MANUAL HOLD RECOMMENDED.

Rusk exhaled through his teeth. "That's what the harbor insurers see."

"Not only insurers," Tamsin said. "Port medical boards. Volunteer corridor coordinators. Any captain with a compliance overlay. The original text can be opened, but the first visible tag is the hold."

"And people obey first visible tags," Ren said.

Tamsin did not answer quickly. Her hands rested above the console as if she had been caught doing something private. "People obey the tags when they are tired, when they are afraid of audit, when they do not know the politics, when the queue is long, when the override name will be their name forever. So yes. People obey first visible tags."

Lael looked at her then, not at the form. "How many times have you seen that happen?"

The clerk's jaw worked once. "Enough to stop counting as a way of staying employed."

For a moment the human side of the membrane had its own current. Not sympathy exactly. Sympathy could become another way of making a clerk carry the institution's sin alone. It was recognition, harsher and more useful: the machine did not make its violence without hands, and the hands were often attached to people with rent, parents, children, debt, suppressant patches, and names that appeared in disciplinary logs more easily than in praise.

Ren wrote a new note:

DOWNSTREAM DISPLAY IS PART OF THE TRANSLATION.

Then another:

THE FIRST TAG TEACHES THE RECEIVER WHAT KIND OF ATTENTION IS ALLOWED.

The second note hurt more. It was too close to the archive problem, too close to every first sentence that had trained human readers to treat Orison speech as metaphor, every label that had turned a living relation into a resource dispute, every tidy category that had made later cruelty feel like compliance.

Third-Voice touched the membrane again.

"Human word travels before human thought?" the speaker asked.

Ren looked at the receiver summary. "Often."

"Then word is not small."

"No."

Salt-Before-Shell's copper filaments shivered. "A first tag is a tide gate."

The old image reached Ren with unpleasant precision. A tide gate did not need to hate the sea. It simply decided which water would arrive late, which water would stagnate, which larvae would fail to reach the inlet, which boats would learn to scrape bottom. The gate could be defended as infrastructure. It could be mapped, inspected, maintained, funded. It could be called neutral by people who lived upstream.

"Yes," Ren said. "A first tag is a tide gate."

Lael copied the phrase, stopped, and deleted it. "Not in the legal packet."

"Coward."

"Professional coward. The distinction matters."

Rusk was still staring at the preview. "If we choose deadlock, the commercial holds are honest."

Ren turned on him. "Deadlock says the medical passage is politically blocked."

"It is politically complicated."

"Complicated is not blocked."

"From the outside, unresolved looks like blocked."

"From the outside, water looks flat."

Rusk accepted the hit without flinching. "And if we choose unanimous, the containers move."

The basin's blue-white bodies brightened at the word. Amber bodies contracted. The cracked vessel gave a small pulse through the repair skin, less a sound than a pressure in the bones. Ren wondered whether the translation channel had carried the menu or whether the Parliament had learned human danger by watching human faces.

Lael said, very quietly, "If we choose unanimous, we teach every later office that this arrangement meant agreement."

"It would be one emergency notice."

"No," Tamsin said.

Everyone looked at her.

The clerk swallowed, but she did not take the word back. "It would be one authoritative emergency notice with a preserved machine tag. It would be copied into receiver sockets, loss models, route-risk training sets, and legal summaries. The text might say provisional. The tag would say unanimous. In two years some analyst would query all unanimous Orison notices and this would be in the pile. In five years someone would say precedent. In ten years someone would ask why the Orison objected to a category they had already accepted during the Salt Parliament."

Lael's expression changed with each sentence, not because the facts were new, but because the facts had arrived from the one person in the room whose daily work involved seeing small bad choices fossilize.

Tamsin kept going now that stopping would have been worse. "If we choose deadlock, the same thing happens in the other direction. This becomes a failed governance tag. The medical corridor becomes a favor granted despite alien instability. Every port official who already thinks the Orison cannot produce reliable public authority gets a clean example."

Rusk's voice lost some of its sponsor polish. "And Other?"

"Other becomes invisible until it becomes expensive. Then it becomes suspicious."

Vela-Short made a low sound over the channel. "That's a whole empire in one dropdown."

No one corrected her.

Ren looked through the membrane. The Parliament was still not listening the way humans listened. It did not pause as a single audience. It received by gradients, by pressure, by the repositioning of bodies that might not be bodies in any humanly useful sense. Still, something had shifted. A blue-white treaty form extended a filament toward amber and held it short of contact. Amber did not retreat. Green-white repair sheets thickened around the vessel's fracture, leaving a narrow visible gap where the two filaments remained separate inside.

The scene refused the menu in every direction.

Ren felt the oldest professional hunger: the desire to coin the right term, to make the hard thing portable without killing it. The hunger was not innocent. A beautiful term could be a theft with better clothes. A precise term could become a stamp. Even a warning could become a style guide for future harm.

"Maybe there should be no translated status," Ren said.

Lael gave a short laugh without humor. "There has to be a status."

"Why?"

"Because the receiving systems are built around one."

"Then receiving systems are not ready to receive."

"Correct," Lael said. "And the containers still need a corridor."

That was the true shape of the problem. Not ignorance versus knowledge. Not good language versus bad language. Movement versus memory. Medicine versus precedent. An injured Parliament and a shipping system that could not wait for philosophy because bodies in cold containers would not survive a seminar.

Rusk said, "We need the least false thing that moves the medicine and does not hand the insurers a doctrine."

Ren almost objected to we. Then did not. In this room, even Rusk's compromises had consequences worth using.

Lael turned to Tamsin. "Can the legal exception carry a visible top-line note into the receiver summary?"

"Only if you attach an emergency authority header. It replaces the machine tag on medical boards, but not in commercial systems."

"Show me."

Tamsin opened another pane. The cursor blinked in a field labeled AUTHORITY NOTE, eighty characters maximum.

Rusk stared. "Eighty?"

"Seventy-two after the required prefix," Tamsin said.

Ren laughed once, sharply. "Civilization."

Lael ignored them. "What is the required prefix?"

Tamsin displayed it:

LEGAL EXCEPTION:

The remaining space looked obscene.

"For medical receivers only?" Lael asked.

"Medical, humanitarian corridor, and sovereign contact duty officer. Insurers see noncompliant status with a link to the full packet."

"Good enough?"

Ren said, "No."

Lael said, "Possibly."

Rusk said, "Better than deadlock."

The three answers hung together, none canceling the others. That, Ren thought, was the human version of same-water-with-different-salt: not harmony, but a set of incompatible insufficiencies held long enough to choose the least damaging action.

Lael put both hands on the console edge. "We are not going to find a truthful word that fits in seventy-two characters."

"Then do not pretend it is truthful," Ren said. "Make it a pointer."

"To what?"

"To the full packet, the active condition, and the fact that classification fails."

Lael's eyes narrowed. "A status that says status unavailable."

"A receiver warning that says attention required before category."

Tamsin's fingers moved before anyone told her to type. She entered a draft:

STATUS CATEGORY INVALID; MEDICAL PASSAGE ACTIVE UNDER EXCEPTION

The system counted down the characters and accepted it with six to spare.

Rusk leaned close. "That will frighten captains."

"Good," Ren said.

"Frightened captains slow down."

Vela-Short cut in. "Frightened captains also read the next line. Confident captains skim themselves into lawsuits."

Lael looked at Tamsin. "Does the next line appear on medical boards?"

Tamsin previewed it. Beneath the authority note, the receiving board showed the humanitarian clock, container identifiers, the emergency physician sign-off, and a link:

FULL POLITICAL CONDITION REQUIRED BEFORE NONMEDICAL RELIANCE.

The phrase was ugly enough to be useful.

Ren nodded. "That is not a translation. It is a brake."

Third-Voice's fan brightened.

"Brake is relation?" the speaker asked.

"Sometimes a brake is what lets motion remain survivable."

Salt-Before-Shell turned half a shell. "Human machines require small wound before large wound is noticed."

Lael kept her eyes on the screen. "Yes."

"Then mark the small wound loudly."

Ren felt the sentence settle. Not as permission. The Orison did not grant them absolution. But it gave the human side a shape for the thing they could do: make the classification injury visible before it grew large enough to become doctrine.

Tamsin saved the authority note draft. The eighty-character field stopped blinking.

It looked pitifully small.

It also looked like a lever.

Third-Voice's fans remained still. Salt-Before-Shell did not rescue them with an alien sentence. The basin had already given the old door. Humans now had to decide whether they would make the door into a handle, a weapon, or an excuse.

Lael leaned closer to the form. "Can you override the field?"

Tamsin shook her head. "Not at my access level."

"Mine?"

"You can force transmit with legal exception, but it appends noncompliant status to the packet."

Rusk said, "Insurers will treat that as a red flag."

"Insurers treat breathing as a red flag if premiums need help," Vela-Short said from the lock.

No one laughed this time.

Ren looked at the menu again. UNANIMOUS. MAJORITY. MINORITY OBJECTION. DEADLOCK. FAILED VOTE. OTHER.

Each word was a small machine. Each machine had a route office behavior attached to it. The wrong word would not merely describe the Parliament badly. It would open or close doors, move medicine or strand it, enrich bypass brokers, harden prejudice, create future precedent, and teach every human system downstream what kind of alien politics counted as real.

"We need a status that is not a status," Ren said.

Lael rubbed both hands over her eyes. "That sounds like something that gets people removed from committees."

"Good."

"Okoye."

"The form asks what state the Parliament is in. The answer is not a state. It is an active relation."

"The form cannot hold active relation."

"Then attach active relation as a condition of passage, not a status."

Lael lowered her hands slowly.

Rusk said, "Explain in words I can hate accurately."

Ren pointed to the required field. "You force transmit under legal exception. You do not fill status as Other. You mark the status field noncompliant because the category set is materially misleading. Then the medical passage notice carries a condition: passage granted while Parliament remains in maintained relation under unresolved embodied dissent, with no withdrawal notice and no stable-party continuity assurance. The route office does not get to call that consensus. It gets to call it the condition under which medical passage is permitted."

Lael's eyes narrowed as she built the sentence in law.

"That will not satisfy the intake," Rusk said.

"No," Lael said. "It will satisfy the exception authority."

"Who reviews that?"

"Me first. Then someone angrier."

"And if they reverse you?"

"The containers will already be moving."

Rusk stared at her.

Ren did too.

Lael did not look heroic. She looked like a person calculating exactly how much of her career could be burned without becoming theater.

"You are using procedural delay against itself," Rusk said.

"I am using exception authority for an actual exception."

"Commercial sponsors will call it abuse."

"They may join the line."

Salt-Before-Shell's copper filaments lifted. "Human makes refusal visible inside machine?"

Lael looked to Ren.

Ren translated carefully, not from Orison to human, but from one human danger into another. "She is making the machine admit it cannot classify the refusal without damage."

The amber body held that.

Third-Voice's blue-white fan brightened because medical passage would move. Amber brightened because the passage would not pretend to own consensus. Green-black pulsed because the exception itself was a route around a simplified route, and routes around routes were never innocent.

Tamsin opened the legal exception field.

Her hands shook only once.

She typed Lael's dictated header:

CATEGORY SET MATERIALLY MISLEADING UNDER CURRENT ORISON PROCESS

Then, under Lael's instruction, she attached Ren's note, Lael's emergency notice, the convoy medical disclosure, and a restricted excerpt of the deep-current register marked:

NOT DOCTRINE / NOT STATUS / ACTIVE CONDITION ONLY

Rusk watched the packet assemble. "You realize every office that receives this will call asking what it means."

Lael said, "Good. They will have to ask before pretending they know."

The clerk looked up from the console. "Transmit?"

Lael glanced once through the membrane.

The cracked vessel remained held, not healed. The Parliament remained arranged, not agreed. The tide remained in the basin, carrying old memory through new categories that did not deserve it and had to be used anyway.

"Transmit," Lael said.

Tamsin sent it.

For seven seconds, nothing happened.

Then the route office system returned an error.

Rusk closed his eyes. "Of course."

Tamsin read the error and, unexpectedly, smiled.

"It failed upward," she said.

Lael leaned over the console.

The packet had not been rejected. It had exceeded automated classification authority and escalated to human emergency review while preserving the medical passage clock. A bad system had not become good. It had been forced, briefly, to stop pretending it was enough.

Ren wrote:

FORM REFUSED TO UNDERSTAND; EXCEPTION PRESERVED MOVEMENT.

Then they crossed out refused. The form had no will. People did.

FORM COULD NOT UNDERSTAND; PEOPLE MUST NOT HIDE BEHIND IT.

The basin gave a low pulse.

Not agreement. Recognition.

The deep-current observer withdrew into shadow, having given them one old door and no permission to build a house on it.

Lael opened a treaty note and began drafting:

For current emergency records, the Parliament's suspension of formal vote should not be described as unanimous, majority, deadlock, or failure. The observed Orison process is better described as maintained relation under unresolved, embodied dissent. Existing human legal categories are insufficient and should be treated as provisional convenience only.

Rusk read over her shoulder. "Route office will hate this one too."

"They are developing a theme," Lael said.

"Will it keep medical passage open?"

"Yes. Because it does not withdraw the humanitarian notice."

"Will it keep commercial passage delayed?"

"Yes."

Rusk rubbed the bridge of his nose. "My sponsors will hate this more than the route office."

"Good," Vela-Short said.

Rusk ignored her. "If commercial cargo remains delayed, private operators will start pricing bypass routes."

Ren looked at him. "There are no safe bypass routes through the Shoals."

"There are expensive ones."

Third-Voice's green-black fan darkened.

Oru-Kel had not yet arrived in person, but the Lumen warning seemed to move through the fan as if summoned by the idea of private shortcuts. Pale Arc rhythms. Route disturbance. Political simplification and spatial simplification sharing one old danger.

"Do not threaten a bypass in front of a cracked voting body," Ren said.

Rusk looked genuinely startled. "I am not threatening. I am telling you what markets do when uncertainty rises."

"Then markets are making a threat with your mouth."

That silenced him.

The cracked vessel pulsed again.

This pulse was weaker.

Vessel-kin moved inside the repair skin, porous body flattening against the outer layer. Green-white repair sheets thickened. Blue-white and amber filaments remained present, still separate, still unable or unwilling to leave.

Ren's cuff displayed a new provisional note:

RELATION MAINTAINED / STRESS LOWER / RESOLUTION ABSENT

For once, Ren did not turn it off.

"That," they said to Lael, "is probably the closest thing to today's answer."

Lael copied it into the emergency record with three qualifiers and one warning.

Rusk read the final line aloud:

"Resolution absent."

He looked at the basin, then at the route manifest, then at the cracked vessel.

"Humans hate paying for absence," he said.

Salt-Before-Shell answered through the speaker, copper filaments bright.

"Then humans should stop making absence carry what they refuse to see."

No one improved on that.

Ren saved the Chapter Six witness packet under a title no court would enjoy:

NO WORD FOR UNANIMOUS.

The slate accepted it.

Outside, the Parliament remained in relation.

Not agreed.

Not failed.

Not comfortable.

Still there.

Chapter 7: Salt-Before-Shell Withdraws a Limb

The protest began without a declaration.

That was the first reason the human systems missed it.

The second was that the protest looked, to human eyes, like injury.

Salt-Before-Shell had been holding position near the amber terraces, shell ridge angled toward the gallery, soft underside turned toward the cracked vote body. Since the early tide, the withdrawal faction's speaker had moved only by small degrees. A rotation. A filament curl. A hardening of shell along one edge when human language tried to make urgency into authority.

Then one of its limbs detached.

The clerk made a small sound.

Rusk stepped forward. "Is that supposed to happen?"

Lael did not answer. She had learned that supposed to was another dangerous human basin.

The limb was not a limb in any clean anatomical sense. It was a copper-filament branch grown from the soft underside, thick enough at the base to carry memory current, fine enough at the end to taste salt-change chemistry. It did not tear free. It narrowed, sealed, and separated in a slow deliberate motion. The severed branch drifted upward through the basin with pale fluid trailing behind it.

The station AI flashed:

POSSIBLE SELF-HARM EVENT

Ren reached for the terminal and stopped one centimeter short.

Do not erase too quickly.

The line was not theirs. It arrived from memory: Mira Cael's voice in a training archive, older, tired, and merciless toward helpful hands.

Ren let the warning remain for one breath.

Then they added:

CLASSIFICATION DISPUTED / DO NOT ASSUME PATHOLOGY

The clerk looked relieved to have words. "Should I alert medical?"

"Ask the basin," Ren said.

The clerk's fingers hovered. "How?"

Ren did not know.

That ignorance would have been humiliating in their first years. Now it was usable. "Lael?"

Lael opened a narrow channel and spoke with the care of someone carrying a full cup across uneven ground.

"Human gallery observes limb separation by Salt-Before-Shell. We request whether this is injury requiring assistance, political act requiring witness, ordinary body process, or another category. We request without claim to intervene."

Salt-Before-Shell rotated.

The detached limb drifted between the withdrawal terraces and the central repair chamber. No Orison body moved to retrieve it. That, more than the separation itself, made Ren's skin tighten.

Third-Voice answered from the central current.

"Political act. Body cost. Witness required. Assistance not requested."

The AI replaced self-harm event with:

POLITICAL BODY COST / WITNESS REQUIRED

Rusk read it twice. "Body cost."

"Yes," Ren said.

"That is a metaphor?"

Salt-Before-Shell's speaker tone scraped through the gallery.

"No."

Rusk shut his mouth.

The detached limb opened as it drifted. Its copper filaments spread into a thin fan, each strand carrying faint amber light. The salt-change tide caught the strands and pulled old images through them: treaty pools, human signatures, Pelagos reef scars, Compact recorders, route maps, medical convoys, mining pylons, witness slates, the cracked exchange vessel. The images were not displayed for humans. Humans happened to be there.

Ren felt the difference and marked it.

NOT PERFORMANCE FOR HUMAN BENEFIT.

Then, after a pause:

HUMANS INCLUDED IN CONSEQUENCE.

Lael read over their shoulder. "That distinction will matter."

"It already does."

The limb drifted toward the formal voting terraces. Bodies moved aside. Not far. Just enough to let it pass without absorbing it by accident. The amber faction did not gather around Salt-Before-Shell. That surprised Ren until they realized gathering might have softened the act into factional theater. The withdrawal body had made itself smaller alone.

Vela-Short's voice came from the lock, quieter than usual. "Does it grow back?"

Third-Voice answered her because, apparently, the pilot's refusal of title had become a kind of permission.

"Perhaps."

"How long?"

"If fed by relation, soon. If fed by grievance only, badly."

Vela-Short said nothing.

Rusk did.

"Why do it?"

Salt-Before-Shell turned shell-side toward him. Its missing branch left a pale sealed ridge along the underside. The absence was visible now that Ren knew where to look.

"Humans require visible loss before believing cost," Salt-Before-Shell said.

Rusk flinched.

"This is not for you," Salt-Before-Shell continued. "This is because you are here."

Ren wrote the sentence exactly and underlined because underlining in a private slate was less destructive than shouting.

Lael asked, "What does the withdrawal signify within Parliament process?"

"Ceremonial form broken," Third-Voice said.

The basin's lights dimmed around the phrase.

Lael waited.

Third-Voice's amber fan answered, not the blue-white, not the green-black.

"A faction in formal relation does not reduce its speaking body during active maintenance unless it refuses the chamber's claim to whole participation."

"Salt-Before-Shell is refusing to participate?"

"No."

Lael breathed through her nose. "Of course not."

Ren said, "They are refusing to let participation be counted as wholeness."

The amber fan brightened.

Salt-Before-Shell added, "A body may remain in relation while withdrawing what relation has made unsafe."

The detached limb reached the old voting platform where the formal vote should have concluded. It did not settle. It hovered above the platform, slowly turning in the current.

The station AI asked:

SHOULD CEREMONIAL PLATFORM STATUS BE UPDATED?

Ren looked at Lael.

Lael looked at Third-Voice.

Third-Voice looked, insofar as a fan-bodied Orison speaker could look, at Salt-Before-Shell.

Salt-Before-Shell said, "Platform contaminated."

The word hit every translator differently.

The main speaker produced contaminated.

Ren's cuff offered: made unsafe by carried residue.

The legal terminal suggested procedurally compromised.

The old Pelagos register, delayed by three seconds, produced: touched by a relation it denied touching.

"Use the Pelagos gloss," Ren said.

The clerk typed with visible concentration.

CEREMONIAL PLATFORM STATUS: TOUCHED BY A RELATION IT DENIED TOUCHING.

Rusk stared at the line. "That cannot go into the official record."

Lael said, "It just did."

"A court will laugh."

"No," Lael said. "A court will become angry because it cannot laugh safely."

Ren admired her and distrusted the admiration. They had started doing that with several people today. It was exhausting, maintaining relation under known difference with one's own opinions.

The detached limb brightened.

A new pulse moved through the amber terraces. Not applause. Not agreement. Something closer to recognition of a cost paid badly but publicly enough to alter the room. Blue-white bodies answered with thin lines of light, careful not to claim the act. Green-white repair sheets around the cracked vessel thickened, adjusting their work to the new platform status.

The Parliament was learning from the protest in real time.

That made the protest dangerous.

The first derivative notice arrived from the route office before the pulse finished crossing the basin.

It was not addressed to Lael. It was addressed to "authorized human observers," which meant someone outside the basin had already decided the Orison event needed human packaging before it could become news. The message slid into the secondary console with a priority flag and a draft summary attached.

Rusk saw the header and went very still.

Lael opened it.

Ren watched her expression flatten.

"What?" they asked.

Lael turned the screen so Ren could read.

ROUTE OFFICE SITUATIONAL NOTE / DRAFT:

WITHDRAWAL FACTION REPRESENTATIVE CONDUCTED SYMBOLIC SELF-INJURY DURING PARLIAMENTARY INSTABILITY. HUMANITARIAN PASSAGE REMAINS UNDER REVIEW. COMMERCIAL RISK ELEVATED PENDING ALIEN GOVERNANCE CLARIFICATION.

For a moment Ren's body became smaller around a very old anger.

"No," they said.

The word was not loud. It did not need to be.

Tamsin, back at the terminal after the medical patch and too little rest, had already opened the edit trail. "It came from automated brief synthesis. It pulled possible self-harm event from the first classification before my correction propagated."

"And symbolic," Lael said, voice low. "Where did symbolic come from?"

Tamsin scanned the metadata. "Human public-communications layer. It treats political body cost as symbolic unless medical intervention requested."

Salt-Before-Shell's missing underside remained exposed to the basin.

Ren looked through the membrane and understood the next harm before it landed. If the note left the room, the body cost would become spectacle and pathology at once. Withdrawal would become instability. Human readers would see alien self-injury, not a faction refusing to be counted whole. Commercial risk would become the main translated consequence. By nightfall, routeholders would cite it as proof that Orison governance could not manage its own bodies. By morning, some helpful analyst would recommend protective oversight.

"Kill the note," Ren said.

Lael's fingers were already moving. "I can block transmission from this node. I cannot kill copies generated upstream."

"Then poison the copies with correction."

Rusk said, "Careful."

Ren turned on him. "Do you want that note carrying your nephew's convoy?"

That reached him where principle had not.

His jaw tightened. "No."

"Then help."

Rusk stepped to the terminal beside Lael and opened a routeholder authentication panel. Lael looked at him as if he had drawn a weapon.

"What are you doing?"

"Adding a private-carrier objection to the draft summary."

"On what grounds?"

"Operational inaccuracy."

"Not moral disgust?"

"Moral disgust has no field."

"Find one."

Rusk did not smile. "Working on it."

He entered a carrier-side correction:

SUMMARY MISSTATES OBSERVED EVENT. BODY COST WAS IDENTIFIED BY PARLIAMENT SPEAKER AS POLITICAL ACT WITH ASSISTANCE NOT REQUESTED. CLASSIFICATION AS SELF-INJURY IS UNVERIFIED AND OPERATIONALLY DESTABILIZING.

Ren hated the phrase operationally destabilizing and loved that it would travel.

Lael added a Compact legal correction:

THE TERM SYMBOLIC IS MATERIALY MISLEADING. OBSERVED BODY COST ALTERED CEREMONIAL PLATFORM STATUS AND PARLIAMENTARY CONDITIONS. DO NOT SUMMARIZE AS REPRESENTATIONAL ONLY.

Tamsin, without waiting for permission, added the witness correction:

INITIAL SELF-HARM CLASSIFICATION DISPUTED AND SUPERSEDED IN BASIN RECORD. CURRENT BASIN RECORD: POLITICAL BODY COST / WITNESS REQUIRED / ASSISTANCE NOT REQUESTED.

The console asked for a unified correction reason.

Lael swore for the first time Ren had heard.

It was a small word, efficiently chosen.

Vela-Short said over the channel, "Now we are getting somewhere."

Ren looked at the unified reason field. One line. Two hundred characters. A tiny bureaucratic throat through which the room's whole harm had to pass.

"Use the platform status," Ren said.

Lael typed:

EVENT TOUCHED BY RELATION IT DENIED TOUCHING; PRIOR SUMMARY REPEATS DENIAL.

The field accepted it.

Rusk looked at the sentence. "No one outside this basin will understand that."

"They will ask," Lael said.

"Or ignore it."

"Then your operational objection will annoy them first."

Tamsin sent the correction bundle.

The draft summary changed status from queued to contested. Not corrected. Not withdrawn. Contested. A poor word, but better than released. Sometimes the work of civilization was reducing the speed of a lie.

Then the console showed them where the lie had already learned to run.

Tamsin opened the propagation map with the reluctance of someone lifting a bandage she already knew would smell bad. The draft note had not left as a formal bulletin, but its synthesis fragments had seeded seven receiver queues: routeholder risk, humanitarian corridor watch, medical container dispatch, sovereign contact duty, insurer early-warning, public communications, and archive pre-index.

Each queue displayed a different version of the same harm.

The medical container dispatch had inherited self-injury but dropped symbolic.

The insurer warning had inherited symbolic and instability but dropped assistance not requested.

The public communications layer had turned political body cost into ritualized protest.

The archive pre-index, which Ren hated immediately for existing in real time, had assigned two suggested tags:

ALIEN SELF-MUTILATION

WITHDRAWAL FACTION ESCALATION

"No," Ren said again.

This no was quieter than the first and more dangerous because it had found work to do.

Lael stared at the archive tags. "Who authorized pre-indexing before witness certification?"

Tamsin gave a small, miserable shrug. "It is automatic for events with cross-system risk flags."

"Cross-system risk flag from whose authority?"

"The first classification."

Ren felt the room tilt, not physically but procedurally: the earliest wrong word had become a parent. Every later system could claim innocence because it had inherited a label rather than invented one. Inheritance was one of bureaucracy's favorite disguises. No one had chosen the lie now; everyone was merely respecting lineage.

"Show lineage," Ren said.

Tamsin did.

The interface unfolded a chain of boxes, each connected by pale blue arrows:

POSSIBLE SELF-HARM EVENT -> POLITICAL BODY COST / WITNESS REQUIRED -> DRAFT SUMMARY -> CONTESTED SUMMARY -> DERIVATIVE QUEUES

At first glance the correction seemed present. The second box existed. The contested status existed. But a narrow annotation under the first arrow read:

PRIOR CLASSIFICATION RETAINED FOR SAFETY REVIEW.

Rusk saw it at the same moment Ren did. "The safety review keeps the self-harm flag alive."

"For good reasons in human contexts," Lael said.

"This is not a human context."

"The system does not know that."

"The system was just told."

Lael looked at him. "By whom? A witness slate? A contested correction? A routeholder objection? A clerk whose access level cannot force deletion? Pick the authority that should override a safety-retained medical flag without creating a different liability hole."

Rusk opened his mouth, then closed it.

Ren did not enjoy the silence. It was too close to the useful trap from Chapter Six wearing a new mask. Wrong tags moved quickly because quick movement had saved human lives in other rooms. Safety retention existed because someone, somewhere, had once erased a real injury too fast and called it cultural respect. Every decent rule carried the memory of a failure, and every memory could become violence when applied without listening.

"We do not delete the first flag," Ren said.

Lael's eyes flicked to them.

Ren forced the sentence out before they could resent it. "We quarantine it."

Tamsin straightened. "Quarantine is available for contaminated source classifications."

"Use that."

"It requires a contamination reason."

Vela-Short said, "Please tell me the reason field is longer than a sneeze."

"Five hundred characters," Tamsin said.

"Luxury."

Lael was already drafting. "Initial machine classification generated before basin response. Retention permitted only for audit of classification failure, not as active event descriptor."

Ren added, "And block derivative display."

Tamsin checked. "I can mark it non-displayable in public communications and archive pre-index. I cannot suppress it from safety review."

"Fine. Safety review can see the knife if it knows the knife cut the record."

Lael typed:

INITIAL MACHINE CLASSIFICATION GENERATED BEFORE BASIN RESPONSE. RETAIN FOR AUDIT OF CLASSIFICATION FAILURE ONLY. DO NOT DISPLAY OR DERIVE AS EVENT DESCRIPTOR.

The quarantine request reached the same mandatory approval gate that had swallowed half the day. It did not reject. It did not accept. It produced a new pane labeled SOURCE INTEGRITY REVIEW and asked for corroborating authority.

Tamsin looked at Lael.

Lael looked at Rusk.

Rusk looked, unwillingly, at the basin.

"The Parliament is the authority," Ren said.

"The review pane will not accept Parliament as a credential," Tamsin said.

"Then the pane is unqualified to review source integrity."

"Yes," Tamsin said, almost gently. "And still here."

Salt-Before-Shell's detached limb turned above the platform, amber light moving along the separated filaments. The missing body part had not asked to become a credential. It had not asked to certify its own misreading. Ren felt a flare of disgust at the human reflex to demand that the injured sign the form proving the injury had been understood badly enough to stop repeating it.

Third-Voice's blue-white fan opened.

"Basin can answer," the speaker said.

Lael lifted her head. "Only if answer does not become consent to the review structure."

"Answer can be wound placed beside wrong tool," Third-Voice said.

Ren translated for the terminal, not for the room. "Parliamentary witness statement offered without acceptance of human source-integrity hierarchy."

Tamsin typed it into an attachment label.

The basin changed.

It did not vote. No formal tally opened. Instead, three kinds of body made three kinds of contact with the suspended limb without taking it. Blue-white treaty forms sent thin light through the water beside it and withdrew. Amber withdrawal bodies rotated shell edges toward the missing ridge and held the angle long enough for the record to mark it. Green-white repair sheets around the cracked vessel pulsed once, not toward healing, but toward recognition of a cost that must not be smoothed away.

The terminal struggled. Its first attempt at a gloss was consensus acknowledgement, which Ren killed before it could breed.

"No," they said. "Witness convergence."

Third-Voice's green-black edge darkened.

"Convergence suggests closing," the speaker said.

"Witness adjacency."

Amber brightened.

Ren entered:

PARLIAMENTARY WITNESS ADJACENCY: MULTIPLE RELATION CURRENTS MARK BODY COST WITHOUT CLAIMING, HEALING, OR CLOSING IT.

The source review pane accepted the attachment as corroborating context, which was not justice but was a handle.

Rusk released a breath. "Will that quarantine the public tags?"

Tamsin ran the preview.

ALIEN SELF-MUTILATION disappeared from public communications.

WITHDRAWAL FACTION ESCALATION changed to:

BODY-COST EVENT UNDER CONTESTED HUMAN CLASSIFICATION

Ren disliked event, contested, and human classification less than the alternatives.

The archive pre-index now displayed:

POLITICAL BODY COST

CLASSIFICATION FAILURE AUDIT

PLATFORM STATUS DISPUTE

"Better," Lael said.

"No," Ren said. "Less harmful."

"I will take less harmful while the medicine is moving."

That was Lael's whole day in one sentence, and perhaps the entire Compact: take less harmful while the medicine is moving; try not to let less harmful harden into good.

The insurer warning proved more stubborn. It retained instability because instability did not require self-harm to frighten money. Rusk opened the carrier-side preview and swore with less elegance than Lael had.

"It is using missing limb as governance volatility," he said.

"Of course it is," Vela-Short said. "Markets see a body do politics and ask what it costs to bet against the body."

Rusk ignored the channel. "If this stays, every private carrier will price the Shoals as a biological-risk corridor."

Salt-Before-Shell's speaker scraped once, not speech yet. Ren waited.

The withdrawal body turned enough that the missing ridge faced the gallery again.

"Human price sees missing. Does human price see why missing was made?"

Rusk looked at the insurer warning as if it had betrayed him personally. "No."

"Then human price is blind and calls blindness risk."

Rusk's face tightened. "Yes."

Ren expected him to defend the models. He did not. That made the moment more useful and less comfortable.

Lael said, "Can you attach your operational objection directly to the insurer feed?"

"Only as routeholder advisory."

"Do it."

"Advisory can be ignored."

"So can law. We still write it."

Rusk gave her a look that was almost respect and then opened the feed. His sponsor credentials gave him more room than Tamsin's public console, which irritated everyone except the system. He inserted a routeholder advisory into the insurer warning:

BODY-COST EVENT DOES NOT INDICATE LOSS OF PASSAGE GOVERNANCE. COMMERCIAL DELAY REFLECTS HUMAN CATEGORY FAILURE AND SEPARATED CARGO REVIEW, NOT ABSENCE OF ORISON AUTHORITY.

The model scored the advisory as low-confidence human interpretation.
