The patterns did not match like identical signals. They related like two bodies adjusting around the same invisible stone.

"Explain," Lael said.

"To which law?" Oru-Kel asked.

"Start with physics."

"Physics is a law with better instruments."

"Start anyway."

The stream shifted. Ren's cuff, still in reduced mode, offered a provisional frame:

ROUTE DISTURBANCE / RELATIONAL SIMPLIFICATION / BOUNDARY RESPONSE

Oru-Kel spoke over it.

"Lumen device did not answer message. It reacted to transit simplification. Route cut through layered relation. Device reduced signal. Not punishment. Boundary."

Rusk leaned forward. "You are saying the Pale Arc object reacted because routes simplified space?"

"Crude enough to begin."

"And Parliament basin?"

"Basin reacts when political relation is simplified beyond body tolerance."

The room held very still.

Ren felt the sentence move through every conflict of the day and touch each one: the cracked exchange vessel, Salt-Before-Shell's missing limb, Lael's rejected stable-party clause, Rusk's convoy clock, the official record that captured the least important truths, the word unanimous failing in human hands.

"You are analogizing politics to spacetime," Lael said.

"No," Oru-Kel replied. "Humans analogize when embarrassed by relation. Oru-Kel observes two boundary practices in different materials."

Ren wrote:

NOT ANALOGY / BOUNDARY PRACTICE ACROSS MATERIALS.

They hated how grand that sounded. They kept it because the alternative was cowardice disguised as modesty.

Rusk said, "Does this mean private bypass routes could trigger Pale Arc response?"

Oru-Kel's stream sharpened.

"Could is soft."

"Would?"

"Would is too hard."

"Then what word do you want?"

"Already participates."

Rusk went quiet.

Lael asked, "Private bypass planning is already part of the boundary risk?"

"Yes."

"Even before execution?"

"Planning changes route pressure. Markets move ships before engines fire."

Ren saw Rusk's face then. Not guilt exactly. Calculation losing altitude.

"How far has that pressure propagated?" he asked.

Oru-Kel sent a map.

It was not a routeholder map. It did not show lanes as lines and ports as profitable interruptions. It showed stress: insurance repricing, cold-chain reroutes, convoy clustering, private corridor speculation, Orison withdrawal currents, Compact emergency classifications, Glassward observers pricing identity risk, Mars clinics requesting backup grafts, Ash Belt escorts quoting hazard premiums, three small stations delaying nonurgent departures because Shoals uncertainty had entered their scheduling models.

All of it before any bypass ship moved.

"That is not a route map," Rusk said.

"Correct," Oru-Kel said. "It is a pressure memory."

Lael looked at him. "Can your consortium stop the bypass pricing?"

"No."

"Can it slow it?"

"Maybe."

Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia rippled. "Maybe returns."

Rusk did not look away from the pressure map. "I can issue an advisory saying medical risk bridge does not imply general Shoals instability pricing."

Lael said, "You should have done that already."

"Yes."

The admission came fast enough to matter and too late to absolve.

Rusk opened a packet.

Ren watched him draft, delete, draft again. This was not like the care-passage request. There, he had asked the room for a way to carry his need without owning the wound. Here, he had to spend power outward into systems that rewarded him for keeping pressure usable.

His first line was bad:

The Parliament situation remains contained.

Ren said, "No."

Rusk deleted it.

His second was worse:

No material instability has been observed.

Lael said, "A cracked vote body is looking at you."

Deleted.

He put both hands flat on the terminal.

"Then give me a sentence that does not cause panic."

Oru-Kel answered, "Panic sometimes honest."

"Panic kills schedules."

"Schedules sometimes hide panic until engines fire."

Oru-Kel's pressure map changed before anyone found a better sentence.

The speculative bypass lines thickened along two private corridors Ren recognized from old Ash Belt hearings: not official routes, not illegal enough to stop at planning stage, the kind of expensive possibility that existed so underwriters could say alternatives were available and charge accordingly. One line skirted the Shoals outer gravity wells and grazed a region the Lumen files marked as rhythm-sensitive. The other passed through a maintenance shadow where Orison acoustic surveys were thin because humans had never funded listening where no profitable route was supposed to go.

The map attached names.

Not poetic names. Company names. Broker pools. Advisory desks. Escort syndicates. A small Mars clinic cooperative suddenly priced into a fallback route it could never afford. A Glassward identity-carrier network delaying a transfer because one underwriter had tagged Orison politics as personhood-risk-adjacent. Three station schools postponing dormitory nutrition culture shipments because a routing algorithm had bundled them with nonurgent commercial freight.

Ren heard Lael inhale.

Rusk looked as if someone had opened a private ledger inside his ribs.

"Those are internal feeds," he said.

Oru-Kel replied, "Boundary does not ask whether pressure is embarrassed."

"How did you get them?"

"They are in route behavior."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the answer before ownership."

Rusk turned to Lael. "Some of this is proprietary."

Lael did not blink. "Some of it is now evidence of risk propagation."

"If this map leaves the room unfiltered, half the private corridor market convulses."

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "Market already convulses. Human asks wound to hide shaking."

The pressure map pulsed again. One speculative line brightened in red: not because a ship had moved, but because pricing bots had started treating the medical risk bridge as proof that Shoals authority was both necessary and unreliable. A contradiction, profitable enough to survive.

Ren felt tired in a place sleep would not reach.

"Oru-Kel," they said, "is the pressure map itself increasing pressure by being seen?"

"Yes."

Rusk spread his hands. "Then why show it?"

"Because hidden pressure still pushes boundary. Seen pressure may be redirected."

"Or accelerated."

"Yes."

That yes entered the room like a tool with no handle.

Lael said, "We need a release rule for the map."

Oru-Kel's dark body had not yet arrived, but the analysis stream answered with immediate severity. "Do not release as route map."

Rusk laughed once. "It is a route map."

"No," Ren said. "It is a pressure map. If it leaves as a route map, every line becomes a proposal."

Lael was already writing.

BOUNDARY PRESSURE MAP: USE ONLY TO IDENTIFY SPECULATIVE ROUTE PRICING, UNDERWRITER CASCADE, AND SIMPLIFICATION RISK. NOT NAVIGATIONAL. NOT CORRIDOR RECOMMENDATION. NOT EVIDENCE OF SAFE ALTERNATIVE PASSAGE.

Rusk read the warning. "Add: dissemination restricted to emergency risk desks."

Ren said, "And Orison witness table."

Rusk hesitated.

Salt-Before-Shell's missing limb drifted at the edge of the repair chamber.

Rusk added it.

Lael said, "And any use in pricing must preserve the distinction between medical care passage and commercial bypass speculation."

Rusk added that too, slower.

Oru-Kel's stream sent a low pulse through the membrane.

"Less harmful container."

"Not harmless," Ren said.

"Harmless is a child's word for power not yet noticed."

Ren wrote that down and immediately regretted how useful it would be.

Rusk closed his eyes.

Ren surprised themselves by helping.

"Try: Humanitarian medical passage remains covered under a single-window care-passage current. This does not indicate generalized treaty continuity or generalized route failure. Commercial speculation on Shoals bypass routes is materially premature and may increase risk."

Lael said, "Add: sponsors treating emergency care passage as evidence of market-route instability may lose risk-bridge priority."

Rusk stared at her. "I cannot threaten every sponsor."

"You can threaten the ones who need your bridge."

"That is ugly."

"Yes."

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "Ugly tool pointed away from wound. Improvement possible."

Rusk added the line.

Oru-Kel's stream pulsed, inspecting the advisory as if it were an acoustic structure rather than a human threat letter.

"Less simplifying," they said.

Rusk sent it.

The advisory did not travel as a sentence.

That was the next lesson.

It broke into permissions, tags, delays, objections, compliance copies, pricing notes, and a dozen abbreviated notices that landed in systems built to read only the part of a warning that could affect their own liability. Rusk opened the outbound trace because Lael asked once and he had apparently decided, for this hour at least, to spend less effort pretending opacity was expertise.

The emergency risk desks received the full text.

The underwriter clusters received the threat line first.

The escort syndicates received a shortened operational note:

CARE-PASSAGE BRIDGE DOES NOT SUPPORT GENERAL SHOALS BYPASS PRICING.

The Mars clinic cooperative received the medical coverage line and nothing about route speculation.

The Glassward identity-carrier network received the phrase personhood-risk-adjacent removed from its active model, but retained it in audit memory.

The Orison witness table received the pressure map with the non-navigational warning attached.

The public communications layer received nothing, which Ren distrusted almost as much as they would have distrusted a summary.

"Why no public notice?" they asked.

Rusk did not answer immediately. He was looking at the outbound trace with the expression of a man discovering that his own tools had been gossiping in different dialects.

"Because public notice would confirm that private bypass speculation exists," he said finally. "That can accelerate it."

"And no public notice lets private systems profit from hidden pressure."

"Yes."

Lael said, "Can we issue a public boundary without naming routes?"

Rusk opened a communications constraint pane. "Maybe. But if it is too vague, everyone reads it as reassurance. If it is too specific, everyone reads it as warning. If it mentions Pale Arc, markets panic. If it does not mention Pale Arc, underwriters treat it as local governance noise."

Keeps-Water-Calm's vessel shifted beside the membrane. "Human public words become gates even when built as windows."

"Yes," Ren said.

Oru-Kel's body, still approaching through the lower current, sent a pressure pulse into the trace display. A new overlay appeared, not from human systems but from Oru-Kel's acoustic analysis: each outbound advisory fragment generated a faint projected response. The threat line dimmed two pricing clusters but brightened one resentment path among sponsors. The non-navigational map warning reduced escort syndicate route conversion but increased requests for private briefings. The Mars clinic note stabilized medical coverage but also made one cold-chain guarantor ask whether clinic-linked cargo should be separated from Orison-maintained infrastructure in future windows.

Every correction cast a shadow.

Ren wrote:

ADVISORY EFFECT IS NOT UNIFORM; EACH RECEIVER MAKES A DIFFERENT HARM POSSIBLE.

Rusk read it. "That is true and unusable."

"Good," Lael said. "Then maybe it cannot become a slogan."

Tamsin, working from the clerk's console, opened the release log for the pressure map. "There is a request."

Everyone looked.

The request came from a Compact emergency risk desk, not a private broker. That should have been comforting. It was not. The desk wanted to share a redacted version of the pressure map with two allied route authorities under a standard situational-awareness clause.

Lael read the clause and said, "No."

Rusk looked over her shoulder. "Those authorities coordinate medical alternates."

"They also coordinate commercial contingency lanes."

"They need warning."

"They do not need lines."

Oru-Kel's stream pulsed:

LINES BECOME INVITATIONS WHEN FEAR NEEDS SHAPE.

Ren added it to the release rule.

Rusk said, "Can you give them a line-free version?"

Tamsin checked the map tool. "Aggregate pressure bands. No corridors, no broker names, no route geometry."

"Would that be useful?"

Oru-Kel answered. "Useful enough to slow. Not useful enough to steer."

Lael nodded. "That is the target."

Rusk rubbed both hands over his face. "I hate that I understand that sentence."

"Keep understanding," Ren said. "It may become habit."

"Do not threaten me with virtue."

"No danger of that yet."

He almost smiled. Almost.

Tamsin generated a line-free pressure brief. It looked strange without routes: a field of intensities around categories of behavior rather than places. Speculative pricing. Escort withdrawal. Medical coverage fragility. Identity-risk contamination. Underfunded listening gaps. Compact classification drag. Orison withdrawal pressure. No corridor could be traced from it. No captain could fly by it. No broker could turn it directly into a premium without doing new harm in their own name.

Ren felt the distinction settle into them. A map told power where to go. A pressure brief told responsibility where to look.

They wrote exactly that, then crossed out power because it sounded too grand and not specific enough.

A ROUTE MAP TELLS ACTORS WHERE TO GO. A PRESSURE BRIEF TELLS RESPONSIBLE PARTIES WHERE TO LOOK WITHOUT PROVIDING A PATH.

Lael copied it into the release rule.

The risk desk request pane asked:

REDACTION BASIS:

SECURITY / COMMERCIAL CONFIDENTIALITY / DIPLOMATIC SENSITIVITY / TECHNICAL UNCERTAINTY

Ren groaned.

"All wrong?" Tamsin asked.

"All incomplete."

Lael selected TECHNICAL UNCERTAINTY and DIPLOMATIC SENSITIVITY.

Oru-Kel's nodes darkened.

"Technical uncertainty suggests better instruments can release map later," the mathematician said.

Lael paused.

Ren saw the problem. If the map was withheld because it was uncertain, someone would argue that better analysis should make it shareable. But the danger was not only uncertainty. It was use.

"Select security too," Rusk said.

Lael looked at him. "Commercial security?"

"No. Boundary security."

"That category does not exist."

"Then misuse the existing one and define it."

Ren gave him a look.

He shrugged. "Learning from the room."

Lael selected SECURITY and wrote:

SECURITY BASIS IS MISUSE RISK: ROUTE GEOMETRY AND BROKER-SPECIFIC PRESSURE LINES COULD CREATE OR ACCELERATE SPECULATIVE BYPASS ACTION. REDACTION DOES NOT CLAIM COMMERCIAL CONFIDENTIALITY AND MUST NOT BE USED TO HIDE ACCOUNTABILITY FOR EXISTING PRESSURE.

The pane accepted the definition.

Keeps-Water-Calm said, "Glove with fingers named."

"Better?" Rusk asked before he could stop himself.

"Better enough to continue. Not enough to admire."

"I will take that."

"Do not take. Carry."

Rusk shut his mouth.

The line-free pressure brief went out to the emergency risk desk with a restricted forwarding rule:

MAY SHARE ONLY AS AGGREGATE PRESSURE BRIEF / NO ROUTE GEOMETRY / NO SAFE-ALTERNATIVE INFERENCE / NO PRICING MODEL IMPORT WITHOUT CARE-PASSAGE SEPARATION.

The first allied authority accepted.

The second requested route geometry under emergency alternate planning.

Lael rejected the request.

It returned with a higher priority flag.

Rusk's shoulders rose.

"They will call," he said.

"Let them," Lael replied.

The call came in text first:

WITHOUT CORRIDOR GEOMETRY WE CANNOT ASSESS WHETHER MEDICAL ALTERNATES EXIST.

Ren looked at Oru-Kel.

The mathematician's body had reached the membrane. Its translucent ribbon folded once, slowly, each acoustic node opening in a different order. The pressure in Ren's teeth changed.

Oru-Kel said, "Answer: if alternate requires boundary-unsafe geometry, alternate is not medical help. It is delayed injury."

Lael typed.

Rusk added, "Offer supervised query instead of map release."

Everyone looked at him.

He said, "They ask a specific medical alternate question. Oru-Kel or the witness table answers pressure-safe, not route-open."

Oru-Kel considered. The pause felt mathematical, not dramatic.

"Accept if query cannot be used to reconstruct corridor."

Tamsin created a supervised-query condition:

QUESTION MUST IDENTIFY CARE NEED AND ORIGIN/DESTINATION CLASS WITHOUT REQUESTING OR RECEIVING FULL ROUTE GEOMETRY. RESPONSE MAY INDICATE UNSAFE / INSUFFICIENT DATA / EXISTING COVERAGE PREFERRED / REVIEW REQUIRED. NO ALTERNATE PATH EXPORT.

The allied authority objected to the lack of path export.

Lael rejected again.

The authority went silent.

Rusk watched the trace. "They are probably angry."

Ren said, "Good."

"Angry offices retaliate."

"Yes."

Keeps-Water-Calm answered, "Boundary that never angers power is decoration."

Ren almost marked it public, caught themselves, and sealed it. Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia moved in what might have been approval of the restraint, or might have been impatience with the need for restraint.

The pressure map shifted again. The second authority's potential alternate lane did not brighten. That was something.

The advisory trace, the line-free brief, the rejected geometry request, and the supervised-query rule attached themselves to the boundary packet like successive layers of an ugly shell.

Ren titled the new exhibit:

PRESSURE WITHOUT PATH.

Then, because titles were traps too, they added:

NOT A MODEL FOR GENERAL RELEASE.

Oru-Kel's nodes pulsed once.

"Less simplifying," they said again.

This time it sounded less like evaluation and more like a warning that the work would have to be repeated every time a receiver asked the same question in a better suit.

The pressure map did not clear. It shifted by fractions. A few speculative corridor lines dimmed. Two underwriter clusters slowed repricing. One Ash Belt escort firm marked its hazard quote pending review. Small movements, not victory.

Oru-Kel said, "Boundary does not reward. It responds."

Ren wrote that down too.

The cracked vote body pulsed inside its repair chamber. The modified exchange vessel beside it answered with a softer pulse. The care-passage current remained narrow but open. Salt-Before-Shell's held limb drifted at the edge of Vessel-kin's body, absence still teaching or festering. No one could yet tell which.

Lael looked at the green-black fan. "Third-Voice, does this analysis change the Parliament's internal process?"

Third-Voice took longer than usual.

When the answer came, each fan spoke in sequence.

Blue-white: "Human need remains."

Amber: "Human pressure spreads."

Green-black: "Boundary hears simplification before law admits sound."

Then all three together, not blended:

"Process must slow where clocks demand false speed."

Rusk made a sound of despair. "We just protected one convoy window."

"Yes," Third-Voice said.

"And now you slow the process?"

"Because the window was protected without closing the wound."

"That is not how human confidence works."

Salt-Before-Shell answered. "We noticed."

The basin's lower current warmed.

Oru-Kel's body had arrived.

It emerged from a shadowed inlet below the Parliament floor: not large, not bright, a long ribbon of dark translucent tissue with pale acoustic nodes along its sides. Each node opened and closed at a slightly different interval. The body seemed less like a person entering a room than an equation choosing to become vulnerable to touch.

Oru-Kel moved toward the membrane.

"Ren Okoye," they said.

"I am here."

"Your witness record must hold two refusals."

"Which two?"

"Refuse to make Parliament wound only local politics. Refuse to make Pale Arc boundary only cosmic threat."

Ren felt the shape of the trap. "If I separate them completely, we miss the shared boundary practice. If I collapse them, I turn Orison politics into evidence for human fear about the Pale Arc."

"Interpreter begins accurately."

Not praise. In this room, accuracy was sometimes the kindest thing anyone offered.

Ren added a new witness packet:

BOUNDARY RELATION / DO NOT COLLAPSE SCALE.

Their hands did not shake this time.

That worried them.

The basin pulse moved again, quieter now, threading Lumen's old silence through Parliament's salt-change wound. Outside the Shoals, markets, clinics, route offices, and private captains would keep counting hours. Inside, the Orison had heard a boundary in the body of their own process.

The next question would be whether human law could recognize a treaty party that had no stable bones.

Chapter 12: Treaty Parties Do Not Have Bones

Lael Shun asked the question in the least offensive form Ren had ever heard.

It was still offensive.

"For emergency record purposes," Lael said, standing with both hands visible and her slate lowered rather than aimed, "what relation may human law address when it must send notice, receive answer, or assign continuing obligation?"

No one in the basin struck the membrane.

Ren counted that as success.

Rusk leaned close enough to murmur, "That was the gentle version?"

"Yes."

"I am developing sympathy for everyone who dislikes us."

"Useful. Keep it uncomfortable."

Third-Voice descended toward the membrane. Oru-Kel remained in the lower current, acoustic nodes opening at intervals that now felt like a second clock under the human one. Keeps-Water-Calm held position near the repair chamber. Salt-Before-Shell's missing limb remained with Vessel-kin, neither reattached nor discarded. The cracked vote body continued. The modified witness vessel rested beside it, bands dim but not dark.

The Parliament had become a room full of unresolved nouns.

Lael's question waited among them.

Third-Voice's blue-white fan opened first. "Human law wants an address."

"Yes."

Amber: "Address becomes handle."

"It can."

Green-black: "No address becomes absence."

Lael inclined her head. "That is the problem."

Ren watched the three fans move. Third-Voice was not debating Lael as one opponent. They were arranging the whole shape of the problem inside one body. Law needed a place to send care, warning, complaint, sanction, and promise. Law also turned addresses into handles, handles into categories, categories into ownership or erasure. No address left the powerful free to claim no one had answered.

"Human law asks who carries treaty," Third-Voice said.

"Yes."

"Treaty carries relation."

"Human law still needs a party."

"Party is celebration word."

Vela-Short said over the channel, "Not in legal."

"Legal humans damage good words," Third-Voice replied.

"Daily," Lael said.

Rusk's mouth twitched. He suppressed it, probably wisely.

Lael continued, "In Compact law, a treaty party is not necessarily a body. It can be a polity, office, recognized council, emergency authority, archive trust, guardianship structure, or other legal person."

Salt-Before-Shell rotated shell-side toward her.

"Legal person," it said.

Lael did not retreat. "A constructed capacity to hold rights and duties."

"Shell with human hinge."

"Often."

"Always?"

Lael paused. "No. Sometimes a legal person protects relation from being reduced to the nearest powerful body."

Keeps-Water-Calm answered from the repair chamber. "Sometimes reef trust prevented miner hand."

"Yes," Lael said.

"Sometimes reef trust made reef paperwork."

"Yes."

"Both."

"Both."

Ren wrote:

LEGAL PERSONHOOD AS SHELL: PROTECTION AND HANDLE.

They disliked how many good records today looked like weapons waiting for the wrong hand.

Third-Voice moved closer to the membrane.

"Treaty parties do not have bones," the speaker said.

Lael lifted her eyes from her slate.

The sentence was too clean. Everyone felt it. Clean sentences in this room were either gifts or traps, and often both.

Third-Voice continued. "Human bodies have bones and still change. Orison bodies have less bone and still bind. Treaty party is not bone. Treaty party is maintained relation with memory of answer."

Lael wrote nothing for once.

Ren did.

TREATY PARTY IS MAINTAINED RELATION WITH MEMORY OF ANSWER.

They marked it direct, provisional, high risk.

Lael said, "If a relation holds memory of answer, can it also receive notice of breach?"

The blue-white fan brightened. "Yes."

Amber: "If breach notice does not force relation to pretend unchanged."

Green-black: "If notice includes boundary disturbance."

Lael started writing.

"Can it make commitments?"

Blue-white: "Yes."

Amber: "Commitment must show which bodies carry cost."

Green-black: "Commitment must show what scale it disturbs."

"Can it refuse?"

All three fans opened.

"Must."

Lael's pen stopped.

Ren saw it land. Human law loved capacity. Capacity to contract, answer, receive notice, make obligations. Refusal was often treated as a failure to complete the business of law. Here, refusal was the first proof a relation had not been captured.

Lael said, quieter, "A treaty party that cannot refuse is not a treaty party."

Salt-Before-Shell brightened along the missing underside.

"Human says old thing as if new."

Lael accepted the hit. "Human says old thing into a record that may otherwise forget."

Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia rippled. "Better."

Oru-Kel's nodes pulsed. "Add scale."

Lael looked toward the lower current. "Explain."

"A relation may refuse at one scale and bind at another. Medical passage moves. Commercial continuity refused. Pale Arc bypass pressure refused. Shoals care relation maintained. Human law often asks one answer to carry all scales."

Rusk said, "Because multiple answers break operations."

Oru-Kel replied, "Single false answer breaks boundaries."

"Operations break people too," Rusk said.

"Yes."

The acknowledgment was immediate enough to unsettle him.

Lael opened a new legal note.

POSSIBLE FORM: RELATIONAL TREATY ADDRESS

Ren leaned over. "Possible?"

"If I call it proposed, someone will route it."

"Fair."

Lael wrote:

A relational treaty address is not a stable sovereign body, office, or individual signer. It is a maintained relation capable of receiving notice, preserving memory of answer, making limited commitments, refusing at specified scales, and identifying which bodies or processes carry material cost. It does not imply unity, unanimity, biological continuity, or administrative sameness.

Rusk read it and looked pained. "That is going to make lawyers reproduce."

"Unfortunately, yes," Lael said.

"Can it keep a route open?"

"Not by itself."

"Can it survive court?"

Lael looked at the basin. "Not alone."

Third-Voice said, "No relation survives alone."

The clerk, who had become bolder with each better pane, raised one hand and then looked embarrassed.

Lael said, "Speak."

"Where does the address live?"

Everyone turned toward her.

She flushed. "I mean, if a notice has to be sent. Not metaphorically. Technically. Does it go to Third-Voice? Parliament Basin? A relay? A living reef? A schedule? A wet archive?"

Ren wanted to hug her and never would. It was exactly the practical question that made philosophy accountable.

Third-Voice's fans moved in sequence.

Blue-white: "Notice to care-passage current for passage matters."

Amber: "Notice to withdrawal-protected chamber for refusal matters."

Green-black: "Notice to boundary-listening archive for route disturbance matters."

Lael's face changed. "Separate addresses by scale and burden."

"Yes," Third-Voice said.

Rusk said, "That means a human sender must know what kind of matter it is before sending."

Ren said, "Yes."

"Humans are bad at that."

Salt-Before-Shell replied, "We noticed."

Rusk ignored the hit because it was now familiar enough to function as weather. "If they choose wrong?"

Keeps-Water-Calm answered. "Wrong address must not create false silence."

Lael wrote faster.

MISADDRESSED NOTICE SHALL NOT BE TREATED AS NONRESPONSE UNTIL RELATIONAL ROUTING REVIEW OCCURS.

Rusk said, "That will slow everything."

Lael did not look up. "Yes."

"People will abuse it."

"Yes."

"You are writing delay into law."

Lael looked up then. "No. I am writing a cost for being wrong about who you are speaking to."

Ren felt that sentence settle beside all the others.

Tamsin opened a test notice without being asked.

The form was not live. She marked it visibly with red bands and the words DRY RUN / NO SERVICE / CATEGORY TEST. Ren appreciated the triple warning. In this room, even practice could become precedent if left unattended.

"Give me a matter," Tamsin said.

Rusk frowned. "You are asking us?"

"I am asking the room."

That was better than asking the humans, and everyone seemed to know it.

Lael said, "Medical container delay."

Tamsin typed:

MATTER: MEDICAL CONTAINER DELAY / CARE RECIPIENT RISK / CONVOY WINDOW FORTY-THREE

The form's default recipient appeared:

ORISON SHOALS PARLIAMENT / GENERAL TREATY OFFICE

No one had to say why that was wrong.

Tamsin selected relational routing review.

Blue-white answered through Third-Voice: "Care-passage current."

The form resisted. It wanted an office, relay address, or named signatory. Tamsin's mouth tightened. She created a temporary destination field:

CARE-PASSAGE CURRENT / PARLIAMENT BASIN WITNESS PACKET / LIMITED TO MEDICAL, STATION-SAFETY, SUBSISTENCE TRANSIT

The form flashed:

NONSTANDARD RECIPIENT.

Lael said, "Accept nonstandard."

Tamsin did.

"Next matter," she said.

Ren said, "Commercial delay penalty."

Rusk gave them a look.

"You wanted practical," Ren said.

Tamsin typed the matter.

The default recipient again became general treaty office. Amber light moved along the upper terrace before anyone spoke.

Salt-Before-Shell answered, "No care-passage current. Commercial pressure to withdrawal-protected chamber if claiming relation. Otherwise no Orison address for sponsor pain."

Rusk inhaled sharply. "Sponsor pain moves ships."

"Sponsor pain is not automatically Orison relation," Lael said.

Tamsin typed:

NO ORISON ADDRESS UNLESS COMMERCIAL CLAIM IDENTIFIES RELATION, BODY COST, AND SCALE OF REQUESTED BURDEN.

The form did not know what to do with that. It opened a legal exception pane.

Lael smiled without pleasure. "Good."

"Next," Tamsin said, a little steadier.

Oru-Kel's nodes pulsed. "Speculative bypass pricing."

The matter entered the form and immediately selected route safety office.

Green-black light moved through the basin floor.

Oru-Kel said, "Boundary-listening archive. Also route office. Also Orison witness table. No single address."

Tamsin created three linked recipients and marked them:

SCALE-SPLIT NOTICE / RESPONSE MAY DIFFER BY SCALE / NONRESPONSE AT ONE SCALE SHALL NOT VOID RESPONSE AT ANOTHER.

Rusk leaned over the pane. "That is going to make response tracking miserable."

"Yes," Tamsin said before Lael could. "But it will stop one office from pretending another office's silence means consent."

The dry-run form immediately proved her right by trying to do exactly that.

Because the notice had three linked recipients, the route office test environment generated three response timers. CARE-PASSAGE CURRENT displayed a blue pending mark. BOUNDARY-LISTENING ARCHIVE displayed a green received mark because Oru-Kel's analysis stream had acknowledged the category. ORISON WITNESS TABLE displayed amber because no single witness body had accepted the notice.

The summary line at the top of the form changed to:

PARTIAL RESPONSE / UNRESOLVED RECIPIENT / ESCALATION AVAILABLE

Then, beneath it, in smaller text:

NO RESPONSE FROM WITNESS TABLE MAY BE TREATED AS PROCEDURAL NONRESPONSE AFTER TIMER EXPIRY.

Lael made a sound so controlled it was worse than swearing.

"There," Tamsin said, and her voice shook with vindication rather than fear. "That is the thing."

Rusk frowned at the pane. "It says may be treated. Not must."

"May is where overworked offices hide decisions they want to call neutral," Tamsin said.

No one corrected her.

Ren wrote:

TEST FORM CONVERTS SCALE-SPLIT AMBER INTO POTENTIAL NONRESPONSE SANCTION.

Salt-Before-Shell's shell ridge lifted. "Human form hungry for silence."

"Yes," Lael said.

Oru-Kel's nodes pulsed under the floor. "Silence at wrong scale becomes false permission at desired scale."

Lael copied that directly into the routing review note, then looked at Ren.

"Direct?" she asked.

Ren listened to the basin before answering. Blue-white had not objected. Amber had brightened in grim recognition. Green-black remained open.

"Direct, high risk," Ren said. "Do not abstract it."

Lael added:

NONRESPONSE SHALL BE SCALE-BOUND. SILENCE, DELAY, OR AMBER STATUS AT ONE RELATIONAL ADDRESS SHALL NOT CREATE PERMISSION, BREACH, WAIVER, OR CONSENT AT ANY OTHER SCALE.

The test form rejected the line as too broad.

Of course it did.

Tamsin opened the rule constraints. "It wants specific legal effects."

"Give it all of them," Lael said.

Tamsin created separate prohibitions:

NO DEEMED CONSENT.

NO DEFAULT BREACH.

NO WAIVER OF OBJECTION.

NO STABLE-PARTY INFERENCE.

NO COMMERCIAL ACCESS INFERENCE.

NO FUTURE ROUTE-RISK PRECEDENT.

NO ADVERSE RELIANCE CLAIM WITHOUT ROUTING REVIEW.

The form accepted each one separately, as if a fence became less offensive when assembled post by post.

Rusk read the last line. "Adverse reliance?"

Lael said, "If a human office sends notice to the wrong relational address and then claims it relied on silence to its detriment, I want the record to answer first."

"That will make sponsors furious."

"Sponsors have had a productive day being furious."

Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia moved. "Fury is not injury by itself."

Rusk looked at the Pelagos diplomat. "You keep saying things my sponsors should hear and absolutely cannot be trusted to hear."

"Then carry without making banner."

He nodded once. Not agreement, exactly. Acknowledgment that some sentences had to become behavior rather than citation.

Tamsin ran the dry notice again.

This time the summary line still read PARTIAL RESPONSE, but the nonresponse sanction warning disappeared. A new warning replaced it:

ROUTING REVIEW REQUIRED BEFORE LEGAL EFFECTS MAY BE DRAWN FROM PARTIAL RESPONSE.

Ren exhaled.

"Better," Lael said.

Tamsin did not smile. "The route office export is worse."

She opened it before anyone asked.

In the export view, all the careful scale-split detail collapsed into a receiver summary:

ORISON RESPONSE: PARTIAL / REVIEW REQUIRED

Below it, a confidence field displayed:

RELIABILITY: REDUCED

Rusk said, "That will hit underwriters."

"No," Ren said, tired already. "No, no, no."

Lael leaned closer. "Why reduced?"

Tamsin checked the derivation. "Multiple addresses. Nonstandard recipient. Partial response."

"Can reliability be scoped?"

Tamsin searched. "There is a note field."

"Not enough."

"There is a reliability subtype, but the options are terrible."

She showed them:

ENTITY RELIABILITY / COMMUNICATION RELIABILITY / PROCEDURAL RELIABILITY / RESPONSE RELIABILITY

Ren said, "It is not Orison reliability. It is human routing confidence."

Lael pointed. "Use procedural."

Tamsin selected PROCEDURAL RELIABILITY and overwrote the summary text:

HUMAN ROUTING CONFIDENCE REDUCED; ORISON RELATIONAL RESPONSE NOT CHARACTERIZED AS UNRELIABLE.

The export system allowed the note but kept the red label:

RELIABILITY: REDUCED

Rusk winced. "Machines love red."

Oru-Kel's body had not reached the membrane yet in this chapter's earlier time, but the analysis stream pulsed as if offended by color. "Red travels faster than sentence."

Ren added:

COLOR-CODE WARNING MAY MISSTATE SUBJECT OF REDUCED CONFIDENCE.

Lael opened the export controls and found the setting behind a permissions fold:

DISPLAY LABEL SUBJECT:

RECIPIENT / SENDER / ROUTING / EVENT

Default: RECIPIENT.

Everyone went still.

"There," Lael said softly. "There is the whole empire in a default."

She changed RECIPIENT to ROUTING.

The export summary changed:

ROUTING RELIABILITY: REDUCED

Then:

HUMAN ROUTING CONFIDENCE REDUCED; ORISON RELATIONAL RESPONSE NOT CHARACTERIZED AS UNRELIABLE.

The red label remained red, but now the wound was closer to the right body.

Salt-Before-Shell answered from the amber terrace. "Human form admits human limp."

"Barely," Ren said.

"Barely counts if limp stops being assigned to other body."

Tamsin saved the corrected export profile as:

SCALE-SPLIT ROUTING / HUMAN CONFIDENCE LIMIT.

The system warned:

PROFILE IS NONSTANDARD AND MAY REQUIRE MANUAL SELECTION FOR FUTURE NOTICES.

Lael's expression hardened. "Manual selection means someone will forget."

"Can we set it as default for Orison matters?" Ren asked.

Tamsin checked. "Not globally. Only for this packet and linked emergency notices."

"Do it."

She did.

Rusk said, "That covers this crisis but not future offices."

"Then future offices will have this failure as evidence," Lael said.

"Evidence is not a patch."

"No. But it is a tool for forcing patches later."

Ren watched Tamsin attach the export correction, the separate prohibitions, and the display-label subject change to the dry-run appendix. The appendix had grown from a practical test into a small anatomy of how forms made alien silence profitable.

They titled the section:

HOW WRONG ADDRESS BECOMES FALSE SILENCE.

Then they added:

HOW FALSE SILENCE BECOMES HUMAN CONFIDENCE.

The second title felt accusatory. It was also exact.

The words came out fast, then she seemed to realize everyone had heard them. Her face reddened.

Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia rippled. "Clerk grows teeth."

Tamsin looked alarmed.

Ren said, "In this room, that is probably not an insult."

"Not insult," Keeps-Water-Calm said. "Also not praise."

"I understand," Tamsin said, and sounded as if she almost did.

Lael saved the dry run as an appendix to the wet record:

APPENDIX: RELATIONAL ADDRESS TEST / NO SERVICE / CATEGORY STRESS CHECK.

Then she added three warnings:

1. DEFAULT GENERAL ADDRESS IS MATERIALY MISLEADING FOR SCALE-SPLIT ORISON MATTERS.

2. MISADDRESS SHALL NOT CREATE DEEMED CONSENT, DEFAULT BREACH, OR NONRESPONSE SANCTION WITHOUT ROUTING REVIEW.

3. WOUNDS, WITNESS BODIES, AND EMERGENCY VISIBILITY SHALL NOT BE USED AS ADDRESSES UNLESS THEY EXPRESSLY accept service in body-first terms.

Ren looked at the third line. "Lowercase accept."

Lael looked down and swore softly. The tool had auto-capitalized it, then translated the phrase into contract language in the background suggestion pane:

EXPRESSLY ACCEPTS SERVICE.

Lael deleted the suggestion with unnecessary force.

"Even the software wants a signer," she said.

Third-Voice touched the membrane.

"Human bone habit lives in tools."

"Yes," Lael said.

"Wet record must include tool misbehavior."

Ren added:

SOFTWARE ATTEMPTED TO CONVERT BODY-FIRST TERMS INTO CONTRACT ACCEPTANCE LANGUAGE; CORRECTED MANUALLY.

Rusk looked exhausted. "We are now recording autocorrect."

"Autocorrect has probably harmed more treaties than several admirals," Ren said.

Vela-Short said, "Finally, a doctrine I can support."

For one brief second, even Lael laughed.

The laugh did not solve anything. It did remind the room that people were still inside the machinery they were trying to make less cruel.

The cracked vote body pulsed.

The pulse was small, but the repair chamber carried it outward. Amber and blue-white remained visible within it. The dark warmth between them had stabilized. Not healed. Not resolved. Stable enough to continue being witnessed.

Vessel-kin spoke.

"Body hears address question."

Ren turned toward the chamber. "Does the body answer?"

"Body remains not address."

Lael went still.

Vessel-kin continued. "Do not send treaty notice to wound because wound taught humans to see."

Ren closed their eyes for one second.

They had almost done that. Not literally, perhaps, but structurally. The wounded body was the most visible thing in the room. Human systems would love it. They would make it emblem, case name, doctrine, routing key.

BODY-FIRST WITNESS, Ren wrote, underlining twice. WOUND IS NOT ADDRESS.

Lael added it to the legal note without comment.

Rusk looked at the note, then the clock, then the basin. "This relational address structure is not going to be ready before the next convoy window."

"No," Lael said.

"So what do we do when the next notice comes?"

Third-Voice answered, "Send small. Mark scale. Admit uncertainty. Do not punish rerouting."

Lael wrote:

INTERIM PRACTICE.

Ren added:

SMALL NOTICE / MARK SCALE / ADMIT UNCERTAINTY / NO PUNISHMENT FOR REROUTING.

The basin pulsed recognition.

Oru-Kel's nodes shifted. "Boundary risk reduced."

"By a legal note?" Rusk asked.

"By refusing false simplification in a place where engines listen later."

Rusk stared at them.

"I hate when I understand just enough to worry more."

"Interpreter condition," Ren said.

"No wonder you all look tired."

Lael saved the note in three places: Compact emergency record, Parliament witness packet, and an unclassified advisory draft marked NOT LAW / DO NOT CITE AS SETTLED. Then she looked at that last warning, grimaced, and added: CITING AS SETTLED SHALL BE EVIDENCE OF BAD FAITH.

Ren said, "Aggressive."

"Practical."

"Both."

Third-Voice touched the membrane lightly.

"Human law brings bone habit."

Lael stood facing them with the note open in her hands. "Yes."

"Today, bone bends."

"Today," Lael said.

Salt-Before-Shell replied from the amber terrace. "Tomorrow, bone tries to harden again."

"Yes," Lael said.

"Then record must remain wet."

Lael looked at Ren.

Ren made the header:

WET RECORD / RELATIONAL TREATY ADDRESS / UNSETTLED.

The clerk smiled faintly. "Courts will hate wet record."

"Good," Lael said. "Dry records have been overconfident."

Outside the membrane, the basin did not celebrate. It maintained. The care-passage current stayed open. The cracked vote body remained unaddressed as doctrine. Oru-Kel's boundary rhythm quieted by one degree. Rusk's clock kept counting, but it had lost the authority to pretend all hours were the same kind of hour.

For the first time since the exchange body cracked, Ren felt the room had not solved a problem, but had stopped making one old problem worse.

In the Salt Parliament, that seemed to count as progress.

Chapter 13: The Minority That Stayed Warm

Progress lasted nineteen minutes.

Ren knew because Rusk's clock, stripped now of its commercial penalty line, still made time visible in the corner of the gallery. The medical transit window moved from abstract pressure toward practical relief. The care-passage current held. The modified exchange vessel remained intact. Lael's wet record sat in three archives like a legal infection waiting to become either fever or immunity.

Then a delegate body on the second amber-blue ring began to steam.

At first Ren thought it was a temperature artifact. Warm brine moved oddly around bodies after salt-change. Human displays exaggerated differences because humans liked heat maps; heat maps made life look actionable.

The clerk saw it too. "Thermal rise in ring two."

Lael looked up from the relational address note. "Repair issue?"

Vessel-kin moved before anyone answered.

That was enough to make the room stop pretending calm.

The delegate body was broad and flat, a pale green form carrying two faint color bands from an earlier procedural exchange. Ren had noticed it only peripherally: a local chamber delegate, perhaps, one of the bodies that helped maintain relation while the visible speakers held the dramatic edges. Now a patch near its lower surface glowed amber-white, not bright, but warm in the way banked coals were warm under ash.

The terminal identified:

DELEGATE BODY / NORTH-EAST MAINTENANCE CURRENT / PRIOR MINORITY POSITION DISSOLVED 2436.188.39.12

Then:

RESIDUAL HEAT DETECTED

Ren's stomach tightened around one word.

Dissolved.

"Who marked that minority position dissolved?" they asked.

The clerk searched. "Automatic procedural record. Before the exchange vessel cracked. The minority position was entered, carried, answered, and marked metabolized into chamber relation."

"Metabolized," Rusk said. "That is good?"

Keeps-Water-Calm replied, "Human asks if digestion is consent."

"I am learning to dislike every verb," Rusk said.

"Good," Ren and Lael said together.

Vessel-kin reached the delegate body. It did not touch the warm patch. It circled, tasting current. Other nearby bodies moved aside with care, not fear exactly, but respect for something that might become injury if crowded.

Third-Voice descended from the central current. "North-east maintenance body carries old minority warmth."

Lael opened the wet record. "Does this relate to the suspended vote?"

"Older."

"How much older?"

The terminal answered with the false confidence of machines that loved timestamps:

POSITION ORIGIN: PRELIMINARY AGENDA ORDER / COMMERCIAL PASSAGE BUNDLING OBJECTION / MINORITY WEIGHT 0.14 / STATUS DISSOLVED

Rusk went very still.

Ren read it twice. "Commercial passage bundling."

Lael looked at him. "Before Salt-Before-Shell withdrew a limb. Before the medical notice. Before you removed the commercial penalty line."

Rusk's face had gone blank in the way powerful people sometimes used blankness to avoid becoming evidence.

Ren said, "Aven."

"I see it."

"Do you?"

His eyes flicked to the warm patch. "A minority objected to bundling commercial cargo with care passage before I made it a human argument."

Salt-Before-Shell answered from the amber terrace. "Before humans heard because humans heard only clock."

The warm patch pulsed.

The official record updated:

RESIDUAL MINORITY HEAT / NONCRITICAL

Ren said, "No."

The clerk was already opening the secondary pane. "Wording?"

Ren thought of the wet record, of treaty parties without bones, of wounds not becoming addresses, of late learning wanting to look beautiful.

"Do not call it residual yet," they said. "Residual means leftover. We do not know if it is leftover."

Lael said, "Continuing?"

Keeps-Water-Calm: "Maybe."

Oru-Kel: "Measure before naming."

Vessel-kin spoke from beside the warm body. "Position metabolizes. Not gone."

Ren wrote:

MINORITY POSITION CONTINUES METABOLIZING IN DELEGATE BODY AFTER PROCEDURAL DISSOLUTION MARK.

The basin pulsed hard enough to ripple the membrane.

Not approval.

Recognition with teeth.

Lael added to the official record:

AUTOMATIC DISSOLUTION MARK CHALLENGED BY BODY-FIRST WITNESS.

The terminal rejected the line.

STATUS ALREADY CLOSED.

The clerk whispered, "It won't reopen."

Lael's expression became bright and unpleasant. "Then we create a reopening event."

"Authority?"

Lael looked at Ren.

Ren looked at the warm delegate body. "Body-first witness request."

Before the clerk could send it, Oru-Kel's nodes opened in a quick irregular sequence.

"Show closure path," they said.

The terminal obliged because it understood audit better than grief.

A procedural chain unfolded beside the heat map:

PRELIMINARY AGENDA ORDER 12-B.

OBJECTION ENTERED BY NORTH-EAST MAINTENANCE CURRENT.

OBJECTION SUMMARY: COMMERCIAL PASSAGE BUNDLING MAY USE CARE NEED TO CARRY UNRELATED ROUTE CLAIMS.

RESPONSE BY BLUE-WHITE TREATY CURRENT: CARE DELAY RISK OUTWEIGHS BUNDLING CONCERN.

RESPONSE BY ROUTE OBSERVER: COMMERCIAL ATTACHMENT NECESSARY FOR COST DISTRIBUTION.

PARLIAMENTARY SYSTEM ACTION: OBJECTION CARRIED INTO CHAMBER RELATION.

STATUS: METABOLIZED.

STATUS CONVERSION: DISSOLVED.

Ren read the chain twice. The first status had at least been Orison-adjacent: metabolized, the idea that a position had entered the relation enough to feed future motion. The second had been human-administrative translation. Dissolved. Gone enough to close a field. Gone enough to stop asking.

"Who converted metabolized to dissolved?" Lael asked.

Tamsin checked the metadata. "Cross-system export. The Compact observer pane did it when the agenda summary synced."

Lael's face went very still.

Ren felt the room tilt.

"Not the Parliament," Rusk said.

Salt-Before-Shell answered, "Parliament allowed pane."

"That is not the same."

"No. Still relation."

Keeps-Water-Calm moved slightly. "At Pelagos, first harm cut reef. Later harm synced forms."

Lael put one hand flat against the console as if she were steadying herself against an old repetition. "The human export collapsed metabolized into dissolved."

Oru-Kel said, "Simplification event."

Green-black light moved through the basin floor, faint but unmistakable.

Ren wrote:

SIMPLIFICATION EVENT: CROSS-SYSTEM EXPORT CONVERTED METABOLIZED MINORITY INTO DISSOLVED STATUS.

They added:

HUMAN RECORD MAY HAVE HELPED HIDE CONTINUING BODY ACTIVITY.

Tamsin looked stricken. "It was automatic."

Lael said, "Automatic is not innocent."

"I know."

"Good. Do not make guilt bigger than work."

Tamsin nodded once, hard, and opened the export settings. "There is a mapping table."

"Of course there is," Ren said.

The table appeared:

ORISON METABOLIZED -> COMPACT DISSOLVED

ORISON HELD-WARM -> COMPACT RESERVED

ORISON REFUSAL-IN-RELATION -> COMPACT OBJECTION PENDING

ORISON BODY-COST -> COMPACT PROTEST ACTION

ORISON CARE-PASSAGE -> COMPACT HUMANITARIAN EXCEPTION

Each arrow was small. Each arrow was a place where the day could have gone wrong and probably had.

Rusk stared at the table. "We route on those exports."

Lael said, "Then you route on translation losses."

"Everyone does."

"Yes."

No one took comfort from the universality.

Third-Voice lowered all three fans toward the warm maintenance body. "North-east warmth hidden by allowed export. Parliament shares fault."

The basin dimmed.

That mattered. Not because it absolved the humans, but because it stopped the accusation from becoming another clean line. Orison process had allowed the human pane. Human pane had simplified Orison process. Routeholder pressure had made the simplification useful. The minority body had carried the heat.

Ren added to the witness packet:

FAULT DISTRIBUTED; HEAT LOCALIZED.

Vessel-kin pulsed recognition.

Lael looked at the mapping table and began disabling automatic export for active Parliament terms.

Tamsin made a small alarmed sound. "That will break downstream summaries."

"Yes."

"Route office will receive untranslated status."

"Good."

"They will ask what it means."

"Excellent."

Rusk said, "That may slow medical passage."

Lael did not look away from the table. "Medical passage is already protected under a narrower packet. We are not letting every future notice inherit a bad dictionary to preserve one convenient summary feed."

Rusk's jaw worked, but he did not object.

Tamsin disabled the first mapping:

ORISON METABOLIZED -> COMPACT DISSOLVED

The interface asked for a replacement.

Ren said, "Leave metabolized untranslated with a warning."

Lael typed:

METABOLIZED [UNTRANSLATED ORISON PROCESS TERM; DOES NOT MEAN DISSOLVED, CLOSED, OR CONSENTED]

The field accepted, barely.

Then the table asked the question every bad table asked once it had been caught:

APPLY RETROACTIVELY?

The three words entered the gallery like a new weather system.

Rusk said, "Careful."

Lael's fingers stayed above the console. "Explain the risk."

"If you apply retroactively, every past export that used dissolved may generate correction notices. Some of those notices feed active routing files, cost calculations, emergency classifications, and litigation holds. You will not only correct the record. You may reopen reliance chains."

Salt-Before-Shell's shell ridge lifted. "Human fears old false comfort losing bed."

"Yes," Rusk said, with less defensiveness than Ren expected. "And some of the people in the bed are clinics, pilots, small stations, and terrified families, not only sponsors."

That was the problem with every honest objection Rusk made. It carried both shield and hostage.

Lael turned to Tamsin. "Can we preview impact without applying?"

"There is an audit mode."

"Run it."

Tamsin selected PREVIEW ONLY. The system objected, then accepted after Lael added LEGAL REVIEW / NO NOTICE / NO RELIANCE CHANGE. The audit spun through the day's exports first, then older Parliament records cached in the Compact observer pane.

Twenty-three entries appeared.

Six were harmless enough to remain only embarrassing: metabolized positions about speaking order, chamber temperature rhythm, and translator fatigue pauses that the Compact had marked dissolved because humans liked a schedule that ended.

Nine were procedural but not dead: local objections carried into later currents without visible status.

Five were active enough to matter for the care-passage dispute.

Three flashed red.

Ren hated red.

Tamsin opened the first.

MINORITY POSITION: LOCAL REPAIR LINEAGE OBJECTION TO COMMERCIAL PACKAGING OF MEMBRANE KITS.

ORISON STATUS: METABOLIZED.

COMPACT EXPORT: DISSOLVED.

DOWNSTREAM USE: MEMBRANE KIT PACKAGING APPROVED AS NONCONTROVERSIAL SUPPORT CARGO.

Rusk's eyes narrowed. "Membrane kits are station-safety."

Lael said, "Packaging of membrane kits is not the same as membrane kits."

The second red entry:

MINORITY POSITION: WITHDRAWAL CURRENT OBJECTION TO ROUTEHOLDER OBSERVER LANGUAGE IN MEDICAL PRIORITY NOTICE.

ORISON STATUS: METABOLIZED.

COMPACT EXPORT: DISSOLVED.

DOWNSTREAM USE: ROUTEHOLDER OBSERVER LANGUAGE RETAINED IN DRAFT PUBLIC SUMMARY.

"That summary was contested," Tamsin said quickly. "It did not release."

"Because we caught another harm later," Ren said. "Not because this one was visible."

The third red entry made the basin dim before the humans finished reading.

MINORITY POSITION: NORTH-EAST MAINTENANCE CURRENT OBJECTION TO CLASSIFYING CARE-PASSAGE AS HUMANITARIAN EXCEPTION RATHER THAN ORISON-BOUND RELATION.

ORISON STATUS: METABOLIZED.

COMPACT EXPORT: DISSOLVED.

DOWNSTREAM USE: CARE-PASSAGE CURRENT DESCRIBED AS EXCEPTION AUTHORITY IN ROUTE BRIDGE DRAFT.

Rusk stepped back from the console.

Lael went very still.

Ren felt the old trap open under their own feet. They had used humanitarian exception language. Lael had used it. Rusk had used it. The packet that moved the medicine had been less devouring than the alternatives, but less devouring did not mean toothless. Somewhere inside the words that saved the convoy window, a minority heat had already objected to becoming exception instead of relation.

"Show timeline," Ren said.

Tamsin did.

The north-east objection had entered before the care-passage current formed visibly, when most human attention had been on Rusk's clock and Salt-Before-Shell's missing limb. The Parliament had carried it inward. The Compact export had marked it dissolved. Lael's later packet had avoided many false continuities, but it had retained emergency exception authority as the human legal spine because something had to hold the field open. The minority position had not stopped the packet. It had stayed warm under it.

Keeps-Water-Calm spoke quietly. "Good act can stand on hidden heat."

Lael's face tightened. "Yes."

Rusk said, "If we change the bridge language now, does it endanger the convoy?"

Tamsin checked dependencies. "Not if we attach a clarification rather than amend authority."

"What clarification?" Lael asked.

Ren looked through the membrane at the warm maintenance body. "That humanitarian exception is the human authority used to move the packet, not the Orison relation that permitted care movement."

Lael began typing immediately:

CLARIFICATION: COMPACT HUMANITARIAN EXCEPTION AUTHORITY DESCRIBES HUMAN LEGAL MECHANISM FOR TRANSMISSION AND ROUTE BRIDGE EXECUTION. IT DOES NOT DESCRIBE THE ORISON CARE-PASSAGE RELATION, DOES NOT CONVERT CARE-PASSAGE INTO HUMAN-DISPENSED EXCEPTION, AND DOES NOT DISSOLVE NORTH-EAST MAINTENANCE CURRENT OBJECTION.

The system warned:

CLARIFICATION MAY REDUCE AUTHORITY CERTAINTY.

Rusk stared at the warning. "Will it?"

Lael said, "Yes."

The medical transit lock had no mercy. It kept counting.

Ren looked at Rusk. "Will reduced authority certainty collapse the bridge?"

Rusk opened the bridge preview. For once, he did not summarize before showing the room. The bridge could tolerate the clarification if the human legal mechanism remained intact and the routeholder reserve maintained coverage. It would produce a review note for future windows. It would not stop this one.

"It holds," he said.

"Say all of it," Lael said.

He swallowed. "It holds for this window. It creates review risk for future windows. It prevents future windows from pretending this objection did not exist."

Salt-Before-Shell's copper filaments brightened.

"Then attach," Lael said.

Tamsin attached the clarification.

The warm patch in the north-east maintenance body cooled another fraction, not because the problem had vanished, but because the record had stopped lying over it quite so heavily.

The audit mode moved to the next question:

ISSUE CORRECTION NOTICES?

Lael did not answer quickly.

Rusk said, "Targeted."

Ren looked at him.

"Not global," he said. "Global correction will flood systems and let everyone claim confusion. Targeted notices to files where the dissolved mapping created active legal, routing, or pricing reliance."

Lael said, "That sounds convenient."

"It is convenient. It is also proportional."

Oru-Kel's nodes pulsed. "Proportional may be care or concealment. Need witness test."

Ren thought of every place proportionality had become an excuse to preserve power. They also thought of Rusk's warning: clinics, pilots, small stations, terrified families. A correction could be a flood. A flood could drown the people it meant to cleanse around.

"Three classes," Ren said. "Immediate correction where active reliance creates harm. Sealed audit where no active reliance. Deferred review where reliance is possible but not current."

Lael nodded slowly. "And each deferred review must say deferred, not resolved."

Tamsin created the notice classes:

ACTIVE HARM RELIANCE / IMMEDIATE CORRECTION.

NO ACTIVE RELIANCE / SEALED AUDIT.

POSSIBLE RELIANCE / DEFERRED REVIEW / NOT RESOLVED.

Rusk added, "No correction notice may convert metabolized into active objection without body-first witness."

Salt-Before-Shell sharpened. "Human wants limit on reappearance."

"Yes," Rusk said. "Because if every metabolized position becomes active in human systems without witness, sponsors will weaponize old warmth as chaos."

Ren hated that he was right.

The north-east maintenance body pulsed once.

Vessel-kin translated: "Warmth does not want human resurrection machine."

Lael added:

CORRECTION SHALL NOT AUTOMATICALLY RECLASSIFY METABOLIZED POSITIONS AS ACTIVE OBJECTIONS. BODY-FIRST OR RELATIONAL WITNESS REQUIRED. PURPOSE IS TO REMOVE FALSE CLOSURE, NOT MANUFACTURE CHAOS.

The phrase manufacture chaos would make some counsel very unhappy. Good.

The audit applied targeted notices.

The membrane kit packaging file moved to immediate correction with a limited cargo review. The routeholder observer language file moved to sealed audit because the summary had not released. The humanitarian exception/care-passage clarification moved to immediate correction because it touched the active bridge.

The remaining entries sorted themselves into deferred review or sealed audit.

No alarms sounded.

No clean victory arrived.

The room had become accustomed to that.

Ren added to the witness packet:

RETROACTIVE CORRECTION LIMITED BY BODY-FIRST WITNESS AND ACTIVE RELIANCE; FALSE CLOSURE REMOVED WITHOUT AUTOMATICALLY INVENTING ACTIVE CHAOS.

Then they paused and added:

THIS LIMIT IS ITSELF DANGEROUS IF USED TO PRESERVE CONVENIENT OLD HARMS.

Lael read the second line and copied it into the legal note.

"You are making the note impossible to use cleanly," Rusk said.

"Yes," Ren said.

"Good," Keeps-Water-Calm said.

The word was so plain that every human in the gallery looked suspicious of it.

Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia rippled. "Do not make praise from one word. Continue."

The warm patch in the maintenance body cooled by a fraction.

Vessel-kin said, "Less hidden."

Not healed.

Less hidden.

The clerk typed:

BODY-FIRST WITNESS REQUEST: DOES NORTH-EAST MAINTENANCE BODY AUTHORIZE REOPENING OF POSITION STATUS?

The warm patch flared.

For three seconds, the gallery smelled of hot salt.

The speakers produced a voice so soft Ren nearly missed it.

"Not reopened. Never closed."

No one moved.

Lael deleted reopening and wrote:

BODY-FIRST WITNESS STATEMENT: POSITION WAS NEVER CLOSED.

The terminal accepted it because the statement had not used the forbidden word reopen.

Vela-Short said, "I am beginning to respect legal trickery when pointed correctly."

"Do not," Lael said. "It becomes vain."

The north-east maintenance body unfolded one edge. The warm amber-white patch became visible as a small internal chamber, no larger than a human palm, lined with tiny cilia. It was not a wound. It was not a tumor. It was a held current, alive enough to answer, small enough that every old record had mistaken its quiet for assimilation.

Rusk spoke carefully. "Why didn't the minority position speak before?"

Salt-Before-Shell: "It did."

Keeps-Water-Calm: "Humans did not hear."

Third-Voice: "Parliament did not hold enough space."

The last answer changed the basin more than the first two.

Amber bodies brightened in satisfaction, but not triumph. Blue-white bodies dimmed, not in defeat but in the pain of a true accusation. Green-white repair lineages moved toward the maintenance body with slow restraint.

Third-Voice had not blamed only humans.

Ren wrote that down because human witnesses loved external blame. It made them feel enlightened without becoming responsible.

THIRD-VOICE ACKNOWLEDGES PARLIAMENT PROCESS FAILED TO HOLD MINORITY SPACE.

High risk. Necessary.

Lael copied it.

Rusk read the line and said, "That matters."

"Yes," Ren said.

"It means this isn't only human contamination."

Ren looked at him.

He raised one hand. "I am not trying to escape blame."

"You noticed the door."

"I am choosing not to walk through it."

Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia moved. "Better enough."

Rusk closed his eyes briefly, then opened them. "The minority objected to commercial-care bundling. If it had remained visible, the care-passage structure might have formed earlier."

"Maybe," Lael said.

"Maybe is doing a lot of work today."

"It is more honest than certainty."

The warm patch pulsed again, and this time the cilia inside it released a thread of amber-white chemical memory into the water. Vessel-kin caught the thread and carried it toward the modified exchange body, not the cracked one. That choice mattered. The minority position would not be fed into the original wound as proof. It would be given a safer witness vessel.

Ren opened a new packet:

MINORITY WARMTH WITNESS / COMMERCIAL-CARE BUNDLING OBJECTION / NEVER CLOSED.

The official record attempted to auto-summarize:

PREVIOUSLY DISSOLVED MINORITY POSITION RECLASSIFIED AS ACTIVE.

Ren stopped it.

"No. That says the record grants activity."

The clerk nodded. "Then?"

Ren dictated: "Previously recorded as dissolved; body-first witness indicates continuing metabolic activity independent of record status."

Lael said, "Add: procedural closure did not create substantive closure."

Oru-Kel: "Add scale."

Ren added: "At least at maintenance-body scale."

The clerk entered it all.

The terminal accepted the line with obvious reluctance, which Ren chose not to anthropomorphize because they had enough relationships in the room already.

The maintenance body warmed, then cooled by one degree.

Vessel-kin said, "Less hidden."

"Not healed?" Rusk asked.

This time no one corrected the question too harshly. He had asked it with the right wound in view.

Vessel-kin answered, "Less hidden."

"That is all?"

Salt-Before-Shell said, "All is a greedy word."

Rusk nodded. "Fair."

Lael opened the relational treaty address note and added a new clause:

Minority positions recorded as metabolized, dissolved, or procedurally closed may retain body-level activity. Later warmth, refusal, or metabolic evidence must be treated as continuing relation, not contradiction, unless body-first witness establishes error.

Ren read it. "That will terrify every administrative office in the Compact."

"Good," Lael said.

"You are enjoying this."

"A little. That concerns me."

"Keep the concern."

"I intend to."

Third-Voice approached the maintenance body. All three fans lowered. The speaker did not touch the warm patch.

"North-east maintenance body," Third-Voice said, "Parliament failed to hold your warmth in visible relation."

The small chamber pulsed.

The answer came without translation for several seconds. Then the speakers produced:

"Warmth remained. Visibility absent. Harm partial. Use careful."

Ren wrote it exactly.

Use careful.

Not use carefully. Use careful: a noun, a warning, a thing with weight.

The minority warmth thread entered the modified exchange vessel. The vessel bands lit softly, amber-white alongside the earlier blue and amber traces. The position did not become larger. It became less alone.

Outside the basin, the medical convoy clock continued toward transit.

Inside, the Parliament had discovered that one of its old closed entries was still alive.

Progress, Ren thought, had lasted nineteen minutes.

This was better than progress.

It was the record learning to be embarrassed while the body was still warm enough to correct it.

Chapter 14: Mira's Delayed Advice

Mira Cael's message arrived eight hours after it had become necessary.

That was normal for advice from people one respected. It traveled through distance, bureaucracy, caution, and the sender's inconvenient habit of thinking before speaking. By the time good advice arrived, the room had usually already made three mistakes, two partial repairs, and one record that would outlive everyone who knew why it was wrong.

Ren saw the sender tag appear on their private slate and felt, with embarrassing force, the desire to be rescued.

MIRA CAEL / TREATY-SCIENCE DELAYED PACKET / PELOGOS ROUTE

MESSAGE RECORDED 2436.187.16.03

DELIVERY DELAY: ROUTE CONGESTION / SHOALS PRIORITY HOLD / MANUAL RELEASE BY SENDER

Ren stared at the last line.

Manual release by sender.

Mira had held it back.

Lael noticed the change in Ren's face. "Bad news?"

"Advice."

"Worse."

Rusk, who had been drafting his third outward advisory in language ugly enough to be useful, looked up. "From whom?"

"Mira Cael."

Keeps-Water-Calm's vessel turned slightly.

The Pelagos diplomat said nothing.

That was its own weather.

Ren opened the packet.

Mira's face did not appear. Of course it did not. She had never liked visual authority in delayed messages. The packet contained voice, text, raw annotations, and a warning header:

DO NOT PLAY THIS AS INSTRUCTION.

Ren smiled despite themselves.

Lael leaned closer. "She knows you."

"Unfortunately."

Ren played the voice low enough that the gallery did not become an audience and loud enough that the relevant witnesses could object if needed.

Mira Cael sounded older than the public lectures. Not frail. Less polished. Her voice carried the dry rasp of someone who had spent too many years breathing station air that knew it should have been water.

"Ren," she said, "if this reaches you after the first crisis, good. If it reaches you before, hold it until after. Advice given too early becomes architecture."

Ren stopped the playback.

Lael's expression had sharpened.

Rusk said, "That is irritatingly good."

Keeps-Water-Calm spoke from the repair chamber. "Mira-Cael remembers some harm."

Ren resumed.

"The Parliament will ask you to translate. The Compact will ask you to stabilize. Routeholders will ask you to make delay legible as risk. Orison factions will ask you to witness without taking custody. These are not the same request. If you confuse them, you will make a beautiful instrument for someone else's hand."

Ren's fingers tightened around the slate.

"You will be tempted," Mira continued, "to protect Orison meaning from human misuse by holding it yourself. That is custody wearing translation's coat. You are not a vault. You are not a parent. You are not a court-appointed guardian for alien truth. Your task is not to keep meaning safe inside your better understanding. Your task is to make the conditions of misunderstanding visible enough that the people and bodies carrying the cost can refuse, revise, or remain opaque."

Lael whispered, "Damn."

Ren did not trust themselves to speak.

Mira's voice softened, which made the next sentence worse.

"If you think I say this because I succeeded at Pelagos, correct yourself. I say it because I failed in ways that later became policy."

Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia moved once.

No one interrupted.

"I kept Orison meaning in my reports because I feared miners, courts, and agencies would damage it. I was right about the danger and wrong about the custody. Once human law trusted me as a careful keeper, it trusted me too much. It asked what Mira Cael meant when Orison relation exceeded the form. It cited my restraint as if restraint were consent. It made my caution into a narrow bridge and then charged others toll to cross."

Ren looked at the wet record headers they had made all day.

BOUNDARY RELATION.

RELATIONAL TREATY ADDRESS.

MINORITY WARMTH WITNESS.

BODY-FIRST.

Good names. Necessary names. Handles, all of them, if left alone with hungry systems.

Mira continued.

"Do not respond by refusing all language. Silence is also custody when others need a way to object. Give language with its wound visible. Give routing back to the bodies that can revoke you. When possible, make your record embarrass itself."

The clerk, listening from the terminal, looked involuntarily proud.

Ren paused the message again.

Lael said, "That supports the insufficiency addendum."

"It supports more than that."

Rusk said, "It attacks all of us."

"Yes," Ren said.

"Good advice often does."

Ren looked at him, surprised.

He shrugged. "I have counsel. Occasionally they earn their fees."

Ren played the final segment.

"A last thing," Mira said. "Keeps-Water-Calm may be present. If so, do not praise them for Pelagos. Do not praise me either. Praise is not always wrong, but humans use it as a solvent. It dissolves continuing obligation into admiration. If you must say something grateful, make it operational. Say: the record remains corrigible. Say: the witness can be refused. Say: no expertise survives without the people it claims to understand retaining power to make it wrong."

The packet ended.

For several seconds, the only sound in the gallery came from the basin pumps and Oru-Kel's low boundary intervals.

Then Keeps-Water-Calm said, "Mira-Cael learned with scar tissue."

Ren swallowed. "Yes."

"Scar tissue is not innocence."

"No."

"Scar tissue is not useless."

"No."

Salt-Before-Shell rotated toward the gallery. "Human elder warns human student not to become better cage."

Ren winced. "Yes."

Lael looked at the wet record. "We need revocation clauses."

Ren closed their eyes briefly.

Of course they did.

Every witness structure they had built today had a preservation instinct. Records wanted to stay. Legal notes wanted to be cited. Routing methods wanted to become practice. Practice wanted to become policy. Policy wanted to become institution. Institution wanted to survive correction.

"For which records?" the clerk asked.

Lael said, "All of them."

Rusk made a sound of pain. "That will make them less reliable."

Ren answered before Lael could. "No. It will make their reliability honest."

Keeps-Water-Calm: "Better enough."

Rusk dragged both hands down his face and looked, for the first time since the crisis had opened, like a man who understood that legal clarity could be physically exhausting. "Define honest reliability in a way I can send to a convoy captain who has twelve sealed med pods and a chief engineer demanding one yes-or-no route instruction."

"No," Ren said.

Rusk stared.

Ren felt Lael's attention sharpen beside them, not warning exactly, more like a hand hovering near a pressure valve.

"If I give you a definition that survives distance better than the bodies it describes, Mira wins her own argument by losing it through me."

"That sentence is why people dislike treaty-science."

"People dislike treaty-science because it notices when yes and no are being used as storage containers for unpaid costs."

The clerk made a small sound that might have been agreement and might have been a cough hidden inside professional caution.

Rusk pointed at the care-passage current. "Those unpaid costs are currently scheduled to move under my name."

"Then your name needs a burden label."

He went very still.

Ren regretted the phrase as soon as it left their mouth. It was too sharp and too satisfying, which meant it probably belonged more to anger than to witness. But the gallery did not let language return unused. The Pelagos diplomat's vessel darkened at one edge. Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia tightened. Even Oru-Kel reduced its boundary interval, as if making room around a thing that might cut.

Rusk lowered his hand. "Say it cleanly."

Ren inhaled through the membrane mask and tasted salt, hot polymer, and the bitter trace of emergency sterilant. "If the Compact route advisory uses your authorization to move medical cargo through a disputed care relation, the record should say what you are accepting and what you are not allowed to claim later. It should not let you borrow Orison care as human certainty."

"I am accepting operational risk."

"And interpretive debt."

Lael looked down at the slate. "That is better."

Rusk laughed once, without humor. "Of course it is."

Vela-Short's voice came from the engineering channel. "Convoy command can live with debt labels. We already use hazard labels, custody labels, contamination labels."

"Debt is not contamination," Salt-Before-Shell said.

"No," Vela-Short replied, surprising everyone by sounding careful. "But unlabeled debt becomes contamination when it enters an engine room."

Oru-Kel's nodes pulsed, a dim amber around the chamber wall. "Machine-body speaker makes near-sense."

"I'll take it," Vela-Short said.

The Pelagos diplomat, silent until then, extended a thin listening filament toward Mira's packet. "Delayed human elder included annotations?"

Ren checked. "Raw annotations, yes."

"Show names of failures. Not content. Names."

Ren hesitated. Mira had marked the packet private to Ren, but not sealed. The annotation index was metadata, not argument. Still, metadata had started wars of emphasis before. They opened only the top layer and projected it into a narrow witness pane:

ANNOTATION INDEX

PELAGOS MINING COURT / TERM: STEWARDSHIP / FAILURE MODE: PRAISE CONVERSION

ORISON TIDE-CUSTODY HEARING / TERM: PROTECTIVE SILENCE / FAILURE MODE: ABSENCE TREATED AS WAIVER

ROUTE NINETEEN MEDIATION / TERM: TEMPORARY RELATION / FAILURE MODE: TEMPORARY CITED AS PRECEDENT

CALDERA RELIEF ACCORD / TERM: EXPERT SUMMARY / FAILURE MODE: LOCAL REFUSAL OMITTED FOR READABILITY

SALT PARLIAMENT ADVISORY / TERM: UNKNOWN / FAILURE MODE: PENDING

Ren looked at the final line and felt the packet become heavier in their hand.

"She wrote that before this happened," Lael said.

"She wrote it because this always happens," Rusk said quietly.

The Pelagos diplomat's vessel paled, one internal tide at a time. "Praise conversion harmed Pelagos worker bodies."

Keeps-Water-Calm did not move.

Ren knew the broad public version. Everyone trained in treaty-science did. Mira Cael had been praised by human courts for "respectful restraint" after refusing to simplify Pelagos kin obligation during a mining injury dispute. The phrase had become doctrine. Respectful restraint meant courts could avoid asking whether a damaged worker's relation owed compensation to a whole cluster of dependent bodies. The mine paid less. The court praised more. Pelagos adapted, not because the injury was accepted, but because adaptation was what living systems did when law injured them and then called the scar a cultural feature.

That was the polite version.

The Pelagos diplomat gave the other one in six words.

"Children ate thinner because praise was cheaper."

No one translated it.

No one needed to.

Rusk sat back as if his chair had moved under him. The clerk's hands hovered above the terminal. Lael closed the annotation pane without being asked, leaving only the index title and the pending line.

Ren forced themselves not to apologize. An apology from the nearest human would make a room for human feeling, and Mira had warned them against rooms that converted continuing obligation into performance.

Instead Ren said, "Then debt label."

The Pelagos diplomat's listening filament withdrew by half. "Operational gratitude acceptable."

Keeps-Water-Calm spoke next. "Record remains corrigible."

The words moved through the gallery like a small, difficult current.

Ren opened a new field under the care-passage advisory:

INTERPRETIVE DEBT LABEL: HUMAN AUTHORIZATION FOR MEDICAL TRANSIT DOES NOT CONVERT DISPUTED ORISON CARE RELATION INTO HUMAN-CERTAIN ROUTE CONSENT. AUTHORIZING OFFICIAL ACCEPTS DUTY TO CARRY CORRECTION, MINORITY WARMTH, AND LATER BODY-FIRST OBJECTION BACK INTO COMPACT RECORDS ATTACHED TO THIS TRANSIT.

Rusk read it twice. His face did not like it. That mattered less than the fact that he did not ask to remove it.

"Add my office," he said.

Ren looked up.

"Not just authorizing official. My office. If I get replaced, the burden should not evaporate into personnel history."

Lael's mouth tightened, the closest thing to approval she had shown him. "Institutional continuity for debt."

"Do not make it sound noble."

"I was making it sound heavy."

"Good."

Ren added:

THE BURDEN SURVIVES PERSONNEL CHANGE INSIDE THE OFFICE THAT ISSUED THE TRANSIT AUTHORIZATION.

The line looked ugly. It looked bureaucratic. It looked difficult to quote beautifully.

Mira would have liked that, Ren thought, and then corrected themselves. Mira would have distrusted liking it.

The authorization system did not like it either.

That was almost comforting.

When Tamsin attached the interpretive debt label to the transit authorization, the route office pane produced a warning with the offended neatness of a machine protecting a workflow from meaning:

NONSTANDARD BURDEN MAY IMPAIR AUTHORIZATION PORTABILITY.

Rusk gave a short laugh. "Portability. There it is."

Lael leaned over the pane. "Show impairment."

Tamsin expanded the warning. Three downstream receivers appeared: convoy command, cold-chain guarantor, and emergency legal archive. Convoy command would see the debt label as an advisory note. The cold-chain guarantor would see it as a documentation condition. The emergency legal archive would see it as a future-review flag.

The fourth receiver made the room go still:

ROUTEHOLDER PERFORMANCE MODEL: OPTIONAL / MAY IGNORE NONFINANCIAL BURDEN.

Ren looked at Rusk.

He was already opening the model profile.

"Before you say it," he said, "yes."

"I was going to say several things."

"Most of them deserved."

The performance model had fields for delay, spoilage, escort retention, reserve drawdown, litigation exposure, sponsor satisfaction, clinic impact, and reputational stability. It had no field for interpretive debt. That absence did not shock anyone anymore. The day had made absence predictable. Predictability did not make it acceptable.

Rusk selected ADD CONDITION.

The model rejected the phrase INTERPRETIVE DEBT:

UNRECOGNIZED PERFORMANCE DIMENSION.

Lael said, "Use litigation exposure."

Ren said, "No."

Rusk said, "If I use litigation exposure, the model will treat the debt as risk to us, not duty owed outward."

Keeps-Water-Calm's cilia moved. "Human notices glove direction."

"Painfully," Rusk said.

He tried REPUTATIONAL STABILITY.

Ren made a sound of disgust.

"I know," he said, and deleted it.

Tamsin checked the administrative settings. "There is a compliance covenant field, but it is usually used for reporting duties."

Lael said, "That is closer."

Rusk opened it.

COMPLIANCE COVENANT:
